Al-Ghazali's Four Categories of People
Presents al-Ghazali's division of people into four categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of people according to al-Ghazali: perished, saved, happy, and perfect in felicity, and how they relate to Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a.

Core Idea
People are divided into four levels based on their response to divine truth.

This passage presents a classification by Imam al-Ghazali that helps us understand the different outcomes for people in the afterlife. It distinguishes between those who reject Islam, those who accept it but with errors, those who follow truth with evidence, and the elite who reach perfection. This sets the stage for identifying the saved group.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four categories
First, read the Arabic text along with its translation. Note the four names: perished (hālik), saved (nājī), happy (sa'īd), and perfect in felicity (kāmil al-sa'āda). See how each is defined.
The categories are based on one's relation to Islam and truth.
2
Understand the 'perished' category
The first category consists of those who reject Islam entirely and choose other paths. They are doomed in the Hereafter.
3
Understand the 'saved' category
The saved are those who profess the shahada and affirm necessary matters of religion, but have some misguidance in beliefs or practices that does not take them out of Islam. Examples are the 'sects of desires' (firaq al-ahwā').
They are saved from eternal hell but not at the highest rank.
4
Understand the 'happy' and 'perfect in felicity' categories
The happy are Muslims who adhere to truth with evidence, following the Prophet and Companions, but do not excel in good deeds. The perfect in felicity are the saints (awliyā') who have reached the station of ihsan; they are Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a.
The highest rank is specifically identified with Ahl al-Sunna.
5
Reflect on the significance of this classification
Ask yourself: What does this classification teach about salvation? How does it define the saved group? Notice that al-Ghazali places Ahl al-Sunna at the top.
This classification is used to discuss sectarian differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قضى المولى جل جلاله باختلاف الأمة مختارين، حتى صارت نِحَلاً وفِرَقاً ومذاهب، والناسُ بعد هذه القسمة الأزليَّة التي لله فيها الحجة البالغة على عباده أقسام أربعة كما يرى حجة الإسلام الغزالي في عموم كتبه: هالك في الآخرة، وناج، وسعيد، وكامل السعادة. فالهالك : من أعرض ونأى بجانبه واختار غير سبیل الهدى، ولم يرتض الإسلام ديناً. والناجي : كلُّ من قال : لا إله إلا الله محمد رسول الله، وأقرَّ بكلِّ معلوم من دين الإسلام بالضرورة، غير أنه جمع إلى هذا ضلالاً فى العلميَّات والعمليَّات اعتقاداً وفقهاً ممَّا لا يبلغ به حدَّ الخروج عن الدين؛ كفرق الأهواء المشهورة. والسعيدُ : المسلم الذي أضاف نفسه للحقِّ بالحجّة والبرهان، وكان على ما عليه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأصحابه الكرام، ولم يُغيّر ولم يبدّل، غير أنه لا يُسابق بالخيرات. وكامل السعادة هو من أولياء الله تعالى الذين بلغوا رتبة الإحسان وهم أهل السنة والجماعة.
And the Lord, exalted be His majesty, has decreed the differing of the ummah by choice, until they became sects, factions, and schools. And mankind after this eternal division – for which Allah has the conclusive argument upon His servants – are four categories, as the Proof of Islam al-Ghazali sees in most of his books: perished in the Hereafter, saved, happy, and perfect in felicity. The perished: one who turns away, distances himself, chooses other than the path of guidance, and does not accept Islam as a religion. The saved: everyone who says 'There is no god but Allah, Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah,' and affirms every matter known from the religion of Islam by necessity, but he has combined with this misguidance in theoretical and practical matters – in belief and fiqh – which does not reach the limit of leaving the religion, like the well-known sects of desires. The happy: the Muslim who attaches himself to the truth with evidence and proof, and is upon what the Prophet (peace be upon him) and his noble companions were upon, and does not change or alter, but he does not race to good deeds. And the perfect in felicity is from the saints of Allah the Exalted who have attained the station of ihsan, and they are Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is a classification of people into four levels. The author states that Allah decreed difference in the ummah, then divides people into four types based on their response to truth. The first is the perished who reject Islam. The second is the saved (nājī) who profess Islam but have errors that do not remove them from the faith, like the sects of desires (ahwā'). The third is the happy (sa'īd) who adhere to truth with evidence and follow the Prophet and Companions but do not excel in good deeds. The fourth is the perfect in felicity (kāmil al-sa'āda), the saints who have reached ihsan, identified as Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a. This classification sets up the discussion of who is the saved group.

Key Terms
هالك
ناجي
سعيد
كامل السعادة
فرق الأهواء
أهل السنة والجماعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the 'saved' (nājī) are the highest category or that they are necessarily Ahl al-Sunna.
Correction: The passage shows that the saved include those with misguidance (like sects of desires), while the highest rank is the 'perfect in felicity,' specifically Ahl al-Sunna.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes the 'happy' (sa'īd) from the 'saved' (nājī)?
A) The sa'īd has evidence and follows the Prophet exactly
B) The sa'īd is a martyr
C) The nājī is from Ahl al-Sunna
D) The nājī does not commit sins

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali classifies people into four levels, with Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a being the highest, the perfect in felicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where do you see yourself in Al-Ghazali's four categories? Are you merely 'saved' by saying the shahada but holding errors, or have you attached yourself to the truth with evidence? What is one area of belief or practice where you might be falling short, and what will you do today to align yourself with the path of the 'happy'?
?
Al-Ghazali says the 'perfect in felicity' are the saints who have attained ihsan—worshipping Allah as if you see Him, and Ahl al-Sunna. Do you truly desire that rank? Be honest: what holds you back from pursuing the highest level of faith? Commit to one concrete action this week to move closer to that station.
?
The 'perished' are those who reject Islam entirely. When you look at your own life, are there any signs of heedlessness or taking your faith for granted that could lead you away from the path? How can you cultivate a sense of urgency and gratitude for your iman, so that you never become among the 'perished'? Write down one habit you will start or stop to protect your faith.

Correcting the Misunderstanding of the Definite Article in Praise
Corrects a misunderstanding about the definite article in praise and clarifies sectarian positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of the definite article 'al' in 'al-hamdu' and why a common sectarian assignment of interpretations is mistaken.

Core Idea
The definite article 'al' in 'al-hamdu' signifies a specified well-known praise, not a sectarian divide.

This marginal note corrects a common misunderstanding. Some readers of al-Zamakhshari's al-Kashshaf mistakenly thought that interpreting 'al' as genus (al-jins) or totality (al-istighraq) divides along sectarian lines—Mu'tazila vs. Ahl al-Sunna. The commentator shows this is false, explains the actual Mu'tazila position, and clarifies the intended meaning of 'al' in the phrase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the error being corrected
Read the first part of the passage where the commentator says someone 'erred'. What is the exact error? It's about assigning interpretations of 'al' to different sects.
The error is based on a superficial reading of 'al-Kashshaf'.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The passage explains that the Mu'tazila, while attributing actions to servants, do not deny that all praises belong to Allah. Why? Because they acknowledge Allah creates the power in them. This shows they agree with Ahl al-Sunna on this point.
3
Learn the purpose of making praise 'well-known'
The commentator says the praise is made 'well-known' (ma'hood) to avoid ambiguity. What specific praise is meant? It is a compound of eternal praise (Allah's praise of Himself) and temporal praise (praise of prophets).
If 'any praise' were intended, 'al' would need to indicate generality or genus.
4
Grasp the conclusion about the definite article
The definite article 'al' here is not a sectarian marker. It functions to specify a particular praise. Both Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna can agree on this interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد وهم؛ إذ اختصاص الأول للمعتزلة والثاني لأهل السنة - أخذاً من ظاهر عبارة «الكشاف» الجنس يفيد الاستغراق والمعتزلة وإن أسندوا الأفعال إلى العباد .. لكنهم لا يتكرون اختصاص المحامد كلها به تعالى؛ لاعترافهم بأن الحق سبحانه هو خالق القدرة فيهم). (۱) وإنما جعله معتدا به كهذا الحمد الذي لا شكّ في كماله .. ليكون معهوداً، فلو قلنا : أي حمد .. تعيَّن جعل (أل) للاستغراق أو للجنس، والقديم هنا : هو حمد الله لنفسه سبحانه، والحادث هنا : هو حمد أنبيائه له جل وعز.
Thus he erred; because the assignment of the first to the Mu'tazila and the second to the Ahl al-Sunna - taken from the apparent wording of 'al-Kashshaf' that the genus indicates totality, and the Mu'tazila, even if they attribute actions to servants... yet they do not deny the assignment of all praises to Him, the Exalted; due to their acknowledgment that Allah is the Creator of power in them). (1) And he only made it considered like this praise which has no doubt in its perfection... to be something well-known, so if we said: any praise... then making 'al' for generality or genus is determined, and the eternal here is the praise of Allah for Himself, and the temporal here is the praise of His prophets for Him.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a corrective marginal note addressing a misunderstanding. Someone erroneously thought that the first interpretation (of 'al') belongs to the Mu'tazila and the second to the Ahl al-Sunna, based on a superficial reading of al-Zamakhshari's 'al-Kashshaf'. The commentator argues that this is a mistake: the Mu'tazila, although they attribute actions to servants, do not deny that all praises belong exclusively to Allah, because they acknowledge that Allah creates the power in them. The note then explains the proper understanding of the definite article 'al' in the phrase 'al-hamdu' (the praise). The commentator says that to make the praise 'well-known' (ma'hood), the definite article is used to specify a particular praise: the compound of eternal praise (Allah's praise of Himself) and temporal praise (the praise of His prophets). If we intended any praise, the definite article would have to be for generality or genus. Thus the intended praise is a mental compound of the eternal and temporal, not the eternal alone.

Key Terms
أل
الاستغراق
الجنس
المعتزلة
أهل السنة
الكشاف
القديم
الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that interpreting 'al' in 'al-hamdu' as genus or totality is a Mu'tazila position, while interpreting it as indicating a well-known praise is an Ahl al-Sunna position.
Correction: The commentator shows that the Mu'tazila do not deny that all praises belong to Allah, and the definite article here specifies a particular well-known praise, not a sectarian marker.

Checkpoint
What is the main error that the commentator is correcting in this marginal note?
A) The error of thinking that the Mu'tazila deny that all praises belong to Allah.
B) The error of assigning the first interpretation of 'al' to the Mu'tazila and the second to Ahl al-Sunna based on a superficial reading of al-Kashshaf.
C) The error of assuming that the definite article 'al' always indicates totality.
D) The error of claiming that the praise of prophets is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The definite article 'al' in 'al-hamdu' refers to a specific compound of eternal and temporal praise, not a sectarian indicator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Alhamdulillahi Rabbil Alameen', do you realize it is a specific praise combining Allah's eternal praise and the praise of His prophets? How will this awareness change the manner in which you say it from now on?
?
The error in this passage came from taking a superficial reading and assuming a sectarian divide. Where in your own study of deen have you made assumptions based on surface-level understanding? Will you commit to verifying before concluding?
?
The Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna agreed on this fundamental point despite deep disagreements elsewhere. Is there a disagreement you have with a fellow Muslim where you need to search for deeper common ground, especially regarding Allah's praise and attributes? What step will you take today to bridge that gap?

The 73 Sects and the Saved Sect
The author cites the hadith on sectarian division and defines the saved sect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hadith about the division of the ummah into 73 sects and the defining criterion of the saved sect.

Core Idea
The saved sect is the one that follows the Prophet and his Companions.

This lesson presents the well-known hadith regarding the fragmentation of the Muslim community. The author identifies the saved sect not by its name or size, but by its adherence to the original teachings of the Prophet and his Companions. Understanding this criterion is essential for theological discernment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the numbers: 73 sects, 1 saved, 72 in the Fire.
The hadith is quoted within the passage.
2
Identify the two groups
The passage mentions two groups: the saved sect (فرقة ناجية) and the rest (72 sects who are in the Fire).
3
Define the saved sect
The saved sect is defined as 'that which follows what the Prophet and his Companions were upon.' This is the core criterion.
The term 'أصحابه' refers to the Companions (Sahaba).
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about why the author emphasizes following the Prophet and Sahaba. This warns against innovation and deviation.
This connects to the broader theme of orthodoxy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد افترقت الأمَّةُ ثلاثاً وسبعين فرقة: منهم فرقة ناجية؛ وهي التي على ما كان عليه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأصحابه، واثنان وسبعون في النار؛ لما في الحديث: «افترقت الأمم السابقة على اثنتين وسبعين فرقةً وستفترقون ثلاثاً وسبعين فرقة؛ منهم فرقةً واحدة ناجية، واثنتان وسبعون في النار»
And the ummah divided into seventy-three sects: among them is one saved sect, which is that which follows what the Prophet, peace be upon him, and his Companions were upon, and seventy-two are in the Fire, because of the hadith: 'The previous nations divided into seventy-two sects, and you will divide into seventy-three sects; one single sect will be saved, and seventy-two will be in the Fire.'
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a brief mention of the well-known hadith about the 73 sects of the Muslim ummah. The author states that the ummah will split into 73 sects, of which only one is saved (najiya). The saved sect is defined as those who follow the path of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) and his Companions. The other 72 sects are destined for the Fire. This serves to warn against deviation and to emphasize the importance of adhering to the authentic teachings of the early Muslims.

Key Terms
فرقة ناجية
افتراق
أصحاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any group claiming to be the saved sect is automatically correct.
Correction: The saved sect is defined by following the Prophet and his Companions, not by claim alone.

Checkpoint
How is the saved sect described in this passage?
A) The largest sect in numbers.
B) The sect that follows what the Prophet and his Companions were upon.
C) The sect that follows the majority of scholars.
D) The sect that avoids all theological discourse.

Key Takeaway
The saved sect is defined by its adherence to the original teachings of the Prophet and his Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you know for certain that the path you are on is the same path the Prophet and his Companions were upon? What evidence do you have from your own practice?
?
If you were to be judged today, would you be among the saved sect simply because of your label, or because you actively follow the way of the Sahaba? What is one thing you can change today to bring you closer to that path?
?
The hadith warns that 72 sects are in the Fire. Does that thought disturb your comfort? Are you willing to examine your beliefs and actions to ensure you are not blindly following a sect that has strayed, even if it is popular?

The Seventy-Three Sects and the Saved Sect
The author quotes the hadith about the seventy-three sects and identifies the saved sect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hadith about the seventy-three sects and how the saved sect is defined as those who follow the Prophet and his companions.

Core Idea
The saved sect is the one that follows the Prophet and his companions exactly.

This lesson centers on a famous hadith about the division of the Muslim community into seventy-three sects. The author quotes it to emphasize that only one sect is saved, and it is distinguished by its adherence to the path of the Prophet and his companions. Understanding this hadith is crucial for recognizing the importance of authentic tradition in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the author's direct citation of the hadith.
The hadith is quoted as proof for the division.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage mentions three numbers: 73 total sects, 1 saved, 72 in the Fire. Understand the relationship.
Also note the contrast with previous nations (72 sects).
3
Reflect on the definition of the saved sect
The saved sect is defined by following the Prophet and his companions. Consider what this implies for identifying the saved sect today.
This connects to the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد افترقت الأمَّةُ ثلاثاً وسبعين فرقة : منهم فرقة ناجية؛ وهي التي على ما كان عليه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ،وأصحابه، واثنان وسبعون في النار ؛ لما في الحديث: «افترقت الأمم السابقة على اثنتين وسبعين فرقةً وستفترقون ثلاثاً وسبعين فرقة؛ منهم فرقةً واحدة ناجية، واثنتان وسبعون في النار».
And the nation divided into seventy-three sects: among them one saved sect, and it is that which was upon what the Prophet, peace be upon him, and his companions were upon, and seventy-two are in the Fire, due to the hadith: 'The previous nations divided into seventy-two sects, and you will divide into seventy-three sects; among them one saved sect, and seventy-two are in the Fire.'
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a citation of a well-known hadith about the division of the Muslim community into sects. It states that the nation will split into seventy-three sects, of which only one is saved (al-firqa al-najiya). The saved sect is explicitly described as the one that follows what the Prophet Muhammad and his companions were upon. The other seventy-two sects are destined for the Fire. The hadith is used to emphasize the importance of adhering to the authentic tradition of the early Muslims (the Salaf).

Key Terms
فرقة ناجية
حديث الافتراق
ثلاث وسبعين فرقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the saved sect is any group that claims to follow the Sunnah.
Correction: The saved sect is specifically the one following the Prophet and his companions in creed and practice, not just any group claiming to follow the Sunnah.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith mentioned, how many sects will be saved?
A) Seventy-three
B) One
C) Seventy-two
D) Two

Key Takeaway
The saved sect is uniquely defined as those who follow the Prophet and his companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the hadith of the 73 sects, does it stir in you a genuine fear that you might be on the wrong path? Or do you assume you are safe because of your affiliation? Today, reflect on one belief or practice where you might not be following the exact way of the Prophet and his companions. Write it down and commit to seeking the truth.
?
The saved sect is defined by following the Prophet and his companions exactly. In which area of your life—worship, character, or dealings—do you feel most distant from that exact following? What specific step will you take this week to align yourself closer to that path?
?
This lesson says 72 sects are in the Fire. Does that reality burden your heart with concern for others, or do you dismiss it as their problem? Ask Allah to make you a means of guidance for someone today, and to keep you firm on the saved way.

Responding to the Philosophers' Seven Demands
Commentator enumerates seven demands and explains the first demand with four syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers' objection to the proof of temporality is countered by a chain of four syllogisms, and how accidents serve as evidence for both the temporality and the perfection of the Creator.

Core Idea
The philosophers' objection is dismantled by four syllogisms that together prove the world's temporality and need for a Creator.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a poetic summary of seven philosophical demands (maṭālib) and explains the first demand in detail. He shows that the argument for the world's temporality is not a single step but requires four interconnected syllogisms. This lesson guides you through these syllogisms and highlights the dual role of accidents as indicators of both temporality and divine perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic summary and its explanation
The poem lists seven demands; the commentator explains the first (Zayd) as a refutation of the philosophers' denial of accidents. Notice the mnemonic structure.
The poem is a mnemonic for the demands.
2
Trace the four syllogisms
The commentator details four syllogisms that build on each other: accidents are temporal, bodies are possible of non-existence, hence not eternal, hence need a maker. Follow the logical chain.
Each syllogism's conclusion becomes the premise of the next.
3
Understand the two aspects of accidents
Accidents indicate temporality (need for a maker) and precision (perfection of the Maker). This double role refines the proof.
This guards against reducing accidents to only one function.

= بيان المطالب السبعة ] واعلم أن لهم هنا مطالب سبعةً، نظمها بعضهم في قوله: [من الرجز] زَيدٌ مَ قامَ مَا انْتَقَلْ ما كَمَنا ما انْفَكَ لا عُدْمَ قَدِيمٌ لا حَنا (١) .۱ فقوله : (زيد) ردُّ لقول الفلاسفة: (لا) نسلّمُ ثبوت زائد على الأجرام حتى يصح الأعراض على أن العالم بمعنى الأجرام يجوز عليه العدم، ثم بجواز عدمه على حدوثه، ثم بحدوثه على أنه يحتاج إلى مُحدث، فيحتاج حينئذ إلى أربعة أقيسة، بيانها : أن يقال : الأعراض شُوهد تغيرها من وجود إلى عدم وعكسه، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو حادث، ينتج أن الأعراض حادثة. ثم يقال : العالم بمعنى الأجرام ملازم للأعراض الحادثة، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو جائز العدم، ينتج العالم يجوز عليه العدم تؤخذ هذه النتيجة وتجعل صغرى . قياس ثالث تقريره هكذا ؛ العالم يجوز عليه العدم وكل ما كان كذلك استحال قدمه، وثبت حدوثه، ينتج أن العالم استحال قدمه وثبت حدوثه، تؤخذ هذه النتيجة وتجعل صغرى. قیاس رابع نظمه هكذا ؛ العالم استحال قدمه وثبت حدوثه، وكل ما كان كذلك فلا بد له من محدث، ينتج أن العالم لا بد له من ،محدث، وهذا هو المقصود بالنظر، وإنما قلنا ذلك؛ لأن المصنف جعل الأعراض دالة على جواز العدم لا على الحدوث، حيث قال: «لكن به قام دليل العدم. انتهى. وقوله : (أيضاً والحاصل ... إلى آخره) تلخص من كلامه هنا أن الأعراض باعتبار حدوثها دالة على حدوث الأجرام الدال على أنها - أي الأجرام - لا بدَّ لها من محدث، وذكر عند قوله : تجد به صنعاً أن الأعراض تدل على كمال الصانع وعموم علمه وإرادته، من جهة إتقانها إتقاناً ؛ فللأعراض :جهتان: جهة حدوثها، وجهة ،إتقانها، ولهذا تعددت دلالتها . انتهى أجهوري. بديعاً ; (۱) انظر (حاشية الأمير على عبد السلام (ص) ،(٤٨) ، وقد نظمها العلامة الأمير بقوله : سبق الإله كذا العدم تدريجه إمكانه مع موجب أثر طرا
= Explanation of the seven demands. Know that they [the philosophers] have here seven demands (maṭālib), which some have versified in his statement (in rajaz meter): 'Zayd, mā, qāma, mā, intaqal, mā, kamanā, mā, infakka, lā, 'udma, qadīmun, lā, ḥanā.' (1). So his statement: (Zayd) is a refutation of the philosophers' statement: 'We do not concede the existence of an additional [entity] upon bodies such that the comparison of accidents is valid.' It is that the world meaning bodies is possible of non-existence, then by the possibility of its non-existence upon its temporality, then by its temporality upon that it needs a maker (muḥdith). Thus at that point it requires four syllogisms. Their explanation: It is said: accidents are observed to change from existence to non-existence and vice versa, and everything that is so is temporal (ḥādith); it yields that accidents are temporal. Then it is said: the world meaning bodies is inseparable from temporal accidents, and everything that is so is possible of non-existence; it yields that the world is possible of non-existence. This result is taken and made a minor premise. The third syllogism is formulated thus: the world is possible of non-existence, and everything that is so is impossible of pre-existence, and its temporality is established; it yields that the world is impossible of pre-existence and its temporality is established. This result is taken and made a minor premise. The fourth syllogism is arranged thus: the world is impossible of pre-existence and its temporality is established, and everything that is so must have a maker; it yields that the world must have a maker. And this is the goal of rational investigation (al-naẓar). We only said that because the author made accidents indicative of the possibility of non-existence, not of temporality, where he said: 'But through it (the world) the evidence of non-existence stands.' End. And his statement: 'Also the conclusion (al-ḥāṣil) ... to the end' summarizes from his speech here that accidents by virtue of their temporality indicate the temporality of bodies which indicates that they—i.e., bodies—must have a maker. And he mentioned at his statement: 'You find through it a wondrous making' that accidents indicate the perfection of the Maker and the universality of His knowledge and will, from the aspect of their perfect precision. So for accidents are two aspects: the aspect of their temporality (ḥudūth) and the aspect of their precision (itqān). Therefore their indication is multiple. End of al-Ujhūrī. Badī'an; (1) See the gloss of al-Amīr on 'Abd al-Salām (p. 48), and al-Allāma al-Amīr has versified it in his statement: 'Sabaqa al-ilāhu kadha al-'admu tadrījuhu imkānuhu ma'a mūjibi atharin ṭarā.'
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of seven demands (maṭālib) that philosophers raise against the proof of the world's temporality. The commentator presents a poetic summary (rajaz) for memorization: 'Zayd, mā, qāma, mā, intaqal, mā, kamanā, mā, infakka, lā, 'udma, qadīmun, lā, ḥanā.' He then explains the first demand (Zayd) as a refutation of the philosophers' refusal to concede any extra entity beyond bodies. The commentator details that the argument requires four syllogisms: (1) proving accidents are temporal by their change, (2) proving bodies are possible of non-existence because they are inseparable from temporal accidents, (3) proving that what is possible of non-existence cannot be eternal, hence the world is not eternal, and (4) proving that what is not eternal requires a maker. This sequence leads to the desired conclusion. The commentator then adds a note that the author's wording ('but through it the evidence of non-existence stands') shows accidents indicate possibility of non-existence, not directly temporality. Finally, he cites al-Ujhūrī, stating that accidents have two aspects: their temporality (indicating need for a maker) and their precision (indicating the perfection of the Maker). This guards against the misconception that accidents only prove contingency, not the attributes of God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world's temporality is proven in a single step.
Correction: The proof actually requires four syllogisms: accidents are temporal, bodies are possible of non-existence, hence not eternal, hence need a maker.

Checkpoint
How many syllogisms does the commentator say are needed to fully respond to the philosophers' objection?
A) One syllogism.
B) Two syllogisms.
C) Three syllogisms.
D) Four syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' objection is met by a carefully structured four-syllogism argument that culminates in the necessity of a Creator, while accidents also point to His perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just walked through four syllogisms that demand a Creator for this world. Which single step in that chain do you find yourself resisting—and what does that resistance say about your own stance before your Maker?
?
The passage distinguishes between accidents as evidence of temporality and as evidence of perfection. Look at one accident in your own life—a change you experienced. Does it remind you of your own contingency, or of the perfect hand behind it? Which reminder moves you more to gratitude?
?
The philosophers demanded proof. But now that you have this reasoning, have you actually let it settle into your heart? When the next doubt about God's existence arises, will you recall this argument—or will you let the world's familiarity deceive you into forgetting your beginning?

The Mu'tazila on Action as Part of Faith and Its Quranic Refutation
Presents the Mu'tazila doctrine and refutes it with Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila claim that action is part of faith and how Quranic verses refute it by showing faith's independence from action.

Core Idea
Faith is affirmation (tasdiq); action is a separate fruit, not a part.

The Mu'tazila defined faith as comprising belief, utterance, and action, thereby placing the sinful believer in an intermediate station. This lesson presents their view and then the decisive Quranic evidence that separates faith from action, proving that faith can exist before commands, coexist with sin, and is linguistically distinct from righteous deeds.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila position
Read the passage carefully to grasp the Mu'tazila definition of faith (three parts) and their conclusion about the sinner's intermediate station.
Contrast with the Khawarij who declare such sinners disbelievers.
2
Examine the refutation's core argument
The author argues that 'iman' linguistically means tasdiq (affirmation) and Sharia did not transfer it to include action. Note the lack of evidence for the Mu'tazila claim.
3
Analyze the three Quranic proofs
Identify each verse: 2:183 (faith before fasting), 31:8 (conjunction implying difference), 6:82 (faith with wrongdoing). Understand how each proves separation.
Note the discussion on tafsir of 'zulm' (sin vs. shirk).
4
Consider the footnote clarifications
Read the footnote about al-Ghazali's position and the Salaf's statement that 'faith is saying and doing' interpreted as perfection.
This reconciles apparent contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن العمل شطر من الإيمان؛ لأنهم يقولون بأنه العمل والنطق والاعتقاد؛ فمن ترك العمل فليس بمؤمن؛ لفقد جزء من الإيمان وهو العمل، ولا كافر؛ لوجودِ التصديق، فهو عندهم منزلةٌ بين المنزلتين؛ أي: بين المؤمن والكافر، ويخلَّدُ في النار ويعذِّبُ بأقل من عذاب الكافر، والخوارج يكفرون مرتكب الكبائر . وإنَّما كانَ المختارُ هو الأوَّلَ؛ لأن الإيمان في اللغة التصديق، فيستعملُ شرعاً في تصديق خاص، ولا دليل على نقله للثلاثة كما زعمه المعتزلة (1)، وقد دلَّتِ النصوص على ثبوت الإيمان قبل الأوامر والنواهي، وعلى أن الإيمان والعمل الصالح متغايران، وعلى أن الإيمان والمعاصي يجتمعان؛ كقوله تعالى: ﴿يَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا كتب عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ [البَقَرَة : ۱۸۳] ؛ فإنه يفيد ثبوت الإيمان قبل الأمر بالصوم، وكقوله تعالى : الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ القمان : ۱۸؛ فإن أصل العطف للمغايرة، وكقوله تعالى : الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَلَمْ يَلْبِسُوا إِيمَانَهُم بِظُلْرِ﴾ [الأنعام: ۸۲] بناءً على أن المراد من الظلم المعصية، فقد اقتضى بمفهومه اجتماع الإيمان مع الظلم بمعنى المعاصي على ما علمت ،وقيل : إن المراد به الشرك؛ لما رُوي أن الآيةَ لمَّا نزلت شَقَّ ذلك على الصحابة، وقالوا : أينا لم يظلم نفسه ؟! فقال : ليس كما تظُنُّونَ، إِنَّما هو كما قال لقمان لابنه : يبقى لا تُشْرِكْ بِاللَّهِ إِن الشِّرْكَ لَظُمُ عَظِيمٌ [لقمان: ١٣] (۲). وعليه فمفهوم الآية من باب : ﴿وَمَا يُؤْمِنُ أَكْثَرُهُم بِاللَّهِ إِلَّا وَهُم تُشْرِكُونَ) [يوسف: ١٠٦]، فيكون المراد بالإيمان مطلق التصديق . (1) هذه الثلاثة هي : الاعتقاد والنطق والعمل، وذهب إمامنا الغزالي إلى الأول فقط . تنبيه : ما ورد عن السلف الصالح رضي الله عنهم بأن الإيمان قول وعمل، فيحمل العمل على الكمال. (2) رواه البخاري (٣٣٦٠) ، ومسلم (١٢٤) من حديث سيدنا عبد الله بن مسعود رضي الله عنه
The Mu'tazila held that action is a part of faith; because they say it [faith] is action, utterance, and belief. So whoever abandons action is not a believer due to the loss of a part of faith which is action, but is not a disbeliever due to the presence of affirmation. So he is, in their view, in a station between the two stations, i.e., between the believer and the disbeliever, and he will abide in the Fire forever and be punished less than the punishment of a disbeliever. The Khawarij declare the committer of major sins to be disbelievers. And the chosen opinion is the first [i.e., the Ash'ari view] because faith in language is affirmation (tasdiq), and it is used in the Sharia for a specific affirmation, and there is no proof for its transferral to the three as the Mu'tazila claim (1). The texts have indicated the establishment of faith before commands and prohibitions, and that faith and righteous action are different, and that faith and sins coexist; as His statement: 'O you who have believed, fasting is prescribed for you' [2:183]; for it indicates the establishment of faith before the command to fast, and His statement: 'Those who have believed and done righteous deeds' [31:8]; for the essential meaning of conjunction is difference, and His statement: 'Those who have believed and do not mix their faith with wrongdoing' [6:82] based on the interpretation that 'wrongdoing' means sin; for its implication entails the coexistence of faith with wrongdoing in the sense of sins as you know. And it is said that what is meant by it is shirk; because it is related that when the verse was revealed, it was difficult for the Companions, and they said: 'Who among us has not wronged himself?' So he said: 'It is not as you think; rather it is as Luqman said to his son: O my son, do not associate partners with Allah. Indeed, association [with Him] is great injustice' [31:13] (2). Therefore, the implication of the verse is of the type: 'And most of them do not believe in Allah except that they associate partners with Him' [12:106]; thus the meaning of faith here is absolute affirmation. (1) These three are: belief, utterance, and action. Our Imam al-Ghazali held only the first. Note: What has been reported from the righteous predecessors, may Allah be pleased with them, that faith is saying and doing, then action is interpreted as perfection. (2) Reported by al-Bukhari (3360) and Muslim (124) from the hadith of our master Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, may Allah be pleased with him.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila doctrine that faith consists of three parts: belief, utterance, and action. Consequently, someone who abandons action is neither a believer (due to missing a part) nor a disbeliever (due to having affirmation), but in an intermediate station. The author then refutes this by arguing that the linguistic meaning of 'iman' is affirmation (tasdiq), and Sharia usage retains that meaning without extension. Quranic verses are cited: one shows faith existing before the command to fast (so faith precedes action), another uses conjunction (wa) which implies distinctness (action is separate from faith), and a third verse addresses believers who commit sins, proving that faith can coexist with wrongdoing. The interpretation of 'wrongdoing' as shirk is addressed via a hadith, but the author notes that if it is shirk, then the verse further supports that faith (meaning general affirmation) can coexist with shirk. Footnotes clarify the three components and the position of al-Ghazali and the Salaf.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
عمل
تصديق
منزلة بين المنزلتين
الخوارج
الكبائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith includes action as an essential component; without action, faith is incomplete or null.
Correction: Faith is essentially affirmation (tasdiq) in the heart; action is a separate requirement for perfection, not part of the essence of faith.

Checkpoint
What Quranic argument does the author use to prove that faith can exist before commands and prohibitions?
A) The verse 'Those who have believed and done righteous deeds'
B) The verse 'O you who have believed, fasting is prescribed for you'
C) The verse 'Those who have believed and do not mix their faith with wrongdoing'
D) The verse 'And most of them do not believe in Allah except that they associate partners with Him'

Key Takeaway
Faith is essentially affirmation (tasdiq) in the heart, and action is a separate consequence, not an integral part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you immediately feel that your faith has diminished or become defective? How does this lesson's distinction between the essence of faith (tasdiq) and the perfection of faith through actions challenge that feeling? What would it mean for you to hold onto the certainty that your core faith is intact, while still striving to improve your actions?
?
The Mu'tazila taught that abandoning action puts you in a dangerous middle state. Many Muslims today struggle with guilt over sins, fearing they are losing their faith. After studying this lesson, how does the fact that faith can coexist with sin (based on the interpretation of 6:82) change your perspective on your own struggles? What is one specific area where you need to separate your identity as a believer from your performance of deeds?
?
The Salaf said 'faith is saying and doing', but the author explains this refers to perfection, not essence. Do you subconsciously treat your actions as a condition for being a 'true believer' rather than as a response to the faith already in your heart? What would it look like for you to pursue good deeds as an expression of your faith, not as a prerequisite for it? Can you name one action you perform (or avoid) primarily out of fear of losing faith rather than out of gratitude for having it?

Unity of Existence and Misleading Expressions
Critique of wahdat al-wujud and misleading expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why certain Sufi statements about unity of being are theologically problematic and the ruling on misleading expressions.

Core Idea
Not all spiritual insights justify legally impermissible statements.

This passage explores the controversial concept of waḥdat al-wujūd (unity of being) and the problematic expressions that arise from it. The author presents examples and clarifies the Islamic legal ruling on such statements, while also acknowledging that some saints may be overwhelmed by spiritual states. Understanding this helps distinguish between acceptable mystical language and what implies heretical doctrines like incarnation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic text and its translation, following the flow from the definition of waḥdat al-wujūd to the examples and the ruling.
2
Identify the key problematic statements
Notice the two examples: al-Hallaj's 'I am God' and the saying 'There is none in the cloak but God'. Understand why these are considered misleading.
3
Understand the ruling and its basis
The author states these statements are not permissible because they imply union (ittiḥād) or incarnation (ḥulūl). Note the mention of al-Junayd's fatwa supporting this stance.
4
Consider the nuance regarding spiritual states
The author adds that sometimes saints are overcome by states and their words are interpreted leniently. This nuance is important for understanding how such statements are evaluated.
5
Reflect on the common phrase 'mawjūd fī kull al-wujūd'
The final part warns against a common expression implying omnipresence, which also suggests unity of being and is forbidden due to its implication of incarnation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعضُهم لا يشاهد لغيره وجوداً، وهذا يُسمَّى عندهم وحدة الوجود، وقد غرق فيه مَنْ غرق حتى وقع من بعض الأولياء ما يُوهمُ الاتحاد والحلول ; كقول الحلاج : أنا الله ، وكقول بعضهم : ما في الجبة إلا الله، وهذا اللفظ لا يجوز شرعاً ; لإيهامه، لكنَّ القومَ تارةً تغلبهم الأحوالُ فَيُؤَوَّلُ ما يقعُ منهم بما يناسبه، وممن أفتى بقتل الحلّاج حين قال المقالة السابقة: الجنيد كما في شرح الكبرى . ومن اللفظ الموهم ما شاع على السنة العوام من قولهم: (موجود في كل الوجود)، ففيه إشارة لوحدة الوجود، لكنه ممتنع لإيهامه الحلول.
And some of them do not perceive existence for other-than-Him, and this is called by them 'unity of being' (waḥdat al-wujūd). And drowned in it those who drowned, until there occurred from some of the saints what suggests union (al-ittiḥād) and incarnation (al-ḥulūl); like the saying of al-Ḥallāj: 'I am God', and like the saying of some of them: 'There is none in the cloak but God.' And this statement is not permissible legally because of its misleading implication, but sometimes spiritual states overcome these people, so what occurs from them is interpreted in a suitable manner. Among those who gave a fatwa to kill al-Ḥallāj when he said the previous statement was al-Junayd, as in Sharḥ al-Kubrā. And from the misleading expressions is what is common on the tongues of the common people: 'He exists in all existence' (mawjūd fī kull al-wujūd), for in it there is an indication of unity of being, but it is impossible because of its implication of incarnation.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of waḥdat al-wujūd (unity of being). The author mentions that some people see no existence for anything other than God, leading to claims of union (ittiḥād) and incarnation (ḥulūl). He gives examples: al-Ḥallāj saying 'I am God' and another saying 'There is none in the cloak but God.' These statements are legally impermissible due to their misleading implications. However, the author notes that sometimes these saints are overcome by spiritual states and their words are interpreted appropriately. He mentions that al-Junayd gave a fatwa to kill al-Ḥallāj. Then he points out a common phrase 'mawjūd fī kull al-wujūd' (He exists in all existence) that also implies unity of being and is forbidden because it suggests incarnation.

Key Terms
وحدة الوجود
الاتحاد
الحلول
إيهام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The statement 'There is none in the cloak but God' is an acceptable expression of spiritual insight.
Correction: The author clearly states that this expression is legally impermissible because it implies incarnation (حلول), which contradicts Islamic monotheism.

Checkpoint
Why is the statement 'ما في الجبة إلا الله' considered impermissible according to the author?
A) It denies the existence of God
B) It implies union (اتحاد) or incarnation (حلول)
C) It is a lie about the Prophet
D) It is grammatically incorrect

Key Takeaway
Misleading expressions about God's relationship with creation, even if arising from spiritual experiences, are legally prohibited because they imply incarnation or union with God.

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
4 min
Misleading Expressions Implying Union with God
Prohibits statements like Hallaj's and common sayings implying wahdat al-wujud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why certain mystical expressions are forbidden due to their misleading implications, and how to correctly interpret the phrase 'Allahu Akbar' in a way that preserves divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Not every spiritual utterance is permissible; even saints' words must align with the language of tawhid.

This passage addresses a delicate issue: the utterances of mystics that seem to imply union with God. While such statements may arise from overwhelming spiritual states, the law does not permit them because they mislead others into false beliefs. The text also clarifies the proper meaning of 'Allahu Akbar', showing that it is not a comparative statement but an affirmation of God's absolute transcendence. As you read, notice how the author balances spiritual experience with doctrinal precision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full English translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Pay attention to the three specific expressions mentioned.
2
Identify the three problematic expressions
The passage lists three sayings that are forbidden: al-Hallaj's 'I am Allah', 'There is none in the cloak except Allah', and the common saying 'Exists in all existence'. Note each one.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the prohibition
All three are prohibited because they are misleading (muwahhim). The first two imply a direct claim to divinity, the third implies hulul (indwelling) and wahdat al-wujud.
Even if uttered by sincere gnostics in a spiritual state, the expressions themselves are impermissible.
4
Examine the reinterpretation of 'Allahu Akbar'
The passage quotes Mishkat al-Anwar to explain that 'Allahu Akbar' does not mean God is greater than others (since there is no other). Instead, it means He is too great for comparison or full comprehension.
This corrects a common misunderstanding that 'Allahu Akbar' is a comparative phrase.
5
Reflect on the balance between spiritual states and legal rulings
Notice that al-Junayd issued a fatwa to execute al-Hallaj despite his spiritual rank. This shows that sharia takes precedence over subjective experiences.
The passage allows for interpretation (ta'wil) of such utterances, but the literal wording remains prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحلاج : أنا الله ، وكقول بعضهم : ما في الجبة إلا الله، وهذا اللفظ لا يجوز شرعاً ؛ لإيهامه، لكنَّ القومَ تارةً تغلبهم الأحوالُ فَيُؤَوَّلُ ما يقعُ منهم بما يناسبه، وممن أفتى بقتل الحلّاج حين قال المقالة السابقة: الجنيد كما في شرح الكبرى ) (۱) . ومن اللفظ الموهم ما شاع على السنة العوام من قولهم: (موجود في كل الوجود)، ففيه إشارة لوحدة الوجود، لكنه ممتنع لإيهامه الحلول. وقال أرضاه مولاه في مشكاة الأنوار (ص) (٥٦) : (ولم يفهموا - يعني : العارفين بالله تعالى ـ من معنى قوله : «الله أكبر أنه أكبر من غيره حاش لله ! إذ ليس في الوجود معه غيره حتى يكون أكبر منه بل ليس لغيره رتبة ،المعية، بل رتبة التبعية، بل ليس لغيره وجود إلا من الوجه الذي يليه، فالموجود وجهه فقط، ومحال أن يقال: إنه أكبر من وجهه بل معناها : أنه أكبر من أن يقال له : أكبر؛ بمعنى الإضافة ،والمقايسة، وأكبر من أن يدرك غيره كنه كبريائه، نبيًّا كان أو ملكاً، بل لا يعرف الله كنة معرفته إلا الله) .
Al-Hallaj: 'I am Allah,' and like some of them saying: 'There is none in the cloak except Allah.' This expression is not permissible legally because it is misleading. However, the people sometimes are overcome by [spiritual] states, so what occurs from them is interpreted appropriately. Among those who gave a fatwa to kill al-Hallaj when he said the previous statement is al-Junayd, as in the commentary on al-Kubra. And from the misleading expressions is what is common on the tongues of commoners from their saying: 'Exists in all existence.' In it is an indication of the unity of existence, but it is forbidden because it implies indwelling (al-hulul). And his patron, his lord, said in Mishkat al-Anwar: 'They did not understand — meaning the knowers of Allah the Exalted — from the meaning of His saying "Allahu Akbar" that He is greater than others, far be it from Allah! For there is no other in existence with Him such that He would be greater than him. Rather, there is for other than Him no rank of accompaniment (al-ma'iyya), but rather the rank of subordination. Rather, other than Him has no existence except from the aspect that follows from Him. So the existent is only His face. And it is impossible to say that He is greater than His face. Rather, its meaning is that He is too great to be said of Him 'greater' in the sense of addition and comparison, and too great for anyone other than Him to grasp the reality of His majesty, whether a prophet or an angel. Rather, Allah does not know the reality of His knowledge except Allah.'
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage forbids certain expressions that imply a form of union with God (wahdat al-wujud) and explains how to interpret such utterances. It begins with al-Hallaj's statement 'I am Allah' and a similar saying, declaring them legally impermissible because they are misleading (muwahhim). However, it acknowledges that sometimes the spiritual states of the gnostics overwhelm them, so their words are interpreted in a suitable manner. It also mentions that al-Junayd issued a fatwa to execute al-Hallaj. Then it addresses a common phrase 'exists in all existence' which also alludes to wahdat al-wujud and is prohibited as it suggests hulul (incarnation). Finally, it quotes from Mishkat al-Anwar to clarify the correct meaning of 'Allahu Akbar': it is not a comparative statement (since there is no other to compare), but rather an affirmation of God's transcendence beyond being understood by any other.

Correction: The passage clarifies that even if such statements arise from spiritual states, the literal wording is forbidden legally because it is misleading to others.

Rejecting the Claim That God Can Create the Impossible
Rejects a report about Ibn Arabi claiming God can create absurdities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God can create intellectual impossibilities is rejected and considered a forgery against Ibn Arabi.

Core Idea
Not every report about a scholar is true—especially if it contradicts established principles.

This lesson addresses a specific claim attributed to the famous mystic Ibn Arabi that God can create absurdities. Such a claim contradicts the foundational principle that God's power is limited to what is rationally possible. The source passage warns that this report is likely a forgery and must not be believed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall meaning. Note the two claims mentioned: (1) God can create intellectual impossibilities, (2) Ibn Arabi entered a city from Adam's leftover clay.
The passage is short but packed with a warning.
2
Identify the commentator's response
The commentator says the apparent meaning is not permissible to believe (la yajuz i'tiqad zahirih) and that the report is considered interpolated upon Ibn Arabi (madstis 'alayh).
This shows the authority of the commentator in rejecting false reports.
3
Reflect on the theological principle at stake
Ask yourself: Why would claiming God can create impossibilities be problematic? It contradicts the principle that God's power relates only to possible things.
This connects to the wider doctrine of divine attributes.
4
Consider the lesson for us today
This passage teaches us to verify reports, especially those that seem to contradict sound creed, and not to attribute false ideas to respected scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما في اليواقيت (۱) للشعراني عن ابن العربي: أنه تعالى يقدر على خلق المحال عقلاً، وأنه دخل الأرض المخلوقة من بقية خميرة طينة آدم، وهي مدينة إنما تدخلها الأرواح، فرأى فيها ذلك بعينه ...) كلام لا يجوز اعتقاد ظاهره، وقد نُقل أنه مدسوس عليه .
And what is in al-Yawaqit by al-Sha'rani, from Ibn al-Arabi: that He Almighty is able to create what is intellectually impossible, and that he entered the created earth from the remaining leaven of Adam's clay, and it is a city that only spirits enter, and he saw therein that very thing... ) — speech whose apparent meaning is not permissible to believe, and it has been reported that it was interpolated upon him.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a warning against a deviant claim. It references a story found in al-Yawaqit by al-Sha'rani, attributed to Ibn al-Arabi, that God can create intellectual absurdities (al-muḥāl 'aqlan) and that Ibn al-Arabi entered a city made from the leftover clay of Adam. The commentator dismisses this as speech whose outward meaning cannot be believed (lā yajūz i'tiqād ẓāhirih) and notes that it is reported to have been interpolated (madstis 'alayh) onto Ibn al-Arabi. Thus, the passage guards against accepting reports that contradict the well-established principle that divine power is limited to possibles.

Correction: The commentator explicitly rejects the outward meaning and notes it is considered an interpolation, so it cannot be used to establish doctrine.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Reports that contradict sound theology by claiming God can create impossibilities should be rejected as interpolations.

Sanusi's Refutation of Ibn Hazm on Divine Power
Sanusi rebukes Ibn Hazm for claiming God can take a son or be powerless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Sanusi rejects Ibn Hazm's claim that God could take a son, and how the orthodox doctrine limits divine power to possible things.

Core Idea
Divine power applies only to possibles, not to absurdities like taking a son.

In this passage, al-Sanusi responds to a controversial argument by Ibn Hazm, who claimed that God could take a son or else be incapable. Sanusi points out that Ibn Hazm misunderstands the scope of divine power. The lesson clarifies the crucial distinction between possible and impossible objects, showing why Ibn Hazm's reasoning leads to intolerable consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then read the English translation. Notice the strong tone of rebuke.
Sanusi uses the word 'shan'a' (denounced) – a strong condemnation.
2
Identify Ibn Hazm's claim
Find what Ibn Hazm argued. He said if God cannot take a son, He would be incapable (ʿājiz).
This might seem plausible initially, but it reveals a flawed understanding of power.
3
Examine Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi explains that incapability only applies when the object is within the functions of power – i.e., it must be a possible thing. Taking a son is impossible, so it is not a valid object.
The concept of 'mutalliq' (object) of power is key.
4
Trace the absurd consequences
Ibn Hazm's logic would imply that God could annihilate His own power and even His essence – utter corruption.
This reductio ad absurdum shows the error of Ibn Hazm's premise.
5
Internalize the lesson
Reflect on why this distinction matters for Islamic theology: it protects God's transcendence and avoids anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد شنّع السنوسي في شرح الصغرى على ابن حزم في قوله : (الله قادر أن يتخذ ولداً وإلا كان عاجزاً، ولم يعقل أن العجز إنما يكون إذا كان المتعلق من وظائف القدرة، بأن كان يقبلُ الوجود لذاته، ويلزم عليه أن المولى قادر على إعدام قدرته، بل وعلى إعدام ذاته، وفي ذلك غاية الفساد.
And al-Sanusi has denounced in Sharh al-Sughra Ibn Hazm for his saying: 'Allah is able to take a son, otherwise He would be incapable,' and he did not realize that incapability only occurs when the object pertains to the functions of power, such that it (the object) is receptive of existence by itself. And it follows from his statement that the Lord is able to annihilate His power, and even to annihilate His essence, and in that is utmost corruption.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a polemical refutation. It reports that al-Sanusi in his commentary Sharh al-Sughra strongly condemned Ibn Hazm's argument. Ibn Hazm claimed that if God could not take a son, He would be incapable (ʿājiz). Al-Sanusi responds that incapability is only relevant when the object in question is within the scope of power, i.e., it is a possible thing receptive of existence by itself. Since taking a son is impossible (as it would imply a partner), it is not a proper object of power. Ibn Hazm's line of reasoning leads to absurdities: that God could annihilate His own power and even His own essence, which is utterly corrupt (fāsid). The passage thus defends the orthodox position that power applies only to possibles.

Key Terms
شنع
عجز
متعلق
وظائف القدرة
إعدام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying God the power to do something (like take a son) means He is incapable.
Correction: Incapacity only applies when the object is possible; impossible actions are not proper objects of power.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does al-Sanusi say follows from Ibn Hazm's claim?
A) That God could create a son.
B) That God could annihilate His own power and essence.
C) That God would be powerless.
D) That God could create an impossible thing.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that God's power extends only to possible things, and that Ibn Hazm's claim leads to absurdities like God annihilating His own essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Hazm was a great scholar, yet his own reasoning about God's power led him to a conclusion that al-Sanusi called 'utmost corruption.' Is there an area of your life where you trust your own logic over what revelation and sound theology teach about Allah? What would it cost you to submit your reasoning to the limits He has set?
?
Sanusi points out that claiming God can do anything, even the impossible, leads to absurdities like God annihilating His own essence. When you think about God's power, do you ever secretly imagine He could contradict His own attributes? What does that reveal about your understanding of His perfection?
?
The passage teaches that 'incapability only occurs when the object pertains to the functions of power.' Are there desires or situations you think God should be able to change for you, even though they are impossible by His wisdom? How might you release that expectation and trust that His power is exercised only over what is compatible with His essence?

The Mu'tazilite Denial of Eternal Names
Refutes the Mu'tazilite denial of eternal divine names and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite denial of God's eternal names and attributes is considered more severe than the heresy of claiming the Quran is created.

Core Idea
Denying God's eternal attributes is worse than claiming the Quran is created.

This lesson examines a strong condemnation from Sunni scholars against the Mu'tazilite claim that God's names and attributes are not eternal. The passage quotes al-Shumunni, who declares this view even uglier than the infamous doctrine of the created Quran. By reading this short but powerful refutation, you will see how seriously Sunni theology guards the eternal perfection of Allah's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation.
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get a feel for the text.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite position.
Identify the three claims attributed to the Mu'tazila: no attributes before creation, attributes acquired after creation, and loss of attributes after annihilation.
This is the view being refuted.
3
Grasp the comparison with the created Quran.
Note al-Shumunni's statement that this view is 'uglier than the saying that the Quran is created.' Reflect on why this comparison is so strong.
The created Quran doctrine was a major heresy in Sunni Islam.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes.
Consider why eternal attributes are essential to Sunni theology and why denying them threatens God's perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإلهيات ولا صفاتٍ، فلما خلق الخلق جعلوها له، وبعد فنائهم يبقى بدونها، وهو قول المعتزلة، قال الشمني: وهو أقبح من القول بخلق القرآن. انتهى. أفاده العلامة الأمير مع بعض زيادة.
The divine matters: and no attributes. When He created creation, they [the Mu'tazila] made them [names and attributes] for Him, and after their annihilation He remains without them. This is the saying of the Mu'tazila. Al-Shumunni said: It is uglier than the saying that the Quran is created. End. This was conveyed by al-Allama al-Amir with some addition.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine that God's names and attributes are not eternal but are acquired after creation. The commentator begins by stating the Mu'tazilite view: before creation, God had no names or attributes; when He created, He acquired them from creation; after creation perishes, He loses them. The commentator then quotes Al-Shumunni, who condemns this view as worse than the claim that the Quran is created—a serious accusation, since the createdness of the Quran was a major heresy in Sunni Islam. The passage ends by attributing the report to al-Allama al-Amir with some addition.

Key Terms

خلق القرآن
الأسماء
الصفات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's names and attributes are acquired only after creation and are not eternal.
Correction: In Sunni theology, God's names and attributes are eternal and not dependent on creation. They are not acquired from creation.

B) It is less serious.
C) It is uglier.
D) It is praiseworthy.

Key Takeaway
Denying the eternity of God's names and attributes is a grave theological error, considered even more severe than the heresy of the created Quran.

The Mihna: Trials of Sunni Scholars
Narrates the historical inquisition and trials of Sunni scholars over the createdness of the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extent of the persecution faced by Sunni scholars during the Mihna and the key incidents involving al-Bukhari, Isa ibn Dinar, al-Shabi, al-Shafi'i, and Imam Ahmad.

Core Idea
The Mihna tested the faith of Sunni scholars; their steadfastness is a legacy of courage.

The historical episode known as the Mihna (inquisition) was a dark period when the caliphate imposed the doctrine that the Quran is created. Sunni scholars who upheld the uncreatedness of the Quran faced severe persecution. This passage from the Jawharat al-Tawhid commentary recounts the trials of several prominent scholars, illustrating their resilience and the divine support they received.

Guided Path
1
Understand the historical context
The Mihna was an inquisition initiated by the Abbasid caliph al-Ma'mun, who enforced the belief that the Quran is created. Sunni scholars who refused were persecuted. This lesson covers the specific trials of five scholars.
This context helps you appreciate the gravity of their suffering.
2
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the literal translation. Notice the brevity and the sequence of events.
The Arabic uses short, vivid statements for each scholar.
3
Identify each scholar and their trial
List the five scholars mentioned: al-Bukhari, Isa ibn Dinar, al-Shabi, al-Shafi'i, and Imam Ahmad. For each, note the specific persecution they endured (flight, imprisonment, clever evasion, dream vision, flogging).
Pay attention to al-Shabi's clever answer and the dream involving al-Shafi'i and Ahmad.
4
Reflect on the dream and the shirt
The dream narrative shows divine reassurance. Ahmad's gift of his shirt and al-Shafi'i's veneration of it symbolize barakah and solidarity among scholars.
This story highlights the spiritual dimension of the Mihna.
5
Connect to the broader lesson of steadfastness
Consider how these stories inspire unwavering commitment to truth despite worldly pressure. The Mihna is a reminder that faith must sometimes be defended at great personal cost.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد وقع في ذلك امتحان كبير لخلق كثير من أهل السنة : فخرج البخاري فارا، وقال: (اللهم اقبضني إليك غير مفتون)، فمات بعد أربعة وسُجن عيسى بن دينار عشرين سنة. وسُئل الشعبي فقال: أما التوراة والإنجيل والزبور والفرقان فهذه الأربعة حادثة، وأشار إلى أصابعه، فكانت سبب نجاته . واشتهرت أيضاً عن الإمام الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه . وحُبس الإمام أحمد، وضُرب بالسياط حتى غُشي عليه . ويُذكر أن النبي قال للإمام الشافعي في المنام : (بشر أحمد بالجنة على بلوى تصيبه في خلق القرآن)، فأرسل له كتاباً ببغداد، فلما قرأه بكي، ودفع للرسول قميصه الذي يلي جسده، وكان عليه قميصان، فلمَّا دُفِعَ للشافعي غسلَهُ وادَّهنَ بمائه .
And indeed there occurred in that a great trial for many of the Ahl al-Sunna concerning the question of the creation of the Quran: al-Bukhari fled, saying, 'O Allah, take me to You untried,' and he died after four [days]. And Isa ibn Dinar was imprisoned for twenty years. And al-Shabi was asked, and he said: 'As for the Torah, the Gospel, the Psalms, and the Criterion [the Quran]—these four are created,' and he pointed to his fingers, and that was the cause of his deliverance. And it also became famous concerning Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him. And Imam Ahmad was imprisoned and beaten with whips until he lost consciousness. And it is mentioned that the Prophet said to Imam al-Shafi'i in a dream: 'Give Ahmad glad tidings of Paradise because of a trial that will befall him concerning the creation of the Quran.' So he sent a letter to him in Baghdad, and when he read it he wept, and he gave the messenger his shirt that was next to his body, and he had two shirts on. When it was given to al-Shafi'i, he washed it and anointed himself with its water.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative recounting the famous Mihna (inquisition) over the createdness of the Quran. It lists several Sunni scholars who suffered: al-Bukhari fled and died shortly after; Isa ibn Dinar was imprisoned for twenty years; al-Shabi cleverly answered that the four scriptures are 'created' (محدثة) but saved himself by a gesture; the report is also attributed to al-Shafi'i; Ahmad ibn Hanbal was imprisoned and flogged. A story is added that the Prophet commanded al-Shafi'i in a dream to send a letter to Ahmad giving him glad tidings of Paradise due to the impending trial. Ahmad wept upon receiving the letter and gave his inner shirt to the messenger, which al-Shafi'i later washed and used for anointing. The passage illustrates the severe persecution faced by the Ahl al-Sunna for affirming that the Quran is uncreated.

Key Terms
امتحان
خلق القرآن
أهل السنة
البخاري
عيسى بن دينار
الشعبي
الإمام الشافعي
الإمام أحمد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Mihna was only about Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal.
Correction: This passage shows that many other Sunni scholars, including al-Bukhari, Isa ibn Dinar, al-Shabi, and al-Shafi'i, were also involved and suffered persecution.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face pressure to compromise your beliefs for societal acceptance, do you pray for deliverance like al-Bukhari, or do you stand firm like Ahmad? What would your breaking point be?
?
The scholars suffered imprisonment, flogging, and exile. How does your current level of sacrifice for your faith compare? What is one area where you have been avoiding discomfort to protect your deen?
?
Ahmad ibn Hanbal wept when he received the prophetic glad tidings via al-Shafi'i. If you were certain that a trial you face would lead to Paradise, would you embrace it with joy? Why or why not?

Moderate Jabriyya and the Maturidi View on Partial Will
Classification and doctrinal clarification on predestination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunna are called 'moderate Jabriyya' and how the Maturidi concept of partial will reconciles divine sovereignty with human responsibility.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Sunna's position on predestination is a nuanced middle path: the choice is necessary, but it is still our choice.

This passage introduces a key label used for Ahl al-Sunna—'intermediate Jabriyya'—and explains the Maturidi concept of partial will. It clarifies that human choice, though necessitated by God's decree, is not completely involuntary. The Maturidi school adds that the servant has a partial will which is a state (ḥāl), uncreated, and distinct from the universal will of God. This nuanced view avoids both extreme determinism and total free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to grasp the key terms: moderate Jabriyya, compulsion by mediation, partial will, state (ḥāl).
Pay attention to the phrase 'jabr bayna jabrayn' which is rejected.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage first defines the term 'intermediate Jabriyya' and then explains the Maturidi view on partial will.
The first part is about the label, the second about a specific doctrine.
3
Understand 'compulsion by mediation'
The servant's choice is necessary (iḍṭirārī) but not directly compelled; it is through the servant's own choice. This is 'jabr bil-wāsiṭa'.
This distinguishes Ahl al-Sunna from other groups.
4
Analyze the Maturidi view on partial will
Learn why the partial will is uncreated (as a state) and how it relates to God's universal will.
The concept of 'ḥāl' is unique to kalām; it means something that is neither fully existent nor non-existent.
5
Reflect on practical implication
While God creates actions, the servant has a real partial will that is not created, preserving human responsibility.
This is a key difference between Ash'aris and Maturidis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسمى أهل السنة بـ (الجبرية المتوسطة) أي: الجبر بالواسطة؛ أي: الاختيار المستند إليه اضطراري أيضاً، وليس المعنى جبر بين جبرين . وقد قال السادة الماتريدية بالإرادة الجزئية للعبد، وهي غير مخلوقة عندهم؛ لأنها حال، والحال لا تتعلق به القدرة؛ لأنه ليس له وجود محض ولا عدم محض فأفعال العباد مخلوقة الله فحسب، ولكن الإرادة الكلية لله، والتعلق الإرادة الجزئية يكون للعبد .
And Ahl al-Sunna are called 'the intermediate Jabriyya', i.e., compulsion (jabr) by mediation, meaning that the choice (al-ikhtiyār) upon which it is based is also necessary (iḍṭirārī). The meaning is not compulsion between two compulsions. And the Māturīdī masters affirmed partial will (al-irāda al-juzʾiyya) for the servant, which is uncreated according to them because it is a state (ḥāl), and a state is not related to power, since it is neither pure existence nor pure non-existence. Thus, the actions of servants are created by God alone, but the universal will (al-irāda al-kulliyya) belongs to God, and the attachment (al-taʿalluq) of the partial will belongs to the servant.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a theological classification and a clarification of the Māturīdī position on human will. It begins by stating that Ahl al-Sunna (the Ashʿarī and Māturīdī schools) are labeled as 'the intermediate Jabriyya' (moderate determinists). The term 'jabr bil-wāsiṭa' means 'compulsion by mediation': the servant's choice, which is the basis of his action, is itself necessary or compelled, but not directly – it is mediated by the servant's own choice. The phrase 'jabr bayna jabrayn' (compulsion between two compressions) is rejected; it is not that the servant is completely compelled like a lifeless object nor completely free. Then it presents the Māturīdī view on the partial will (al-irāda al-juzʾiyya). According to the Māturīdīs, the servant has a partial will that is not created by God because it is a 'state' (ḥāl), a concept in kalām that something that is neither fully existent nor non-existent. As a state, it is not an entity that can be created or have power applied to it. Therefore, God creates the actions themselves, but the servant's partial will is not created; it is a state that attaches to the action. The universal will (al-irāda al-kulliyya) belongs to God, meaning God's will encompasses everything. The partial will is the servant's own, but it is not a created object. This clarifies the Māturīdī position on free will and predestination, distinguishing it from both pure libertarianism and pure determinism.

Key Terms
الجبرية المتوسطة
الجبر بالواسطة
الإرادة الجزئية
الحال
الإرادة الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'intermediate Jabriyya' means the servant has no choice.
Correction: It actually means the choice itself is necessitated by God's decree, but it is still the servant's choice.

Checkpoint
According to the Māturīdīs, why is the partial will of the servant not created by God?
A) Because it is identical with the action, and God creates actions directly.
B) Because it is a state (ḥāl) that is neither pure existence nor pure non-existence.
C) Because it is an accident that does not persist.
D) Because the servant creates his own will.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna affirm both divine sovereignty and human responsibility by describing human choice as 'compelled by mediation' (jabr bil-wāsiṭa) and, for Maturidis, a partial will that is an uncreated state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that your choices are both necessitated by God's decree and yet truly your own. Identify one area of your life where you tend to blame God for your failures or take full credit for your successes. How does the doctrine of 'jabr bil-wāsiṭa' compel you to re-evaluate that attitude? Tonight, when you reflect on your day, actively attribute both the will to act and the action itself to God while still acknowledging your personal responsibility. What will you do differently tomorrow?
?
The Māturīdīs affirm a partial will that is uncreated—a state that is neither existence nor non-existence. When you consider your own desires and intentions, do you treat them as if they are fully your own or fully from God? How does this subtle distinction affect your sense of gratitude and humility before God? Write down one intention you had today and consciously recognize it as a state from God, not a creation of your own. How does that change your prayer?
?
You now know that Ahl al-Sunna are called 'intermediate Jabriyya'—the middle path between compulsion and free will. Are you truly living in that middle ground in your daily life, or do you veer towards fatalism (whatever happens is God's will) or self-reliance (it's all up to me)? Identify a specific situation this week where you will consciously practice this middle path: acknowledging God's sovereignty while taking full responsibility for your choice. What is that situation and what will you say to yourself?
