The Maturidi View on the Inevitability of Divine Threat
States Maturidi view on inevitability of threat and response to forgiven believer objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position on divine threats and how they reconcile it with the forgiveness of believers.

Core Idea
For Maturidis, God's threat is as binding as His promise for the unforgiven.

This lesson explores a key difference between Ash'aris and Maturidis: whether God can break His threat. The Maturidis argue that divine threat must be fulfilled just like promise, except for believers who are forgiven. They address an obvious objection: what about forgiven sinners? Their response hinges on interpreting general threat verses. Let's unpack their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the objection raised and how it is answered.
2
Identify the Maturidi position and the objection
Clearly state what the Maturidis believe about divine threats. Then identify the objection that arises from the case of a forgiven believer.
3
Understand the response and its implication
Notice how the Maturidis respond: they argue that forgiven believers are excluded from the general threat verses. This means the threat still applies to the unforgiven. Consider why this matters for the difference between Ash'aris and Maturidis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبت الماتريدية إلى أنه يمتنع تخلُّفُ الوعيد كما يمتنع تخلُّفُ الوعد، ولا يرد على ذلك أن الوعيد يتخلَّفُ في المؤمن المغفور له ؛ لأن الآيات الواردة بعموم الوعيد مُخْرَجٌ منها المؤمنُ المغفور له، وأما غير المغفور له .. فلا بد من نفوذ الوعيد فيه؛ فقولهم : لا بدَّ من إنفاذ الوعيد فيه ولو في واحد الآتي في قوله :
وواجب تعذيب بعض ارتكب
كبيرة .... إلى آخره
إنَّما يظهر على كلام الماتريدية، وينبني على الخلاف بين الأشاعرة
And the Maturidis held that the failure of the threat is impossible just as the failure of the promise is impossible, and it is not an objection against that that the threat fails in the case of the forgiven believer, because the verses that came with generality of the threat have the forgiven believer excluded from them. As for the one not forgiven, there must be execution of the threat upon him. So their saying: 'It is necessary to execute the threat upon him, even if only on one' – as [the author] will say in his saying: 'And it is obligatory to torture some who commits a major sin... etc.' Appears only in the speech of the Maturidis, and it is built upon the difference between the Ash'aris.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi position on divine threats. The Maturidis hold that it is impossible for God to break His threat just as it is impossible to break His promise. They address the apparent objection that threats are indeed broken in the case of a believer who is forgiven: they answer that the verses which provide general threats are understood to exclude the forgiven believer. Therefore, for the unforgiven, the threat must be executed. The author notes that the statement 'the threat must be executed, even if only on one' appears in the later line of the poem (referring to 'wa-wajib ta'dhib ba'd irtakaba kabirah' [and it is obligatory to torture some who commits a major sin]), and that this view is based on the difference between Ash'aris and Maturidis. The passage thus highlights a key sectarian difference: Ash'aris allow breaking the threat as an act of generosity, while Maturidis insist on its execution.

Key Terms
ماتريدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi view that threat must be fulfilled contradicts the possibility of forgiveness.
Correction: The Maturidis argue that forgiven believers are excluded from the general threat verses, so the threat is still fulfilled for those not forgiven.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi position, what happens to a believer who commits a major sin and is not forgiven?
A) He must be punished.
B) He is pardoned by default.
C) His punishment is deferred to the afterlife.
D) The threat does not apply to believers.

Key Takeaway
For Maturidis, God's threat is binding and must be executed on the unforgiven, while forgiven believers are excluded from the threat verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Reflect on a time you assumed God's forgiveness without considering the possibility that you might not be among the forgiven. How does this lesson's emphasis on the binding nature of the threat call you to a more serious repentance and accountability before God?

Disagreement on Forgiveness for All Believers
Difference on collective forgiveness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari and Maturidi disagreement over collective forgiveness is considered a real theological difference and not merely a verbal one.

Core Idea
A prayer for all believers' forgiveness exposes a real theological fault line between Ash'aris and Maturidis.

In this short but dense passage, the commentator explores a subtle theological disagreement between two major Sunni schools: the Ash'aris and the Maturidis. The question is whether it is permissible to ask Allah to forgive all believers for all their sins. This might seem like a minor point, but the author insists it reflects a deeper doctrinal divergence. Let's read the passage and uncover what makes this difference 'real' rather than 'verbal'.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage

The Ash'ari position is only implied, but the Maturidi position is directly mentioned.

The author states the disagreement is 'real' (haqiqi) despite some calling it verbal. Reflect on why the author insists on this classification.

4

A verbal difference would mean both schools agree in essence but differ in wording.
5
Summarize the lesson

Your summary should reflect the real nature of the disagreement.

Disagreement on Saying 'I am a Believer if Allah Wills'
Discussion of the permissibility of saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different rulings on saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills' according to various schools and the distinction between permissible and prohibited doubt.

Core Idea
Saying 'if Allah wills' about faith can be a blessing or a doubt, depending on intent.

This passage explores a nuanced theological and legal issue: whether a believer should qualify their faith by saying 'if Allah wills.' Different schools have different views based on their understanding of the nature of faith and the future. We'll examine the Ash'ari-Maturidi disagreement and the positions of early imams.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and footnote
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Pay attention to the footnote which elaborates on types of doubt.
The footnote is incomplete but gives the key distinction.
2
Identify the main positions
Note the Ash'ari view that it is permissible, the Maturidi view that it is not, and the differing views of Shafi'i, Malik, and Abu Hanifa.
3
Understand the prohibition and exception
The prohibition is tied to doubt. The footnote explains that doubt about perseverance in faith is prohibited, but doubt about the end (death state) is not, because the end is unknown.
4
Reflect on the intentions
The passage mentions that the phrase can be used for blessing (tabarruk), conditionality (ta'liq) with doubt, or simply unqualified. Consider how intent affects the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلافُ في قول القائل: (أنا مؤمنٌ إن شاء الله)] ويترتب على الخلاف بين الأشاعرة والماتريدية أنه يصح أن تقول: أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله على قول الأشاعرة، وأنَّه لا يصح ذلك على الثاني. وحكى بعضُهم في ذلك خلافاً على غير هذا الوجه حيث قال: جوزه الشافعي، ومنعه مالك وأبو حنيفة، وقال بعضُ أتباع مالك بوجوبه، وذلك إن لم يرد الشك(۳) (۳) قوله : (وذلك إن لم يرد الشك إلخ)، الشك الممنوع أن يتردد في أنه هل يستمر على الإيمان أو يقطعه، وأما لو تردد الآن في أنه هل يكون مؤمناً عند الموت أو لا، فهذا شك غير ممنوع؛ لأن الخاتمة مجهولة . والحاصل أن الآتي بالمشيئة، إما أن يقصد التبرك، وإما أن يقصد التعليق ومن لازمه الشك، وإما أن يطلق، وللتعليق باعتبار الشك اللازم صورتان؛ لأنه تارةً يشك في أنه هل يستمر على الإيمان =
The disagreement on the saying of one who says: 'I am a believer if Allah wills.' And it follows from the disagreement between the Ash'aris and the Maturidis that it is correct for you to say: I am a believer if Allah wills according to the Ash'ari position, and it is not correct according to the second (Maturidi). And some reported a disagreement on this in another manner, where (Imam) al-Shafi'i allowed it, Malik and Abu Hanifa prohibited it, and some followers of Malik deemed it obligatory, provided that it does not indicate doubt. (3) His statement: ('provided that it does not indicate doubt' etc.): The prohibited doubt is when one hesitates about whether he will continue upon faith or abandon it. As for hesitating now about whether he will be a believer at death or not, this is non-prohibited doubt because the end is unknown. And the conclusion is that the one who utters the phrase either intends blessing (tabarruk), or intends conditionality (ta'liq) which necessarily implies doubt, or he is unqualified. And conditionality in terms of the necessary doubt has two forms: because sometimes he doubts whether he will continue upon faith...
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills.' First, the author links this to the broader Ash'ari-Maturidi disagreement: According to the Ash'aris, since happiness (and thus final faith) is determined in the future, it is permissible to qualify one's current faith with a condition. For the Maturidis, faith is already present, so such qualification would imply doubt in one's current faith. Then a secondary disagreement is noted among the early imams: al-Shafi'i permitted it, Malik and Abu Hanifa forbade it, and some Malikis deemed it obligatory, with the condition that it does not express doubt. The footnote explains the distinction between prohibited doubt (about perseverance in faith) and permissible doubt (about the unknown end). The one who uses the phrase may have different intentions: blessing, conditionality (with doubt), or unqualified. The footnote begins to elaborate on the two forms of conditionality but is cut off.

Key Terms
أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله
تعليق
تبرك
شك
الخاتمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills' always implies doubt in one's faith.
Correction: It can be a form of seeking blessing (tabarruk) or acknowledging that the outcome (death in faith) is unknown, not necessarily doubting one's current faith.

Checkpoint
According to the footnote, which type of doubt is prohibited when saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills'?
A) Doubt about whether one is a believer now
B) Doubt about whether one will remain a believer until death
C) Doubt about the existence of God
D) Doubt about the truth of Islam

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills' depends on the intention: it may be a blessing or a condition about the unknown future, provided it does not express prohibited doubt about persevering in faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'if Allah wills' about your faith, do you truly intend blessing (tabarruk) or does it reveal an underlying doubt about your commitment to Allah? Examine your heart: is there any hesitation about whether you will continue on the path of faith?
?
The lesson teaches that doubting your state at death is permissible because the end is unknown. But does this knowledge make you more complacent or more urgent about securing a good end? What specific action will you take today to improve your certainty of faith?
?
You have likely used this phrase many times. Now that you understand the difference between a condition of blessing and a condition of doubt, will you continue to say it without thought? Or will you pause and reflect on your intention? What change will you make starting today?

The Branching: Reward and Punishment Are Not Earned
Explains the tafri' from divine creation and notes on humanity, angels, jinn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the line branches from Allah's exclusive creation of actions, and that humans have no inherent claim to reward or punishment.

Core Idea
Reward and punishment are pure grace and justice, not earned by acts.

This lesson begins the commentary on the poetic line by showing its logical connection to the earlier discussion of Allah's sole creation of actions. It establishes that since humans only acquire (kasb) and do not create their deeds, they do not deserve reward or punishment. The lesson also notes the agreed-upon fact that humans are rewarded and punished, with a brief aside on jinn.

Guided Path
1
Read the line and its tafri' explanation
First, read the Arabic line and its translation. Then carefully read the first paragraph of the commentary, which explains the branching (tafri') from the previous topic.
The faa (ف) here indicates a logical consequence, not necessarily temporal sequence.
2
Understand the basis for no deserving
The commentary states that servants have not done good or evil that would deserve reward or punishment. This is based on Allah being the sole creator of all actions.
3
Consider the note on humans, angels, and jinn
The tanbih clarifies that humans are definitely rewarded and punished, angels will be discussed later, and jinn have differing scholarly opinions. This shows the scope of the discussion.
This note reinforces that the principle applies primarily to humans.
4
Reflect on the overall principle
The core idea is that reward is pure grace and punishment is pure justice. This lesson establishes that they are not obligatory or deserved.

فَإِنْ يُثِبْنَا فَبِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ وَإِنْ يُعَذِّبْ فَبِمَحْضِ العَدْلِ قوله : (فَإِنْ يُشِبْنَا ... إلخ) مفرّح على ما تقدَّم من وجوب انفراده تعالى بخلق خير أفعال العباد، وأنَّه ليس لهم فيها سوى الكسب، ووجه التفريع أنّه لم يحصل منهم يستحقون به ثواباً، ولا شر يستحقون به عقاباً، فالفاء للتفريع، ويصح أن تكون فاء الفصيحة؛ لأنَّها أفصحت عن شرط محذوف، والتقدير : إذا علمت انفراده تعالی بخلق أفعالنا خيراً كانت أو شرًا فإن يثبنا .. إلى آخره . تنبيه : اتفقوا على أن بني آدم مثابون ومعاقبون. وأما الملائكة فسيأتي الكلام في إثابتهم عند قول المصنف: (بكل عبد حافظون وكلوا). وأما الجن فقد اتفق العلماء على أنَّ كافرهم مُعذِّبٌ في الآخرة، واختلف في مؤمنهم على أقوال، فقيل: إنَّهم كالإنس فيثابون على الطاعة، ويعاقبون على المعصية، وقيل: لا ثواب لهم إلا النجاة من النار، ثم يقال لهم : كونوا تراباً كالبهائم، وقيل : يكونون في ربض الجنة يراهم الإنس من حيث لا يرونهم، عكس ما كانوا عليه في الدنيا، وقيل: يكونون في الأعراف، ذكره الجلال السيوطي مع ما يشهد لكل من الأحاديث. انتهى شنواني بتصرف. قوله : (فَبِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ) أي: فإثابته لنا إنَّما هي بفضله المحض ؛ أي: الخالص، فالإضافة في كلامه من إضافة الصفة للموصوف، ومعنى الفضل المحض : الإعطاء عن اختيار كامل لا عن إيجاب، بحيث يثيبنا ولا اختيار له في الإثابة أبداً؛ لكونها علة تنشأ عنها معلولاتها من غير اختيار لها، كما يقوله الحكماء، ولا عن وجوب بحيث تصير الإثابة مستحقة لازمة يقبح عليه تعالى تركها فيثيبنا باختياره لكن مع الوجوب كما يقوله المعتزلة، فمذهب أهل السنة أنَّ إثابته تعالى لنا بالفضل الخالص غير مشوبة بإيجاب ولا وجوب. فقولنا : (بالفضل) ردُّ لكلام الحكماء. وقولنا : (الخالص) ردُّ لكلام المعتزلة، ويدلُّ لمذهب أهل السنة أنَّ طاعات العبد وإن كثرتُ لا تفي بشكر بعض ما أنعم الله به عليه فكيف يتصور استحقاقه عوضاً عليها؟ . قوله : (وَإِنْ يُعَذِّبْ فَبِمَحْضِ العَدْلِ أي : وإن يعذبنا فتعذيبه إنَّما هو بالعدل المحض (۱)؛ أي: الخالص، فالإضافة في كلامه من إضافة الصفة للموصوف كما مرَّ في نظيره ومعنى العدل المحض وضع الشيء في محله من غير اعتراض على الفاعل، ضدُّ الظلم الذي هو وضع الشيء في غير محله مع الاعتراض على فاعله . (۱) قوله : (بالعدل المحض) وصف العدل بأنه محض لبيان الواقع ؛ لأن عدل الله لا يكون إلا محضاً. وقوله : ( معنى العدل المحض) الأولى إسقاط (المحض) ؛ لأن ما ذكره معنى للعدل بقطع النظر عن كونه محضاً، وعبارة المصنف في شرحه: (ومعنى (العدل)، ولم يذكر لفظ المحض، والمقصود بقول المصنف : (وإن يعذب فبمحض العدل الردُّ على المعتزلة في قولهم بوجوب تعذيب العاصي، لقولهم بوجوب إثابة الطائع ، وبَنَوا ذلك على قاعدتهم من أن العبد يخلق أفعال نفسيه الاختيارية التي منها الطاعة والمعصية. وأما أهل السنة فقاعدتهم أن الله هو الخالق للأفعال كلّها ومنها الطاعة والمعصية، وبنوا على ذلك أن الإثابة بالفضل والتعذيب بالعدل، وليسا واجبين عليه تعالى، يُفهم ذلك كله من شرح صاحب المتن. انتهى أجهوري.
So if He rewards us, it is by pure grace, and if He punishes, it is by pure justice. His saying: (فَإِنْ يُثِبْنَا ... etc.) is a branching off from what preceded of the necessity of His exclusive creation of the good actions of servants, and that they have only acquisition (kasb) in them. The aspect of branching is that they have not done good by which they deserve reward, nor evil by which they deserve punishment. So the faa is for branching, and it may be a faa of eloquent brevity because it indicates a deleted condition, the estimation: if you know His exclusive creation of our actions, whether good or evil, then if He rewards us... etc. A note: They agreed that the children of Adam are rewarded and punished. As for angels, discussion of their reward will come at the author's saying: 'With every servant guardians and eaters.' As for jinn, the scholars agreed that their disbelievers are punished in the afterlife, and they differed regarding their believers. It is said they are like humans, rewarded for obedience and punished for disobedience. It is said they have no reward except salvation from fire, then they are told: 'Be dust like animals.' It is said they are in the gardens of Paradise, seen by humans but not seeing them, opposite of what they were in the world. It is said they are on the heights. Al-Suyuti mentioned this along with hadiths that support each view. Ends the Shanawani paraphrase. His saying: (فَبِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ) i.e., His reward to us is only by His pure grace, i.e., unadulterated. So the possessive construct in his speech is of the adjective to the described. The meaning of pure grace: giving by complete choice, not by obligation, such that He rewards us while having no choice in the reward at all because it is a cause that produces its effects without choice, as the philosophers say, nor by necessitation such that reward becomes obligatory and incumbent, making it ugly for Him to abandon, so He rewards us by His choice but with obligation, as the Mu'tazila say. So the Sunni school is that His reward to us is by pure grace unmixed with imposition or obligation. So our saying 'by grace' is a refutation of the philosophers, and our saying 'the pure' is a refutation of the Mu'tazila. And that the servant's acts of obedience, even if numerous, do not suffice to thank even a little of what Allah has bestowed upon him, so how can he deserve compensation for them? His saying: (وَإِنْ يُعَذِّبْ فَبِمَحْضِ العَدْلِ) i.e., if He punishes us, His punishment is only by pure justice, i.e., unadulterated. So the possessive construct is of the adjective to the described as before. And the meaning of pure justice: placing a thing in its proper place without objection to the doer, opposite of injustice which is placing a thing in its wrong place while objecting to the doer. The footnote says: His description of justice as pure is to clarify the reality, because Allah's justice can only be pure. And his saying 'the meaning of pure justice' would be better without 'pure' because what he mentioned is the meaning of justice regardless of purity. And the author in his commentary said 'and the meaning of justice' and did not mention the word 'pure'. And the purpose of the author's saying 'and if He punishes, it is by pure justice' is refutation of the Mu'tazila in their saying that punishment of the disobedient is obligatory, based on their principle that the slave creates his own voluntary actions including obedience and disobedience. As for the Sunnis, their principle is that Allah is the creator of all actions including obedience and disobedience, and they base on that that reward is by grace and punishment by justice, and both are not obligatory upon Him. All this is understood from the commentary of the author of the text. Ends the Ajhuri.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage explains the line that states if Allah rewards us, it is by pure grace; if He punishes, it is by pure justice. The commentator first shows how this line follows the previous discussion about Allah's exclusive creation of actions (the 'faa' is for branching). Then a note clarifies that humans are definitely rewarded and punished, angels will be discussed later, and jinn have differing scholarly opinions. The main commentary then defines 'pure grace' as giving purely by choice without any obligation or necessitation, refuting both the philosophers (who think reward follows necessarily as a cause) and the Mu'tazila (who think reward is obligatory). For punishment, 'pure justice' means placing things properly, refuting the Mu'tazila who claim punishment is obligatory for sin. The footnote adds that describing justice as 'pure' is for emphasis and that the author's intent is to refute the Mu'tazili doctrine of obligation.

Key Terms
ثواب (reward)
عقاب (punishment)
فضل (grace)
عدل (justice)
كسب (acquisition)
تفريع (branching)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why do servants not deserve reward or punishment?
A) Because they are not good enough
B) Because Allah creates all actions and they only acquire them
C) Because reward and punishment are not part of Islam
D) Because they have free will

Key Takeaway
Reward and punishment are not deserved; they are acts of pure grace and justice based on Allah's sole creation of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that your good deeds do not earn you reward, but that any reward is pure grace from Allah, how does that change your motivation in worship? Will you still strive, or will you rely solely on His grace? What does that mean for your daily prayers and charity?
?
Consider a sin you have committed. The lesson says punishment is pure justice. Does that make you fear Allah more, knowing He is perfectly just? How does this affect your repentance and your hope in His mercy?
?
This lesson claims that humans have not done any good deserving reward. Does that make your acts of worship feel meaningless, or does it humble you and increase your gratitude? How will you respond the next time you feel proud of your good deeds?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
6 min
Pure Grace and Pure Justice Defined
Defines pure grace and pure justice, refuting philosophers and Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meanings of 'pure grace' and 'pure justice' as applied to Allah's reward and punishment, and how they refute the philosophers and Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Pure grace means no obligation; pure justice means no injustice.

This lesson delves into the commentary's detailed explanation of the two key terms: 'pure grace' (al-faḍl al-maḥḍ) and 'pure justice' (al-ʿadl al-maḥḍ). The commentator defines each term in contrast to erroneous views: the philosophers who think reward follows necessarily, and the Mu'tazila who consider it obligatory. Similarly, punishment is defined as placing things in their proper place, refuting the Mu'tazila claim that punishment is obligatory for sin. These definitions establish the Sunni position that Allah acts by pure grace and justice, free from any external compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of pure grace
Read the paragraph beginning with 'قوله : (فَبِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ)'. Notice how the commentator distinguishes between grace given by choice versus obligation or necessitation.
The term 'pure' (محض) indicates unmixed, without any trace of obligation.
2
Identify the two refutations
The commentary explicitly says that 'by grace' refutes the philosophers (who say reward is necessary cause) and 'pure' refutes the Mu'tazila (who say it is obligatory).
This is a key point of Sunni theology.
3
Read the definition of pure justice
Read the paragraph on pure justice. Note the contrast with injustice: placing things in their proper place vs. placing them wrongly.
4
Understand the refutation of Mu'tazila on punishment
The commentary states that the Mu'tazila believe punishment is obligatory for the disobedient, while Sunnis say it is by pure justice and not obligatory.
This ties back to the earlier point about reward not being obligatory.
5
Reflect on the Sunni principle
The final part of the commentary summarizes the Sunni position: Allah is the creator of all actions, reward is by grace, punishment by justice, and neither is obligatory.
This is the doctrinal conclusion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that Allah is obligated to reward the obedient and punish the disobedient.
Correction: Sunnis believe reward is pure grace and punishment is pure justice; neither is obligatory upon Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what do the philosophers (al-ḥukamā') believe about divine reward?
A) It is obligatory
B) It is necessary as a cause-effect
C) It is by grace
D) It is by justice

Key Takeaway
Allah’s reward is pure grace (no obligation), and His punishment is pure justice (no injustice); both refute sectarian errors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that your acts of obedience do not suffice to thank Allah for even a little of His blessings. Do you secretly rely on your deeds to 'earn' Allah's reward? How does this lesson expose that attitude, and what would it mean for you to truly depend on His pure grace?
?
If Allah punished you right now for your sins, it would be pure justice. Would you accept that as fair? Reflect on your usual expectation that He should forgive you. How does the reality of pure justice and pure grace change how you approach Him in your daily life?
?
The Mu'tazila felt that God must reward the obedient or else He would be unjust. Do you find a similar sense of entitlement in your heart? What would it look like to surrender that expectation and instead live in humble gratitude for unearned grace?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Doctrines of Wujub al-Salah and al-Aslah
The author refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrines of wujub al-salah and wujub al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school rejects the Mu'tazilite claim that Allah is obligated to do what is beneficial or most beneficial, and how this follows from the principle of divine freedom.

Core Idea
Nothing is obligatory upon Allah; He is free to act according to His will.

In this lesson, we examine a crucial point of difference between the Ash'ari and Mu'tazilite schools. The Mu'tazilites argue that Allah must do what is beneficial (salah) and even what is most beneficial (aslah). The Ash'ari response, stated concisely by the author, reasserts divine freedom. Let's read the passage and understand the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the key terms: الصلاح, الأصلح, الفساد.
2
Identify the two Mu'tazilite formulations
The passage explains two statements: wujub al-salah (obligation of the beneficial) and wujub al-aslah (obligation of the most beneficial). Note how they are defined.
Salah is what is better than fasad; aslah is what is better than salah.
3
Understand the refutation strategy
The author refutes only wujub al-salah, but this also refutes wujub al-aslah because salah is more general. Understand why invalidating the general invalidates the specific.
This is a logical deduction: if the broader claim is false, the narrower one cannot stand.
4
Appreciate the author's conciseness
The author does not elaborate on the Mu'tazilite position; he merely refutes it. His goal is correction, not exposition.
The commentator explains that this is due to the author's purpose being refutation.
5
Connect to the broader principle
Recall the earlier principle that Allah may do or leave any possible act. This refutation is a direct application of that principle.

عدم وجوب الصلاح والأضلح على الله تعالى] ٥١ - وَقَوْلُهُمْ: إِنَّ الصَّلَاحَ وَاجِبُ عَلَيْهِ زُورٌ، مَا عَلَيْهِ وَاجِبُ. قوله : (وَقَوْلُهُمْ ... إلخ) هذا عُلم ممَّا تقدم من أنَّه يجوز في حقه تعالى فِعْلُ كلِّ ممكن وتركُهُ، لكن لما كان خطر الجهل في هذا الفن عظيماً .. لم يكتف فيه إلا بالتصريح. و(قولهم) مبتدأ وخبره (زور) ، والضمير عائد على المعتزلة، وإن لم يتقدم لهم ذكر الشهرة هذا المذهب عنهم. وجملة قوله : (إِنَّ الصَّلَاحَ وَاجِبٌ) مقول (قولهم). واعلم أن للمعتزلة عبارتين : الأولى : وجوب الصلاح، والمراد به ما قابل الفساد كالإيمان في مقابلة الكفر، فيقولون : إذا كان هناك أمران: أحدهما صلاح والآخر فساد وجب على الله أن يفعل الصلاح منهما دون الفساد. والثانية: وجوبُ الأصلح، والمراد به ما قابل الصلاح، ككونه في أعلى الجنان في مقابلة كونه في أسفلها، فيقولون : إذا كان هناك أمران أحدهما صلاح والآخر أصلحُ منه ، وجب على الله أن يفعل الأصلح منهما، دون الصلاح، والمصنف تكلَّم في إبطال مذهبهم على الأول دون الثانية ؛ لأنَّ الصلاح أعم من الأصلح، وإذا بطل الأعمُّ بطل الأخصُّ، وفي كلام المصنفِ إجمال في نسبة القول بذلك إليهم، لعدم تعلّق غرضه بمذهبهم، وإنَّما غرضه الردُّ عليهم .
The non-obligation of al-salah and al-aslah upon God Almighty. 51 - And their statement: 'Verily al-salah is obligatory upon Him' is false; there is nothing obligatory upon Him. His saying (And their statement... etc.): This is known from what has preceded, that it is permissible in His right, exalted, to do and to leave every possible thing, but because the danger of ignorance in this art is great, he was not satisfied except with explicit mention. And (their statement) is the subject and its predicate is (false), and the pronoun refers to the Mu'tazilites, though their mention has not preceded, due to the fame of this doctrine from them. And the clause 'Verily al-salah is obligatory' is the content of 'their statement'. Know that the Mu'tazilites have two expressions: The first: wujub al-salah, and what is meant by it is what is opposed to corruption (al-fasad), like faith in contrast to disbelief. They say: If there are two matters, one being salah and the other fasad, then it is obligatory upon God to do the salah of the two to the exclusion of the fasad. The second: wujub al-aslah, and what is meant by it is what is opposed to salah, like being in the highest paradise in contrast to being in its lowest. They say: If there are two matters, one being salah and the other more beneficial (aslah) than it, then it is obligatory upon God to do the aslah of the two, to the exclusion of the salah. The author (al-Musannif) spoke about refuting their doctrine concerning the first without the second, because al-salah is more general than al-aslah, and when the more general is invalidated, the more specific is invalidated. And in the author's speech there is brevity in attributing that saying to them, due to his purpose not being concerned with their doctrine, but rather his purpose is to refute them.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine that it is obligatory for God to do what is beneficial (salah) and even what is most beneficial (aslah). The author begins with the line of the poem: 'And their statement that al-salah is obligatory upon Him is false; there is nothing obligatory upon Him.' The pronoun 'their' refers to the Mu'tazilites. The commentary explains that this refutation is built on the previously established principle that God may do or leave any possible act; nothing is incumbent upon Him. However, due to the gravity of ignorance in this field, explicit mention is necessary. The commentator then clarifies the two Mu'tazilite formulations: 1) Wujub al-salah: When faced with a choice between a beneficial act (salah) and a corrupt one (fasad), God must choose the beneficial. 2) Wujub al-aslah: When there is a choice between a beneficial act and a more beneficial one (aslah), God must choose the more beneficial. The author only directly refutes the first, but that is sufficient because salah is more general than aslah; invalidating the general invalidates the specific. The commentator notes that the author's wording is concise in attributing the doctrine to the Mu'tazilites because his goal is simply to refute them, not to elaborate on their position.

Key Terms
الصلاح
الأصلح
الفساد
وجوب الصلاح
وجوب الأصلح
المعتزلة
زور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari rejection of wujub al-salah means that Allah acts arbitrarily without any purpose.
Correction: The rejection only means that Allah's actions are not subject to external obligations; He acts according to His own wisdom and will, which is not bound by human notions of benefit.

Checkpoint
What are the two Mu'tazilite doctrines refuted in this passage?
A) Wujub al-tawhid and wujub al-nubuwwa.
B) Wujub al-salah and wujub al-aslah.
C) Wujub al-'adl and wujub al-hikma.
D) Wujub al-qada' and wujub al-qadar.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite claim of obligation upon Allah is false; refuting the general claim of wujub al-salah suffices to refute the more specific claim of wujub al-aslah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is not obligated to give you what is most beneficial, how does that challenge your expectations of Him when you face hardship? Are you willing to trust His choices even when they seem less than optimal to you?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought they could determine what Allah 'must' do. In what areas of your life do you find yourself telling Allah what He 'should' do for you? What would it look like to surrender that judgment?
?
When you pray for something and don't receive it, do you ever feel that Allah 'owes' you a better outcome? How does this lesson's truth—that nothing is obligatory upon Him—reshape your attitude in supplication and contentment?

The Mu'tazila Doctrine of Wujub al-Salah and al-Aslah
Explains the Mu'tazila doctrine of wujub al-salah and al-Aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila position on the obligation of al-salah and al-Aslah, the differences between the Baghdad and Basra schools, and why the Ash'ari refutes only the first.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila claimed God must choose the best option, but they disagreed on its scope.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid explains the Mu'tazila doctrine that God is obligated to do what is right (al-salah) and what is most right (al-Aslah) for His servants. The commentator details their two expressions and the internal divisions within the Mu'tazila school. He then notes that the author refutes only the first expression because a refutation of the general covers the specific.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Mu'tazila expressions
Read the passage to find the two expressions: 'wujub al-salah' and 'wujub al-Aslah'. Understand how they define each term.
Al-salah is opposite of corruption; al-Aslah is the better of two good options.
2
Distinguish Baghdad and Basra schools
Note the difference: Baghdadis say obligation covers religion and worldly affairs; Basrans limit it to religion only.
Also note their different definitions of al-Aslah.
3
Understand the refutation strategy
The author refutes only the obligation of al-salah, because if the more general obligation is false, the more specific (al-Aslah) is automatically false.
This is a logical economy in the argument.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this doctrine is problematic from an Ash'ari perspective: it imposes a duty on God, which contradicts His absolute freedom.
This connects to the broader Ash'ari commitment to divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عدم وجوب الصلاح والأصلح على الله تعالى]
٥١ - وَقَوْلُهُمْ: إِنَّ الصَّلَاحَ وَاجِبُ عَلَيْهِ زُورٌ، مَا عَلَيْهِ وَاجِبُ ) قوله : (وَقَوْلُهُمْ ... إلخ) هذا عُلم ممَّا تقدم من أنَّه يجوز في حقه تعالى فِعْلُ كلِّ ممكن وتركُهُ، لكن لما كان خطر الجهل في هذا الفن عظيماً .. لم يكتف فيه إلا بالتصريح.
و(قولهم) مبتدأ وخبره (زور) ، والضمير عائد على المعتزلة، وإن لم يتقدم لهم ذكر الشهرة هذا المذهب عنهم.
وجملة قوله : (إِنَّ الصَّلَاحَ وَاجِبٌ) مقول (قولهم).
واعلم أن للمعتزلة عبارتين :
الأولى : وجوب الصلاح، والمراد به ما قابل الفساد كالإيمان في مقابلة الكفر، فيقولون : إذا كان هناك أمران: أحدهما صلاح والآخر فساد وجب على الله أن يفعل الصلاح منهما دون الفساد.
والثانية: وجوبُ الأصلح، والمراد به ما قابل الصلاح، ككونه في أعلى الجنان في مقابلة كونه في أسفلها، فيقولون : إذا كان هناك أمران أحدهما صلاح والآخر أصلحُ منه ، وجب على الله أن يفعل الأصلح منهما، دون الصلاح، والمصنف تكلَّم في إبطال مذهبهم على الأول دون الثانية ؛ لأنَّ الصلاح أعم من الأصلح، وإذا بطل الأعمُّ بطل الأخصُّ، وفي كلام المصنفِ إجمال في نسبة القول بذلك إليهم، لعدم تعلّق غرضه بمذهبهم، وإنَّما غرضه الردُّ عليهم .
والحاصل : أنَّهم قالوا بوجوب الصلاح والأصلح عليه تعالى، ثم اختلفوا : فذهب معتزلة بغداد إلى أنَّه يجب على الله تعالى مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح لعباده في الدين والدنيا، وذهبت معتزلة البصرة إلى أنه يجب عليه تعالى مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح لهم في الدين فقط، ثم اختلفوا أيضاً في المراد بالأصلح، فعند البغدادية : الأوفق في الحكمة والتدبير، وعند البصرية : الأنفع.
وهذه المسألة كانت سبباً لافتراق الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري من شيخه أبي هاشم الجبائي، فإن أبا الحسن سأل الجبائي في درسه، وقال: ما تقول في ثلاثة إخوة؛ أي: مثلاً، مات أحدهم كبيراً مطيعاً، والآخر كبيراً عاصياً، والثالث صغيراً، فقال الجبائي : الأول يُثاب بالجنة، والثاني يُعاقب بالنار، والثالث لا يُثاب ولا يُعاقب. فقال له الأشعري فإن قال الثالث : يا رَبِّ ؛ لم أمتني صغيراً، وما أبقيتني فأطيعك فأدخل الجنة؟ ماذا يقول الرَّبُّ؟
فقال الجُبائي : يقول الرَّبُّ : إني أعلم أنَّك لو كبرت عصيت فتدخل النار، فكان الأصلح لك أن تموت صغيراً .
فقال الأشعري : فإن قال الثاني : يا رَبِّ؛ لِمَ لَمْ تمتني صغيراً فلا أدخل النار؟ ماذا يقول الرَّبُّ؟ فَبُهِت الجبائي فترك الأشعري مذهبه واشتغل هو وأتباعه بإبطال ما ذهبت إليه المعتزلة، وإثباتِ ما وردت به السنة، ومضى عليه الجماعة، فلذلك سُمُّوا بأهل السنة والجماعة .
[The heading: The non-obligation of al-salah and al-Aslah upon Allah, exalted is He.]
51 - And their saying: 'Indeed, al-salah is obligatory upon Him' is false; nothing is obligatory upon Him. His saying (And their saying... etc.): This is known from what preceded, that it is permissible with respect to Him, exalted is He, to do every possible thing and to leave it. However, since the danger of ignorance in this art is great, he was not content with it except by explicit statement.
And (their saying) is a subject, and its predicate is (false), and the pronoun refers to the Mu'tazila, even if there was no prior mention of them due to the fame of this doctrine from them.
And the clause of His saying: (Indeed, al-salah is obligatory) is the object of (their saying).
Know that the Mu'tazila have two expressions: The first: The obligation of al-salah, and by it is meant what is opposite of corruption, like faith in contrast to disbelief. They say: If there are two matters, one of which is al-salah and the other corruption, then it is obligatory upon God to do al-salah from them, not corruption. The second: The obligation of al-Aslah, and by it is meant what is opposite of al-salah, like being in the highest of gardens in contrast to being in its lowest. They say: If there are two matters, one of which is al-salah and the other is more right (aslah) than it, then it is obligatory upon God to do the al-Aslah from them, not al-salah. And the author (al-Laqani) spoke about invalidating their doctrine with respect to the first without the second, because al-salah is more general than al-Aslah, and if the more general is invalid, the more specific is invalid. And in the author's speech there is succinctness in attributing that statement to them, because his purpose was not attached to their doctrine; rather his purpose was to refute them.
And the summary: That they said there is an obligation of al-salah and al-Aslah upon Him, exalted is He. Then they differed: The Mu'tazila of Baghdad went to that it is obligatory upon God, exalted is He, to observe al-salah and al-Aslah for His servants in religion and this world. And the Mu'tazila of Basra went to that it is obligatory upon Him, exalted is He, to observe al-salah and al-Aslah for them in religion only. Then they also differed regarding what is meant by al-Aslah: according to the Baghdadis, it is the most conducive in wisdom and management; according to the Basrans, it is the most beneficial.
And this issue was the cause for the separation of Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari from his teacher Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i. For Abu al-Hasan asked al-Jubba'i in his lesson, saying: What do you say about three brothers—for example—one of them died as an adult obedient, the other as an adult disobedient, and the third as a child? Al-Jubba'i said: The first is rewarded with Paradise, the second is punished with Hellfire, and the third is neither rewarded nor punished. Al-Ash'ari said to him: If the third says, 'O my Lord, why did You cause me to die as a child and not keep me alive so that I could obey You and enter Paradise?'—what would the Lord say? Al-Jubba'i said: The Lord would say, 'I know that if you had grown up you would have disobeyed and entered Hellfire, so it was more right (aslah) for you to die as a child.' Al-Ash'ari said: If the second says, 'O my Lord, why did You not cause me to die as a child so that I would not enter Hellfire?'—what would the Lord say? Al-Jubba'i was confounded. So al-Ash'ari abandoned his doctrine, and he and his followers occupied themselves with invalidating what the Mu'tazila had gone to and affirming what is reported by the Sunnah and what the community (al-jama'a) followed. For that reason they are called Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the verse (line 51) of the poem 'Jawharat al-Tawhid'. It begins by explaining that the statement 'Indeed, al-salah is obligatory upon Him' is false (zoor). The commentator notes that this is known from the general principle that God may do or not do any possible thing, but due to the danger of ignorance, explicit mention is needed. The pronoun in 'their saying' refers to the Mu'tazila. The Mu'tazila have two positions: the obligation of al-salah (what is right as opposed to corrupt) and the obligation of al-Aslah (the most right, i.e., the better of two good options). For al-salah, they say if there are two options, one right and one corrupt, God must choose the right. For al-Aslah, they say if there are two right options, God must choose the better one. The commentator notes that the author (Ibn 'Ashir) refutes only the first because the general refutation suffices for both (since if al-salah is not obligatory, al-Aslah is not either). The summary then states that the Mu'tazila are unanimous on the obligation of al-salah and al-Aslah, but they differ: the Baghdadis extend it to both religion and worldly affairs, the Basrans restrict it to religion. They also differ on the definition of al-Aslah: the Baghdadis say it is what is most conducive to wisdom, the Basrans say it is what is most beneficial. The passage then narrates the famous debate that led to al-Ash'ari's conversion: al-Ash'ari asked al-Jubba'i about three brothers—one obedient adult, one disobedient adult, and one child. Al-Jubba'i's responses led to a contradiction: if the child would have been obedient if allowed to live, why was he killed? And if the disobedient adult would have been better off dead, why was he kept alive? Al-Jubba'i was confounded, and al-Ash'ari abandoned Mu'tazilism, dedicating himself to refuting their doctrines and affirming the Sunni position, which follows the revealed texts and the consensus of the early community (al-Salaf). Thus they are called Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah.

Key Terms
الصلاح
الأصلح
وجوب الصلاح
وجوب الأصلح
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila held identical views on God's obligation.
Correction: The passage shows that the Baghdad and Basra schools differed on scope and definition of al-Aslah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do the Mu'tazila of Baghdad and Basra differ regarding the obligation of al-salah and al-Aslah?
A) Baghdadis limit it to religion; Basrans include worldly affairs.
B) Baghdadis include religion and worldly affairs; Basrans limit it to religion.
C) Both limit it to religion.
D) Both include religion and worldly affairs.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila doctrine of wujub al-salah and al-Aslah was not a single position but had internal divisions; the Ash'ari refutation targets the general principle, making fine-grained refutations unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face two good choices, do you ever assume that God is obligated to let you choose the better one? How does the Mu'tazila claim reflect a subtle desire to hold God accountable to your own standard of 'better'? Will you surrender your judgment to God's wisdom, even if His choice seems less beneficial to you?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted God must observe 'al-salah and al-Aslah' for His servants. In your own life, have you ever felt that God 'should have' given you a better outcome? What does that feeling reveal about your view of God's rights versus your own? Are you willing to affirm that God owes you nothing, yet He gives generously?
?
This lesson shows that the Mu'tazila themselves could not agree on what 'al-Aslah' means. When you claim to know what is best for you, are you sure you are not imposing a limited human definition on God's infinite wisdom? How can you train yourself to say, 'God knows what is truly best, even when I don't understand'?

The Debate That Converted al-Ash'ari
Recounts the debate that led al-Ash'ari to abandon Mu'tazilism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logic of the debate between al-Ash'ari and al-Jubba'i over three brothers, and how it exposed a fatal flaw in the Mu'tazila doctrine, leading al-Ash'ari to abandon their school.

Core Idea
A simple story about three brothers revealed the inconsistency of Mu'tazila divine obligation.

This passage narrates the famous debate that led Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari to leave the Mu'tazila. He asked his teacher al-Jubba'i about three brothers with different fates. The teacher's answers led to a contradiction when the brothers questioned God's justice. This story is a key moment in Islamic theological history, marking the birth of Ash'arism.

Guided Path
1
Read the debate story carefully
Read the Arabic passage describing the three brothers: one righteous adult, one sinful adult, and one child who died young. Notice al-Jubba'i's initial answers.
2
Analyze al-Ash'ari's first objection
The third brother (child) asks why he was not kept alive to obey. Al-Jubba'i responds that God knew he would disobey if he lived, so it was better for him to die young.
This answer assumes God's foreknowledge of future disobedience.
3
Analyze the second objection
The second brother (disobedient adult) asks why he was not made to die as a child to avoid hell. Al-Jubba'i has no answer because consistency would require him to say God should have made him die young, but God did not.
This exposes the contradiction in the Mu'tazila position.
4
Understand the outcome
Al-Jubba'i was confounded (buhita). Al-Ash'ari left the Mu'tazila and devoted himself to refuting their doctrines and upholding the Sunni position.
This event marks the origin of the Ash'ari school.

و(قولهم) مبتدأ وخبره (زور) ، والضمير عائد على المعتزلة، وإن لم يتقدم لهم ذكر الشهرة هذا المذهب عنهم.
وجملة قوله : (إِنَّ الصَّلَاحَ وَاجِبٌ) مقول (قولهم).
واعلم أن للمعتزلة عبارتين :
الأولى : وجوب الصلاح، والمراد به ما قابل الفساد كالإيمان في مقابلة الكفر، فيقولون : إذا كان هناك أمران: أحدهما صلاح والآخر فساد وجب على الله أن يفعل الصلاح منهما دون الفساد.
والثانية: وجوبُ الأصلح، والمراد به ما قابل الصلاح، ككونه في أعلى الجنان في مقابلة كونه في أسفلها، فيقولون : إذا كان هناك أمران أحدهما صلاح والآخر أصلحُ منه ، وجب على الله أن يفعل الأصلح منهما، دون الصلاح، والمصنف تكلَّم في إبطال مذهبهم على الأول دون الثانية ؛ لأنَّ الصلاح أعم من الأصلح، وإذا بطل الأعمُّ بطل الأخصُّ، وفي كلام المصنفِ إجمال في نسبة القول بذلك إليهم، لعدم تعلّق غرضه بمذهبهم، وإنَّما غرضه الردُّ عليهم .
والحاصل : أنَّهم قالوا بوجوب الصلاح والأصلح عليه تعالى، ثم اختلفوا : فذهب معتزلة بغداد إلى أنَّه يجب على الله تعالى مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح لعباده في الدين والدنيا، وذهبت معتزلة البصرة إلى أنه يجب عليه تعالى مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح لهم في الدين فقط، ثم اختلفوا أيضاً في المراد بالأصلح، فعند البغدادية : الأوفق في الحكمة والتدبير، وعند البصرية : الأنفع.
وهذه المسألة كانت سبباً لافتراق الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري من شيخه أبي هاشم الجبائي، فإن أبا الحسن سأل الجبائي في درسه، وقال: ما تقول في ثلاثة إخوة؛ أي: مثلاً، مات أحدهم كبيراً مطيعاً، والآخر كبيراً عاصياً، والثالث صغيراً، فقال الجبائي : الأول يُثاب بالجنة، والثاني يُعاقب بالنار، والثالث لا يُثاب ولا يُعاقب. فقال له الأشعري فإن قال الثالث : يا رَبِّ ؛ لم أمتني صغيراً، وما أبقيتني فأطيعك فأدخل الجنة؟ ماذا يقول الرَّبُّ؟
فقال الجُبائي : يقول الرَّبُّ : إني أعلم أنَّك لو كبرت عصيت فتدخل النار، فكان الأصلح لك أن تموت صغيراً .
فقال الأشعري : فإن قال الثاني : يا رَبِّ؛ لِمَ لَمْ تمتني صغيراً فلا أدخل النار؟ ماذا يقول الرَّبُّ؟ فَبُهِت الجبائي فترك الأشعري مذهبه واشتغل هو وأتباعه بإبطال ما ذهبت إليه المعتزلة، وإثباتِ ما وردت به السنة، ومضى عليه الجماعة، فلذلك سُمُّوا بأهل السنة والجماعة .
[The heading: The non-obligation of al-salah and al-Aslah upon Allah, exalted is He.]
51 - And their saying: 'Indeed, al-salah is obligatory upon Him' is false; nothing is obligatory upon Him. His saying (And their saying... etc.): This is known from what preceded, that it is permissible with respect to Him, exalted is He, to do every possible thing and to leave it. However, since the danger of ignorance in this art is great, he was not content with it except by explicit statement.
And (their saying) is a subject, and its predicate is (false), and the pronoun refers to the Mu'tazila, even if there was no prior mention of them due to the fame of this doctrine from them.
And the clause of His saying: (Indeed, al-salah is obligatory) is the object of (their saying).
Know that the Mu'tazila have two expressions: The first: The obligation of al-salah, and by it is meant what is opposite of corruption, like faith in contrast to disbelief. They say: If there are two matters, one of which is al-salah and the other corruption, then it is obligatory upon God to do al-salah from them, not corruption. The second: The obligation of al-Aslah, and by it is meant what is opposite of al-salah, like being in the highest of gardens in contrast to being in its lowest. They say: If there are two matters, one of which is al-salah and the other is more right (aslah) than it, then it is obligatory upon God to do the al-Aslah from them, not al-salah. And the author (al-Laqani) spoke about invalidating their doctrine with respect to the first without the second, because al-salah is more general than al-Aslah, and if the more general is invalid, the more specific is invalid. And in the author's speech there is succinctness in attributing that statement to them, because his purpose was not attached to their doctrine; rather his purpose was to refute them.
And the summary: That they said there is an obligation of al-salah and al-Aslah upon Him, exalted is He. Then they differed: The Mu'tazila of Baghdad went to that it is obligatory upon God, exalted is He, to observe al-salah and al-Aslah for His servants in religion and this world. And the Mu'tazila of Basra went to that it is obligatory upon Him, exalted is He, to observe al-salah and al-Aslah for them in religion only. Then they also differed regarding what is meant by al-Aslah: according to the Baghdadis, it is the most conducive in wisdom and management; according to the Basrans, it is the most beneficial.
And this issue was the cause for the separation of Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari from his teacher Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i. For Abu al-Hasan asked al-Jubba'i in his lesson, saying: What do you say about three brothers—for example—one of them died as an adult obedient, the other as an adult disobedient, and the third as a child? Al-Jubba'i said: The first is rewarded with Paradise, the second is punished with Hellfire, and the third is neither rewarded nor punished. Al-Ash'ari said to him: If the third says, 'O my Lord, why did You cause me to die as a child and not keep me alive so that I could obey You and enter Paradise?'—what would the Lord say? Al-Jubba'i said: The Lord would say, 'I know that if you had grown up you would have disobeyed and entered Hellfire, so it was more right (aslah) for you to die as a child.' Al-Ash'ari said: If the second says, 'O my Lord, why did You not cause me to die as a child so that I would not enter Hellfire?'—what would the Lord say? Al-Jubba'i was confounded. So al-Ash'ari abandoned his doctrine, and he and his followers occupied themselves with invalidating what the Mu'tazila had gone to and affirming what is reported by the Sunnah and what the community (al-jama'a) followed. For that reason they are called Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the verse (line 51) of the poem 'Jawharat al-Tawhid'. It begins by explaining that the statement 'Indeed, al-salah is obligatory upon Him' is false (zoor). The commentator notes that this is known from the general principle that God may do or not do any possible thing, but due to the danger of ignorance, explicit mention is needed. The pronoun in 'their saying' refers to the Mu'tazila. The Mu'tazila have two positions: the obligation of al-salah (what is right as opposed to corrupt) and the obligation of al-Aslah (the most right, i.e., the better of two good options). For al-salah, they say if there are two options, one right and one corrupt, God must choose the right. For al-Aslah, they say if there are two right options, God must choose the better one. The commentator notes that the author (Ibn 'Ashir) refutes only the first because the general refutation suffices for both (since if al-salah is not obligatory, al-Aslah is not either). The summary then states that the Mu'tazila are unanimous on the obligation of al-salah and al-Aslah, but they differ: the Baghdadis extend it to both religion and worldly affairs, the Basrans restrict it to religion. They also differ on the definition of al-Aslah: the Baghdadis say it is what is most conducive to wisdom, the Basrans say it is what is most beneficial. The passage then narrates the famous debate that led to al-Ash'ari's conversion: al-Ash'ari asked al-Jubba'i about three brothers—one obedient adult, one disobedient adult, and one child. Al-Jubba'i's responses led to a contradiction: if the child would have been obedient if allowed to live, why was he killed? And if the disobedient adult would have been better off dead, why was he kept alive? Al-Jubba'i was confounded, and al-Ash'ari abandoned Mu'tazilism, dedicating himself to refuting their doctrines and affirming the Sunni position, which follows the revealed texts and the consensus of the early community (al-Salaf). Thus they are called Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah.

Key Terms
أبو الحسن الأشعري
أبو هاشم الجبائي
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate was merely a clever logical puzzle without real theological impact.
Correction: The passage states it was the direct cause of al-Ash'ari's conversion and the formation of the Ash'ari school.

Checkpoint
Why was al-Jubba'i unable to answer al-Ash'ari's second question?
A) He did not know what the third brother would have done.
B) The same logic that justified the child's death would require the disobedient adult to have been killed as a child.
C) He forgot the details of the Mu'tazila doctrine.
D) The question contained a grammatical error.

Key Takeaway
The debate over three brothers demonstrates that the Mu'tazila principle of al-Aslah leads to self-contradiction when applied consistently, prompting al-Ash'ari to reject it and embrace the Sunni doctrine that God's actions are not subject to external obligations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ash'ari left his teacher and a comfortable position after seeing the truth. Is there a belief or practice you hold onto simply because it is familiar or from a respected figure, even when you sense inconsistency? Will you have the courage to follow the truth wherever it leads, even if it means standing alone?
?
When the disobedient brother asked why God did not save him from Hell, al-Jubba'i was silenced. What if you stood before God with a similar complaint about your own sins—that He did not prevent you from sinning? Does this lesson challenge you to take full responsibility for your choices rather than blaming God's decree?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they could define what God 'must' do. Have you ever, even subtly, felt that God owes you something—good health, success, or an easy life? Let this debate remind you that God is not bound by any external obligation; He does as He wills. Will you submit to His absolute sovereignty today, even when His plan is beyond your understanding?

The Meaning of Zoor and Refutation of the Mu'tazila
Explains the term 'zoor' and refutes the Mu'tazila via reductio

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'zoor' refers to the Mu'tazila doctrine and why it is considered false due to its implications.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila doctrine is false because it would require God not to create disbelievers destined for eternal punishment.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the word 'zoor' (false) used in the poem to describe the Mu'tazila doctrine of the obligation of al-salah and al-Aslah. By examining its outward appearance and inner corruption, we see a powerful reductio ad absurdum that demonstrates its impossibility. Let's read the passage and its explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the source text
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument.
Pay attention to the compound expression 'zoor'.
2
Understand the definition of 'zoor'
Notice that 'zoor' is defined as outwardly adorned but inwardly corrupt. The commentator explains why it is considered false.
3
Analyze the reductio ad absurdum
Follow the logical argument: if God were obliged to do what is most beneficial, He would not create disbelievers destined for eternal punishment. Since they exist, the doctrine is false.
This is a key argument against the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (زُورُ) أي: مزين الظاهر فاسد الباطن، فهو باطل، ويصح تفسيره من أول الأمر بالباطل، وإنما كان مزين الظاهر للتعبير عنه بالصلاح والأصلح، وإلا فهو مِنْ أسمج المذاهب، وإنَّما كان فاسد الباطن؛ لأنه لو وجب عليه تعالى الصلاح والأصلح لعباده لما خلق الكافر الفقير المعذَّب في الدنيا بالفقر، وفي الآخرة بالعقاب الأليم المخلد ؛ لأنَّ الأصلح له عدم خلقه، وإن خُلق فالأصلح له إماتته صغيراً أو سَلْبُ عقلِهِ قبل التكليف.
His saying: (false) i.e., its outer appearance is adorned but its inner is corrupt, so it is false, and it is valid to interpret it from the beginning as false. Rather, its outer appearance is adorned because it is expressed in terms of al-salah and al-Aslah; otherwise it is among the ugliest of doctrines. And it is only corrupt inwardly because if al-salah and al-Aslah were obligatory upon Him, exalted is He, for His servants, He would not have created the disbelieving, poor person who is tormented in this world by poverty and in the Hereafter by painful, eternal punishment—because the most beneficial (al-Aslah) for him would be not to create him; and if he is created, then the most beneficial for him would be to cause him to die as a child or to deprive him of his intellect before the onset of moral obligation (taklif).
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the word 'zoor' (false) in the poem line. The commentator explains that 'zoor' means something outwardly embellished but inwardly corrupt, i.e., false. It appears outwardly attractive because it uses terms like al-salah and al-Aslah, but in reality it is one of the ugliest doctrines. The inner corruption is demonstrated by a reductio ad absurdum: if it were obligatory for God to do what is most beneficial (al-Aslah) for His servants, then He would never have created a disbeliever who suffers poverty in this world and eternal punishment in the next. The most beneficial for such a person would be non-existence, or if created, to die before moral obligation (i.e., as a child) or to be deprived of intellect so that he is not accountable. Since such people exist, the Mu'tazila doctrine is false.

Key Terms
زور
الصلاح
الأصلح
التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila might argue that God's obligation does not preclude temporary suffering for ultimate benefit.
Correction: The refutation focuses on eternal punishment, which has no compensating benefit.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the Mu'tazila doctrine of the obligation of al-salah and al-Aslah considered 'corrupt inwardly'?
A) Because it contradicts the existence of prophets.
B) Because if it were true, God would not have created disbelievers destined for eternal punishment.
C) Because it denies God's power.
D) Because it is based on reason rather than revelation.

Key Takeaway
The term 'zoor' in the poem refers to the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-salah and al-Aslah, which is false because its logical consequence contradicts observed reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if God owes you something—comfort, success, or salvation? How would your worship change if you truly internalized that God is not obligated to give you any of it?
?
The Mu'tazila thought God must do what is best for every individual. When you face hardship, do you feel entitled to ease? Examine your heart: do you complain against God's decree as if He has wronged you?
?
This lesson affirms that God creates disbelievers who will suffer eternally. If you truly believed that some people will never be saved, would it change how you interact with those who reject Islam? Do you feel urgency for their fate, or have you become numb to it?

Poetic Defense of Bila Kayf against Zamakhshari
Presents and refutes Zamakhshari's poetic mockery of the Sunni use of 'bila kayf'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sunni scholars poetically defended the doctrine of seeing Allah without modality (bila kayf) against Mu'tazili mockery.

Core Idea
Poetic refutation defends the Sunni creed of seeing Allah without modality.

Zamakhshari, a Mu'tazili scholar, composed a poem mocking the Sunni use of 'bila kayf' as a way to avoid anthropomorphism. In response, Sunni poets like al-Sayyid al-Balidi and others countered with verses affirming the Quranic basis of ru'ya (vision) and condemning the mockery. This lesson presents these poetic exchanges and explains the doctrinal points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the poetic exchange.
Notice the poetic meter and rhyme.
2
Identify the two sides
Identify Zamakhshari's mocking poem and the two refutations. What is the core accusation and the response?
The accusation is that Sunnis twist 'bila kayf' into 'balqafa'.
3
Understand the term 'bila kayf'
Recognize that 'bila kayf' means without modality, emphasizing transcendence and avoiding anthropomorphism.
4
See the Quranic basis
Notice that the refutations cite Quranic verses about seeing Allah (e.g., al-A'raf 143) to support the Sunni position.
The second response explicitly references 'the verse of al-A'raf'.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Understand that such poetic debates were a form of theological defense in Islamic history.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وانتحتوا من قول أهل السنة ربلا كيف البلكفة . وقد أنشد الزمخشري في الكشاف» يهجو أهل السنة فقال: [من الكامل] لَجَمَاعَةٌ سَمَّوا هَواهُمْ سُنَّةٌ وَجَمَاعَةٌ حُمْرٌ لَعَمْرِي مُوكَفَهُ قَدْ شَبَّهُوْهُ بِخَلْقِهِ فَتَخَوَّفُوا شَنْعَ الوَرَى فَتَسَكَّرُوا بِالبَلْكَفَة ورد عليه السيد البليدي بقوله : [من الكامل] هَلْ نَحْنُ مِنْ أَهْلِ الهَوَى أَوْ أَنْتُمُ؟ وَمَنِ الَّذِي مِنَّا حَمِيرٌ مُوْكَفَهُ؟ اِعْكِسُ تُصِبْ فَالوَصْفُ فِيْكُمْ ظَاهِرٌ كَالشَّمْسِ فَارْجِعْ عَنْ مَقَالِ الزَّخْرَفَهُ يَكْفِيكَ فِي رَدِّي عَلَيْكَ بِأَنَّنَا نَحْتَجُ بِالآياتِ لَا بِالسَّفْسَفَهُ وَبِنَفْي رُؤْيَتِهِ فَأَنْتَ حُرِمْتَها إِنْ لَمْ تَقُلْ بِكَلامِ أَهْلِ المَعْرِفَهُ فَنَراهُ فِي الْأُخْرَى بِلَا كَيْفِيَّةٍ وكَذَاكَ مِنْ غَيْرِ ارْتِسَامِ لِلصَّفَهُ وقال بعضهم في الرد عليه : [من الكامل] شَبَّهْتَ جَهْلاً صَدْرَ أُمَّةِ أَحْمَدٍ وَذَوِي البَصَائِرِ بِالحَمِيرِ المُوكَفَهُ وَجَبَ الخَسَارُ عَلَيْكَ فَانْظُرْ مُنْصِفاً في آية الأَعْرَافِ نَهْيَ المُنْصِفَهُ أَتَرى الكَلِيْمَ أَنَّى بِجَهْلِ مَا أَتَى وَأنَى شُيُوخُكَ مَا أَتَوْا عَنْ مَعْرِفَهُ؟ إِنَّ الوُجُوهَ إِليهِ نَاظِرَةٌ بِذَا جَاءَ الكِتَابُ فَقُلْتُمُ: هَذَا سَفَه نَطَقَ الكِتابُ وَأَنْتَ تَنْطِقُ بِالهَوَى فهَوَى الهَوَى بِكَ في المَهَاوِي المُتْلِفَة وقد شنعوا في الردّ عليه بغير ذلك .
And they distorted the saying of Ahl al-Sunna 'bila kayf' into 'balqafa'. And al-Zamakhshari recited in al-Kashshaf, lampooning Ahl al-Sunna, saying: 'A group who called their desires a sunna, and a group of red ones, by my life, mawkafa. They likened Him to His creation, then feared the ugly talk of people, so they intoxicated themselves with 'balqafa'.' And al-Sayyid al-Balidi replied to him, saying: 'Are we the people of desire, or are you? And who among us is a being mawkafa? Reverse and you will hit [the truth], for the description is apparent in you like the sun. So retract from the speech of ornamentation. Sufficient in my refutation of you is that we argue by the verses, not by frivolity. And by [your] denial of seeing Him, you are deprived of it if you do not say with the words of the people of knowledge. So we see Him in the Hereafter without modality, and likewise without imprinted form.' And others said in refutation of him: 'You ignorantly likened the vanguard of Ahmad's community and the people of insight to mawkafa donkeys. Loss is incumbent on you; so look justly at the verse of al-A'raf, the prohibition of the just. Do you see that the speaker (Moses) came with ignorance? And your elders came with knowledge? Indeed, the faces are looking towards Him, with this the Book came, and you said: This is foolishness. The Book spoke and you speak with desire, so the desire carried you to the destructive abysses.' And they launched severe refutations against him beyond this.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents a poetic refutation of Zamakhshari's attack on the Sunni doctrine of seeing Allah without modality (bila kayf). Zamakhshari, a Mu'tazili, composed a poem mocking the Sunnis for using the term 'bila kayf' and likening Allah to creation. The commentator then presents two responses: first by al-Sayyid al-Balidi, who turns the accusation back on the Mu'tazila, stating that they are the ones who follow desire and that the Sunnis argue from the Quran, not from baseless talk. The second response by another poet defends the Sunni position, citing the Quranic verse that faces will look at Allah, and condemns Zamakhshari's mockery. The passage concludes that the refutations were numerous. This lesson shows the rhetorical defense of Sunni creed.

Key Terms
بلكفة
الزمخشري
الكشاف
هجو
الرؤية
بلا كيفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'bila kayf' is an invented term without Quranic basis.
Correction: The refutations show that the Sunni stance is rooted in Quranic verses about seeing Allah, such as in Surah al-A'raf and al-Qiyamah.

Checkpoint
What did Zamakhshari mock the Sunnis for in his poem?
A) Their belief that Allah is seen
B) Their use of the term 'bila kayf'
C) Their denial of ru'ya
D) Their reliance on the Quran

Key Takeaway
Sunni poets defended the doctrine of seeing Allah without modality by invoking Quranic verses and exposing Mu'tazili mockery as rooted in desire.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone mocks a belief you hold sacred, does your reaction rely on emotional defense or do you turn to the Quran for proof like al-Balidi? Which poet's approach do you see in your own heart right now?
?
The poets accused Zamakhshari of following his desire (hawa). Can you identify a theological point where you might be resisting clear scripture because it doesn't align with your own reasoning or comfort? Would you be willing to submit to the Quran even if it feels difficult?
?
The second poet speaks of faces looking at Allah. Do you truly long for that vision in your daily life, or is your hope for Paradise more about its pleasures than seeing Him? What change would it take to make that vision your deepest aspiration?

Dispute Over Prophetic Superiority
Verse and commentary on the disagreement over the superiority of prophets and angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement regarding the superiority of prophets over angels and how the author directs readers to a key treatise on the matter.

Core Idea
The debate over prophetic superiority remains unresolved; a treatise refines the discussion.

This lesson examines a poetic verse from the Jawharat al-Tawhid and its commentary, which points to a longstanding dispute among scholars: Are prophets superior to angels? The verse acknowledges that some scholars have detailed the matter, while the commentary directs us to an important treatise for deeper study.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Start by reading the Arabic verse and its literal English translation to grasp the basic content.
The verse is concise; note the key terms: 'detailed' and 'some surpass each other.'
2
Identify the reference of 'this'
Understand that 'this' refers to the previous discussion on the superiority of prophets over angels. The commentary clarifies this.
3
Explore the cited treatise
Note that the author wrote a treatise 'al-Inkifaf 'an Iqra' al-Kashshaf', cited by al-Suyuti. Consider its significance in refining the debate.
This shows the author's engagement with the topic beyond the verse.

Source Text

٦٧ - هَذَا وقَومٌ فَصَّلُوا إِذْ فَضَلُوا - وَبَعْضُ كُلِّ بَعْضَهُ قَدْ يَفْضُلُ قوله : (هَذَا) مفعول لمحذوف؛ أي افهم هذا، ويصح غير ذلك كما تقدم في نظيره واسم الإشارة عائد على المذكور من تفضيل الأنبياء على الملائكة، وقاله رحمه الله تعالى في رسالة الانكفاف عن إقراء الكشاف ، وقد أوردها السيوطي وهي لطيفة في تحفة الأديب في نحاة مغني اللبيب»، فلينظر .
67 - This, and a group detailed when they made a distinction, and some of each may surpass each other. He said: (This) is an object of a deleted verb, i.e., understand this, and other things are possible as previously mentioned in its equivalent. The demonstrative pronoun refers to the mentioned discussion of preferring prophets over angels. And he, may Allah have mercy on him, said in his treatise 'al-Inkifaf 'an Iqra' al-Kashshaf', and al-Suyuti cited it, and it is refined in 'Tuhfat al-Adib fi Nahat Mughni al-Labib', so look.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents the poetic verse (number 67) and its commentary. The verse states: 'This, and a group detailed when they made a distinction, and some of each may surpass each other.' The commentary explains that the demonstrative pronoun 'this' refers to the preceding discussion about the superiority of prophets over angels. It then notes that the author (al-Laqqani) wrote a treatise called 'al-Inkifaf 'an Iqra' al-Kashshaf' (Refraining from Teaching al-Kashshaf), which was cited by al-Suyuti and is considered a refined work in the field of Arabic grammar. The commentator advises the reader to consult that treatise for further elaboration.

The Two Views on Preference Between Angels and Humans
The commentator explains the Ash'ari and Maturidi views on tafdil of angels over humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari and Maturidi views on the preference (tafdil) between angels and humans, and the Maturidi detailed ranking.

Core Idea
Angels vs. humans: majority say all angels are superior, but Maturidis distinguish leaders and commoners.

The poet al-Laqqani presents the view that angels are superior to all humans without distinction, but the commentator clarifies that this is the method of the majority. Then he explains the Maturidi distinction between leaders and commoners, which is considered the preponderant view.

Guided Path
1
Read the majority view
Notice that the poet presented the view that angels are better than all humans without specification, which is the Ash'ari position.
This is mentioned as the weaker view.
2
Understand the Maturidi distinction
The commentator explains the line 'Wa qawmun fassalu idh faddalu', referring to the Maturidis who made a detailed ranking.
They distinguished between leaders of angels, common angels, prophets, common humans, and immoral humans.
3
Identify the rankings
Follow the hierarchy: prophets > angel leaders > common humans (saints) > common angels > immoral humans.
4
Learn about the bearers of the Throne and Cherubim
The commentator provides details about common angels: the bearers of the Throne (now four, later eight) and the Cherubim who circle the Throne.
This illustrates the Maturidi view.
5
Conclusion
The commentator states that this Maturidi method is the preponderant (rajiha) view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النبوات . وتفضيل الملائكة على بقية البشر من غير تفصيل كما هو طريقة جمهور المرجوحة، وإنما قدَّمها الناظم؛ لأنه وضع منظومته على مذهبهم. وقوله : (وقَومٌ فَصَّلُوا إِذْ فَضَلُوا) أي وقوم من الماتريدية فصلوا بين رؤساء الملائكة وعوامهم وعوام البشر حين فضلوا بين الفريقين، فقالوا : الأنبياء أفضلُ من رؤساء الملائكة ؛ كجبريل وميكائيل، ورؤساء الملائكة أفضل من عوام البشر، وهم أولياؤهم غير الأنبياء؛ كأبي بكر وعمر رضي الله تعالى عنهما، وليس المراد بعوام البشر ما يشمل الفساق فإن الملائكة أفضلُ منهم على الصحيح، وعوام البشر المذكورون أفضل من عوام الملائكة ؛ وهم غير رؤسائهم كحملة العرش؛ وهم أربعة الآن، فإذا كان يوم القيامة أيَّدهم الله بأربعة أخرى قال تعالى: ﴿وَيَحملُ عَرْشَ رَبِّكَ فَوْقَهُمْ يوميذ تمنيةٌ [الحاقة : ۱۷] ; لمزيد الجلال عليه يوم القيامة وكالكروبيين بفتح الكاف وتخفيف الراء؛ وهم ملائكة حافون بالعرش، طائفون به، لقبوا بذلك؛ لأنه متصدون للدعاء برفع الكرب عن الأمة، وقيل غير ذلك، وقد علمت أن هذه الطريقة هي الراجحة .
The prophecies. And preferring angels over the rest of mankind without specification is the method of the majority which is the weaker (or preponderant?), and the poet presented it because he composed his poem according to their school. And his saying: (And a group distinguished when they preferred) meaning a group of the Maturidis distinguished between the leaders of angels and their commoners and commoners of mankind when they preferred between the two groups, and they said: The prophets are better than the leaders of angels like Gabriel and Michael, and the leaders of angels are better than the commoners of mankind, who are their saints other than the prophets like Abu Bakr and Umar, may Allah be pleased with them, and the intended by commoners of mankind does not include the immoral because angels are better than them according to the correct view, and the mentioned commoners of mankind are better than the commoners of angels, who are other than their leaders like the bearers of the Throne; they are four now, and on the Day of Resurrection Allah will support them with four others, He said: 'And the Throne of your Lord will be borne above them on that day by eight' (Al-Haqqah 69:17) for increased majesty on the Day of Resurrection, and like the Karubiyyun (Cherubim) with a fatha on the kaf and lightening of the ra; they are angels surrounding the Throne, circumambulating it, so named because they are concerned with supplication for removing distress from the community, and other opinions have been given. And you know that this method is the preponderant.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage explains the two views on the preference (tafdil) between angels and humans. The commentator first notes that the poet (al-Laqqani) presented the view that angels are superior to all humans without differentiation, which is the method of the majority. Then he explains the line 'Wa qawmun fassalu idh faddalu'—a group (the Maturidis) made a distinction. They distinguished between the leaders of angels (like Gabriel and Michael) and common angels, and between common humans and common angels. They held that prophets are better than angel leaders, angel leaders are better than common humans who are saints (like Abu Bakr and Umar), and those common humans are better than common angels. Common angels include the bearers of the Throne (four now, eight on Judgment Day) and the Cherubim. The commentator concludes that this Maturidi view is the preponderant (al-rajiha).

كروبيون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all humans are better than all angels.
Correction: The Maturidi view distinguishes between leaders and commoners: prophets are better than angel leaders, but angel leaders are better than common humans, common humans better than common angels, and angels are better than immoral humans.

Checkpoint

B) Common humans are better than angel leaders.

D) Prophets are better than both.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidis hold a detailed ranking: prophets > angel leaders > saintly humans > common angels > immoral humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Maturidi view elevates common saintly humans (like Abu Bakr and Umar) above the common angels who constantly worship and supplicate for the community. Do you truly believe that your deeds can surpass the worship of those angels? How does that truth challenge the way you spend your time and effort in worship? What will you change starting tonight?
?
This lesson teaches that the leaders of angels—Gabriel and Michael—are inferior only to prophets. How deeply do you honor the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ in your daily life? Is your love for him reflected in your priorities, your speech, and your actions? If you were to be held to this standard right now, what would you need to confess and change?
