Refutation of the Rawandiyya and Shi'a Sects
Refutation of two extreme sects on precedence of Companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positions of the Rawandiyya and Shi'a sects regarding the precedence of Companions, and how the commentator distinguishes them from other groups.

Core Idea
Extremism in love leads to doctrinal error.

This passage addresses two sects that held extreme views about the rightful Imam after the Prophet. The Rawandiyya claimed precedence for al-Abbas, while the Shi'a elevated Ali above all Companions. The commentator refutes both and clarifies the more moderate positions of other groups, warning against confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the names of the sects and their claims.
Notice the note about the name change from 'Abbasiyya to Rawandiyya.
2
Identify the two sects and their claims
The Rawandiyya claim precedence for al-Abbas. The Shi'a claim precedence for Ali over all Companions. Understand what makes them extreme.
3
Distinguish the extreme Shi'a from moderate groups
The commentator lists groups that only prefer Ali over Uthman, not over all. Note the difference and the warning about possible confusion.
4
Reflect on the commentator's purpose
The commentator refutes both sects and clarifies the correct Sunni position on the ranking of Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رد على الراوندية، وكانوا في الأصل يُقال لهم: العباسية، يقولون بتقديم العباس ابن عبد المطلب، وإنما غير اسمهم؛ لئلا يتوهم أنهم أولاد العباس. وفيه ردُّ أيضاً على الشّيعة - بفتح الياء - وهم فرقة تتغالي في حب سيدنا علي رضي الله عنه، فتقدمُهُ على سائر الصحابة، وأما أهل الكوفة وبعض أهل السنة وجمهور المعتزلة وسيدنا مالك في قوله الأول .. فيقدمون عليا على عثمان فقط، ففرق بين قول الشيعة وقول هؤلاء، وإن أوهم كلام الشارح خلاف ذلك.
A refutation of the Rawandiyya, who were originally called the 'Abbasiyya, claiming the precedence of al-'Abbas ibn 'Abd al-Muttalib, and they only changed their name to avoid being thought that they were the children of al-'Abbas. And in it there is also a refutation of the Shi'a - with the yā' made open - and they are a group that exaggerates in love of our master 'Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, so they give him precedence over the rest of the Companions. As for the people of Kufa, some of the Ahl al-Sunna, the majority of the Mu'tazila, and our master Malik in his first opinion, they give 'Ali precedence only over 'Uthman. So there is a difference between the statement of the Shi'a and the statement of these, even if the commentator's statement might suggest otherwise.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two sects: the Rawandiyya (also known as al-'Abbasiyya) and the Shi'a. The commentator first explains that the Rawandiyya originally were called al-'Abbasiyya because they held that al-'Abbas, the uncle of the Prophet, was the rightful Imam after the Prophet, and that the caliphate should have been in his line. They changed their name to avoid being mistaken as descendants of al-'Abbas. The commentator then refutes the Shi'a, a group that exaggerates in love for 'Ali, to the point of giving him precedence over all other Companions. He distinguishes them from other groups such as the people of Kufa, some Ahl al-Sunna, the majority of the Mu'tazila, and even Imam Malik in his first opinion, who only prefer 'Ali over 'Uthman, not over all other Companions. The commentator emphasizes that there is a clear difference between the extreme Shi'a position and these moderate positions, and he warns that the commentator's own wording might inadvertently suggest otherwise, but the distinction is real.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Shi'a are simply those who prefer 'Ali over other Companions, but that is too broad.
Correction: The true Shi'a in this context give 'Ali precedence over all Companions, whereas moderate groups only prefer him over 'Uthman.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Ten Companions and the Angels of Badr
Explains the superiority of the ten companions over non-leader angels of Badr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced ranking between the ten companions and the angels of Badr, including the exception for angelic leaders.

Core Idea
The ten companions are superior to non-leader angels, but angel leaders surpass common humans.

This lesson unpacks a key theological discussion about the relative status of the ten companions promised Paradise and the angels who fought at Badr. The author clarifies that the companions' superiority is over non-leader angels, while the leaders of angels (like Jibril) hold a higher rank than ordinary humans. This passage also sets a principle that applies to believing jinn.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on ten companions
Read the Arabic and translation of the first paragraph carefully. Note the author's qualification about 'other than their leaders'.
The phrase 'what is meant by them is their saints' refers to angelic leaders.
2
Identify the rule and its exception
The general rule is that the ten companions are superior to the angels of Badr. The exception is that angelic leaders are superior to common humans.
This prevents the misconception that all humans are superior to all angels.
3
Understand the ranking within angels
The text also states that angels who witnessed Badr are superior to those who did not. This creates a layered hierarchy.
Analogy to jinn extends the same reasoning.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to confirm you grasped the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنَّ ما اقتضاه كلام الناظم من أن الأربعة الخلفاء والستة الذين هم تمام العشرة أفضلُ من الملائكة الذين هم حضروا بدراً .. محمول على غير رؤسائهم؛ لما تقدَّم من أن رؤساءهم أفضل من عوام البشر، وقد علمت أن المراد بهم أولياؤهم كأبي بكر وعمر ثم الملائكة الذين شهدوا بدراً أفضل ممن لم يشهدها منهم، وقياسه أن يقال: كذلك في مؤمني الجن.
·
قوله : (العَظِيمِ الشَّانِ): صفةٌ لبدر من حيث غزوتها، واحترز بذلك عن غزوتيها الأخيرتين، فإن غزواتها ثلاثة :
الأولى : لم يقع فيها قتال، بل كانت لطلب إنسان أغار على مواشي المدينة،
وخرجوا في طلبه فلم يجدوه
(1)
والثالثة : قد تواعد لها أبو سفيان مع النبي ، وتخلف أبو سفيان خوفاً .
والوسطى هي العظمى ؛ لحضور الملائكة والجن فيها مع الإنس.
Then what the statement of the poet requires—that the four caliphs and the six who complete the ten are superior to the angels who attended Badr—is applied to other than their leaders; because it was previously established that their leaders are superior to the common people of mankind. And you have known that what is meant by them is their saints like Abu Bakr and Umar. Then the angels who witnessed Badr are superior to those who did not witness it from among them. And by analogy it is said: likewise among the believing jinn. 
·
His statement: 'the great in status' is an attribute of Badr in terms of its battle, and he guarded by that against its two latter battles, for its battles are three: 
The first: no fighting occurred in it, rather it was for seeking a man who raided the livestock of Medina, and they went out in pursuit of him and did not find him. (1)
The third: Abu Sufyan had agreed with the Prophet to meet for it, but Abu Sufyan stayed away out of fear.
And the middle one is the great one, due to the presence of the angels and the jinn in it along with the humans.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage addresses two topics. First, the ranking of the ten companions (the four caliphs plus the six other promised paradise) over the angels who attended Badr. The author clarifies that this superiority applies only to non-leader angels; the leaders of the angels (like Jibril) are superior to common humans. He also notes that the angels who witnessed Badr are superior to angels who did not. The same principle applies to believing jinn. Second, the commentator explains the phrase 'the great in status' in the poem, which describes Badr's battle. He distinguishes three distinct expeditions called Badr: the first was a pursuit without fighting; the third was a planned encounter that Abu Sufyan avoided; the middle one is the great Badr where the angels and jinn fought alongside humans.

الملائكة

Misconception: All angels are inferior to all humans.
Correction: Only non-leader angels are inferior to the ten companions; angelic leaders are superior to common humans.

Checkpoint

A) The angels of Badr
B) The leaders of the angels
C) The common humans

The Three Battles of Badr
Classifies the three expeditions of Badr and explains why the middle is the great battle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three expeditions called Badr and know why the second one is called the great battle.

Core Idea
Three expeditions are named Badr, but only the middle one was a major battle with angels and jinn.

The term 'Badr' in Islamic history is often associated with the famous battle, but there were actually three military expeditions that went by that name. This passage clarifies each one: the first was a pursuit without fighting, the third was a planned encounter that never happened, and the middle—the great Badr—was the one where angels and jinn fought alongside the Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three battles
Read the Arabic and translation of the second paragraph. Notice that the poem's description 'the great in status' specifically refers to the middle battle.
The phrase 'العَظِيمِ الشَّانِ' distinguishes it from the other two.
2
Identify each battle
List the three battles: first (no fighting, pursuit of a raider), third (aborted due to Abu Sufyan's fear), middle (the famous Badr).
The first and third are minor expeditions.
3
Understand why the middle is great
The middle battle is called great because angels and jinn participated alongside humans. This sets it apart from the other two.
This is a key theological point: angelic participation elevates its rank.
4
Test your knowledge with the checkpoint
Answer the question about why the middle battle is considered great.

He also notes that the angels who witnessed Badr are superior to angels who did not. The same principle applies to believing jinn. Second, the commentator explains the phrase 'the great in status' in the poem, which describes Badr's battle. He distinguishes three distinct expeditions called Badr: the first was a pursuit without fighting; the third was a planned encounter that Abu Sufyan avoided; the middle one is the great Badr where the angels and jinn fought alongside humans.

D) Because Abu Sufyan participated

Key Takeaway
Three expeditions are named Badr, but only the middle one involved divine support and is therefore called the great battle.

Who Are the Foremost Ones? Defining al-Sabiqun al-Awwalun
Defines al-Sabiqun al-Awwalun and explains scholarly disagreement on their identity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement over the identity of the early Muslim pioneers (al-Sabiqun al-Awwalun) and the basis for their virtue.

Core Idea
The Foremost Ones are those whom Allah has honored with precedence, but scholars differ on their exact identity.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid addresses a key question: who exactly are the 'Foremost Ones' mentioned in the Qur'an? While their virtue is clearly established by divine text, their precise identification has been a matter of debate among early scholars. We will explore the three main views and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Follow the grammatical analysis
The commentator begins by parsing the poetic line to show that the virtue of the Foremost is known from the Qur'anic text. Notice how the grammar emphasizes that their excellence is divinely established.
2
Learn the three opinions
Three different identifications are given: those who prayed toward both qiblas, the participants of Badr, and those who took the Pledge of Ridwan. The commentator states the first is the most correct.
This shows that scholarly disagreement is normal in such matters.
3
Understand individual vs. category preference
The passage explains that preference can be considered by individuals (like Abu Bakr, Umar, etc.) or by categories (like the four caliphs, then the ten, then Badr, etc.). This clarifies how the Foremost can be seen both as a group and as individuals within that group.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَنْ هم السابقون الأولون؟] ٧٩ - وَالسَّابِقُونَ فَضْلُهُمْ نَصَّا عُرِف هَذَا وَفِي تَعْيِينِهِمْ قَدِ اخْتُلِفْ قوله : (والسَّابِقُونَ فَضْلُهُمْ نَصَّا عُرِف : هذه جملةٌ مستأنفة، ولهذا لم يأتِ بحرف الترتيب، (والسابقون) مبتدأ أول، و(فضلهم) مبتدأ ثان، وجملة قوله : (عرف) خبر المبتدأ الثاني، وهو وخبره خبر عن المبتدأ الأول ؛ و(نصا) منصوب على نزع الخافض، وفي عبارة بعضهم : منصوبٌ على التمييز والمعنى: والمتقدمون الأولون فضلهم بمعنى كثرة ثوابهم على غيرهم ممن لم يشركهم في هذه الصفة، عُرف من نص القرآن أو من جهة نص القرآن كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَالسَّبِقُونَ الْأَوَّلُونَ مِنَ الْمُهَاجِرِينَ وَالْأَنصَارِ [التوبة: ١٠٠] الآية. وقوله : (هَذَا ) أي : افهم هذا ، فهو مفعول لمحذوف، ويصح غير ذلك. وقوله : ( وفِي تَعْيِينِهِمْ قَدِ اخْتُلِف أي: وفي تعيين السابقين قد اختلف العلماء، فقال أبو موسى الأشعري وغيره من الأكابر الذين صلوا إلى القبلتين؛ أي: قبلة بيت المقدس والكعبة، وهذا هو قول الأكثر ، وهو الأصح ، وقال محمد بن كعب القرظي وجماعةٌ هم أهل بدر، وقال الشعبي هم أهل بيعة الرضوان، فالأقوال ثلاثة أرجحها أولها، وقد عُلم من كلام الناظم أن التفضيل تارةً يكون باعتبار الأفراد، وتارة يكون باعتبار الأصناف. فالأول كتفضيل أبي بكر ثم عمر ثم عثمان ثم عليّ، والثاني كتفضيل الخلفاء الأربعة، ثم الستة الباقية من العشرة ، ثم أهل بدر، ثم أهل أحد، ثم أهل بيعة الرضوان، وبعض أهل هذه المراتب ربما دخل في بعضها، وربما دخل في الجميع، فقد يكون سابقاً خليفةٌ بدريًا أُحديًّا رِضوانيًا كالمشايخ الأربعة، لكن عثمان بدري أجراً
Who are the Foremost Ones? 79 - 'And the Foremost, their virtue is known by text, this [is understood], and regarding their specification, there has been disagreement.' His saying: 'And the Foremost, their virtue is known by text': This is an independent sentence, and for this reason it did not come with a conjunction. 'And the Foremost' is the first subject, 'their virtue' is the second subject, and the sentence 'known' is the predicate of the second subject, and together with its predicate is the predicate of the first subject. 'By text' is in the accusative case by removing the preposition, and in the expression of some: it is in the accusative as a specification. The meaning: The early predecessors, their virtue, meaning the abundance of their reward over others who did not share this attribute, is known from the text of the Qur'an or by way of the Qur'anic text, such as His saying: 'And the Foremost, the first of the Emigrants and the Helpers...' [al-Tawba 9:100] the verse. And His saying: 'this' means: understand this, so it is an object of an omitted verb, and other [interpretations] are possible. And His saying: 'and regarding their specification, there has been disagreement': meaning: regarding the specification of the Foremost, the scholars have disagreed. Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and others among the great ones who prayed toward the two qiblas—that is, the qibla of Jerusalem and the Kaaba—said [they are those who prayed toward both qiblas]. This is the opinion of the majority, and it is the most correct. Muhammad ibn Ka'b al-Qurazi and a group said they are the people of Badr. Al-Sha'bi said they are the people of the Pledge of Ridwan. So there are three opinions, the most preponderant is the first. And it is known from the words of the poet that preference is sometimes by individuals and sometimes by categories. The first is like the preference of Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali. The second is like the preference of the four caliphs, then the remaining six of the ten, then the people of Badr, then the people of Uhud, then the people of the Pledge of Ridwan. Some of the people of these ranks may enter into some of them, and may enter into all. So a person could be a foremer, a caliph, a Badri, an Uhudi, a Ridwani, like the four shaykhs. But Uthman is a Badri in reward.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from the poem Jawharat al-Tawhid. It begins by raising the question of who the 'Foremost Ones' (al-Sabiqun al-Awwalun) are. The poetic line states that their virtue is known from the Qur'anic text (nass), but their exact identification is a matter of disagreement. The commentator then provides a grammatical analysis of the line: 'al-Sabiqun' is the first subject, 'fadluhum' is the second subject, and 'urifa' is the predicate. The word 'nassan' is accusative either by removing a preposition (i.e., 'min nass') or as a specification. The meaning is that the virtue of the early predecessors—understood as the abundance of their reward over those who did not share this description—is known from the Qur'anic text, specifically citing verse 9:100. The commentator then explains the phrase 'this' as an imperative to understand, and then addresses the disagreement regarding their specification. Three opinions are mentioned: the opinion of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and others that the Sabiqun are those who prayed toward both qiblas (i.e., the early Muslims who migrated and prayed toward Jerusalem before the qibla change)—this is stated to be the opinion of the majority and the most correct. The second opinion, from Muhammad ibn Ka'b al-Qurazi, is that they are the participants of the Battle of Badr. The third opinion, from al-Sha'bi, is that they are the participants of the Pledge of Ridwan at Hudaybiyyah. The commentator then notes that from the poet's words, it is understood that preference (tafdil) is considered in two ways: by individuals (e.g., the ranking of Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali) and by categories (e.g., the four caliphs, then the remaining six of the ten Companions promised Paradise, then the people of Badr, then Uhud, then Ridwan). The commentator further notes that some individuals may belong to multiple categories, such as the four caliphs who were present at Badr, Uhud, and Ridwan, but Uthman is considered a Badri in reward despite not being physically present.

Key Terms
السابقون الأولون
المهاجرون
الأنصار
أهل بدر
أهل بيعة الرضوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'al-Sabiqun al-Awwalun' refers exclusively to the Companions of Badr.
Correction: The passage shows there are multiple valid opinions, with the most correct being those who prayed toward both qiblas. The people of Badr are only one opinion.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which opinion on the identity of 'al-Sabiqun al-Awwalun' is considered the most correct?
A) They are the participants of Badr
B) They are the participants of the Pledge of Ridwan
C) They are those who prayed toward both qiblas
D) They are the four caliphs

Key Takeaway
The identity of the Foremost Ones is a matter of scholarly interpretation, with the most correct view being that they are those who prayed toward both qiblas, but the concept also applies to individuals of highest merit in a hierarchical order.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The most correct opinion identifies the Foremost Ones as those who turned their whole lives in obedience when the qibla changed. What qibla – what direction of your life – is Allah calling you to shift right now, and are you willing to endure the sacrifice it demands?
?
You have just learned that there is a hierarchy of merit among the believers. If you are honest with yourself, are you actively striving to move up that hierarchy, or are you complacent with being a 'regular' Muslim? What concrete change will you make this week to draw closer to the rank of the Foremost?
?
The passage shows that some Companions combined multiple honors. Which single act of worship or sacrifice could you commit to that would give you multiple 'precedences' – such as knowledge, charity, and patience – in the sight of Allah?

The Command to Interpret Companion Disputes and the Principle of Ijtihad
Introduces the command to interpret Companion disputes and explains the ijtihad principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author commands interpreting disputes among the Companions, the background of the Ali-Muawiya conflict, and the principle that both the correct and mistaken mujtahid are rewarded.

Core Idea
Companion disputes are to be interpreted positively; ijtihad ensures reward even in error.

After praising the Companions as the best generation, the author addresses the elephant in the room: the conflicts that occurred among them, especially between Ali and Muawiya. This lesson unpacks the rationale behind the command to interpret these disputes, the three stances taken by Companions, and the crucial principle of ijtihad that safeguards their integrity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage from 'وأول التشاجر' to 'وقد شهد الله ورسوله لهم بالعدالة' along with its translation. Notice the poetic line and its commentary.
The translation is literal; some phrases may appear dense.
2
Identify the three factions
Look for the division of the Companions into three groups regarding the Ali-Muawiya conflict. Note how each group is described.
The third faction abstained entirely.
3
Understand the reward system
Focus on the statement: 'المصيب بأجرين والمخطئ بأجر'. Reflect on how this preserves the integrity of both parties.
This is a key Sunni doctrine regarding ijtihad.
4
Connect to the command
Consider why the author commands interpretation (ta'wil) of the disputes rather than condemnation. How does the ijtihad principle support this?
Interpretation removes the impression of sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَوِّلِ التَّشَاجُرَ الذِي وَرَدْ إِنْ خُضْتَ فِيهِ وَاجْتَنِبُ دَاءَ الحَسَدِ قوله : وأَولِ التَّشَاجُرَ الذِي وَرَدْ) ، لما ذكر أن صحبه خير القرون، احتاج للجواب عما وقع بينهم من المنازعات الموهمة قَدْحاً في حقهم، بمعنى أنهم لا يُصرُّون على عمد المعاصي، وإن لم يكونوا معصومين . وقد وقع تشاجر بين عليّ ومعاوية رضي الله عنهما، وقد افترقت الصحابة ثلاث فرق فرقة اجتهدت فظهر لها أن الحق مع عليّ فقاتلت معه، وفرقة اجتهدت فظهر لها أن الحق مع معاوية فقاتلت معه، معاوية فقاتلت معه، وفرقةٌ توقفت، وقد قال العلماء: المصيب بأجرين والمخطئ بأجر، وقد شهد الله ورسوله لهم بالعدالة. قوله : (الذي ورد) أي : ثبت بالأحاديث المتصلة الأسانيد، كما يُؤخذ من شرح المصنف، والشيخ عبد السلام والمراد أنه ثبت بتلك الأحاديث عند الأئمة، وأما ما لم يثبت عند الأئمة فهو باطل لا يحتاج إلى تأويله . وقوله الآتي : (إن خضت فيه أي اطلعت عليه ،وعلمته والفرض أنه ثابت عند الأئمة بالأحاديث المتصلة. والحاصل أن التأويل لا يجب إلا بشرطين : الأول : أن يكون التشاجر ثابتاً عند الأئمة بالأحاديث المتصلة الأسانيد. والثاني : أن يخوض الشخص فيه بأن يطلع على ما جرى بينهم. وخرج بالأول ما لم يثبت عند الأئمة، فهو مردود باطل، فنحكم عليه أنه مردود باطل، ولا يحتاج إلى تأويله، وخرج بالثاني ما ثبت عند الأئمة، لكن لم نطلع عليه ، فلا يجب تأويله أيضاً ؛ لأن تأويل الشيء فرح عن العلم به والفرض أنه غير معلوم. انتهى أجهوري.
And interpret the dispute that has come down, if you engage in it, and avoid the disease of envy. His saying: 'And interpret the dispute that has come down': After he mentioned that his Companions are the best of generations, he needed to answer about what occurred among them of conflicts that give an impression of fault against them, meaning that they do not persist in intentional sins, even if they are not infallible. And a dispute occurred between 'Ali and Mu'awiya, may Allah be pleased with them. The Companions split into three factions: a faction that exercised ijtihad and it appeared to them that the truth was with 'Ali, so they fought with him; a faction that exercised ijtihad and it appeared to them that the truth was with Mu'awiya, so they fought with him; and a faction that abstained. And the scholars have said: the correct one has two rewards, and the mistaken has one reward. And Allah and His Messenger have testified to their integrity. His saying: 'that has come down' means that it is established through connected chains of transmission, as can be taken from the explanation of the author and Shaykh 'Abd al-Salam. The intended meaning is that it is established by those hadiths according to the imams. As for what is not established according to the imams, it is false and does not need interpretation. And his following saying: 'if you engage in it' means if you become aware of it and know it, assuming it is established with the imams through connected chains. The conclusion: interpretation is only obligatory under two conditions: first, that the dispute is established with the imams through hadiths with connected chains; second, that the person engages in it by becoming informed about what occurred among them. Excluded by the first is what is not established according to the imams, so it is rejected and false, and we judge it as rejected false, and it does not need interpretation. Excluded by the second is what is established with the imams but we have not been informed of it, so interpretation is also not obligatory, because interpretation of something depends on knowledge of it, and the assumption is that it is unknown. End of al-Ujhuri.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage explains the line from the poem commanding the interpretation of disputes among the Companions and avoidance of envy. The commentator begins by stating the reason: after praising the Companions as the best generation, the author must address the conflicts that occurred among them, which might suggest fault. The explanation clarifies that the Companions, though not infallible, did not persist in intentional sins. It then recounts the specific dispute between 'Ali and Mu'awiya, noting that the Companions split into three groups: those who supported 'Ali, those who supported Mu'awiya, and those who abstained. The principle is that those who exercised ijtihad and hit the truth get two rewards, those who made a mistake get one, and the Companions are attested for their integrity. The commentator then unpacks the key phrase 'that has come down' to mean that it is established through authentic connected chains according to the imams. He stresses that not everything reported is authentic; only what is established by the imams requires interpretation. The phrase 'if you engage in it' means if one becomes aware of the established report. The upshot is that interpretation (ta'wil) is only obligatory under two conditions: 1) the dispute is authentically transmitted with connected chains according to the imams, and 2) the person becomes aware of it. Without the first condition, the report is false and needs no interpretation. Without the second, even if authentically transmitted, if one does not know of it, no interpretation is required because interpretation presupposes knowledge. The passage ends with a citation from al-Ujhuri.

Key Terms
تأويل
تشاجر
الصحابة
الاجتهاد
المصيب
المخطئ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any Companion who took part in the conflict must be sinful and thus their integrity is compromised.
Correction: The passage shows that each side was acting on ijtihad, and the mistaken one is still rewarded, preserving their integrity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reward for a mujtahid who makes a mistake?
A) No reward
B) One reward
C) Two rewards
D) Punishment

Key Takeaway
Companion disputes must be interpreted positively; ijtihad guarantees reward for both correct and mistaken efforts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about a disagreement between two Companions—like Ali and Muawiya—does any feeling of criticism or unease arise in your heart? This lesson commands you to interpret it positively. Will you obey this command today, or will you let the disease of envy take root?
?
The mistaken mujtahid still earns one reward. Do you apply the same mercy to yourself when you make a mistake after sincere effort, or do you drown in guilt? And do you extend that same benefit of the doubt to others who disagree with you on religious matters?
?
The Companions split into three groups over a grave issue. If you were in their place, would you have the humility to admit uncertainty and abstain? Or would your zeal for one side cause you to bear ill will against the other? This lesson demands you check your heart right now: do you hold any grudge against any Companion, even subtly?

Two Conditions for Obligatory Interpretation of Companion Disputes
Specifies the two conditions for obligatory interpretation of Companion disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two conditions under which interpretation of a Companion dispute becomes obligatory: authentic transmission and personal knowledge of the report.

Core Idea
Interpretation is only required when the report is authentic and you are aware of it.

The previous lesson established that we must interpret Companion disputes positively. But is every reported dispute subject to interpretation? The commentator now clarifies that only authentically transmitted reports that one has personally encountered require interpretation. This lesson details the two conditions and their exclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage from 'قوله: (الذي ورد)' to the end. Notice how the commentator explains 'that has come down' and 'if you engage in it'.
This is a more legalistic part; pay attention to conditions.
2
Identify the two conditions
The commentator explicitly states two conditions. Find them and note how they are phrased.
They are: (1) authenticity via connected chains, (2) personal engagement/knowledge.
3
Understand the exclusions
The passage mentions what is excluded when each condition is missing. Work through each exclusion.
If not established, the report is false; if not known, no obligation.
4
Reflect on the wisdom
Think about why these conditions are necessary. How do they prevent excessive speculation and protect the Companions?
Interpretation requires knowledge; ignorance is an excuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage explains the line from the poem commanding the interpretation of disputes among the Companions and avoidance of envy. The commentator begins by stating the reason: after praising the Companions as the best generation, the author must address the conflicts that occurred among them, which might suggest fault. The explanation clarifies that the Companions, though not infallible, did not persist in intentional sins. It then recounts the specific dispute between 'Ali and Mu'awiya, noting that the Companions split into three groups: those who supported 'Ali, those who supported Mu'awiya, and those who abstained. The principle is that those who exercised ijtihad and hit the truth get two rewards, those who made a mistake get one, and the Companions are attested for their integrity. The commentator then unpacks the key phrase 'that has come down' to mean that it is established through authentic connected chains according to the imams. He stresses that not everything reported is authentic; only what is established by the imams requires interpretation. The phrase 'if you engage in it' means if one becomes aware of the established report. The upshot is that interpretation (ta'wil) is only obligatory under two conditions: 1) the dispute is authentically transmitted with connected chains according to the imams, and 2) the person becomes aware of it. Without the first condition, the report is false and needs no interpretation. Without the second, even if authentically transmitted, if one does not know of it, no interpretation is required because interpretation presupposes knowledge. The passage ends with a citation from al-Ujhuri.

Key Terms
تأويل
الأحاديث المتصلة
الأئمة
الشرطان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that every report of a Companion dispute must be interpreted, even if it is weak or fabricated.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only authentic reports require interpretation; false reports are simply rejected.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a Companion dispute report that is established but unknown to a person?
A) Interpretation is obligatory
B) Interpretation is recommended
C) Interpretation is not obligatory
D) The report must be rejected

Key Takeaway
Interpretation of Companion disputes is obligatory only when the report is authentically transmitted and you are personally aware of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever formed a negative opinion about a Companion based on a story you heard without verifying its authenticity? How does this lesson call you to repent from that and commit to only speaking from verified knowledge?
?
What reports about the Companions have you heard that you now suspect might not be authentic? Will you stop repeating them and urge others to do the same?
?
This lesson says you are not required to interpret what you do not know. Are you willing to admit ignorance regarding unclear reports about the Companions, rather than speculating? Will you actively seek to know only what is authentically established?

Interpreting Conflicts Among the Companions
Author commands favorable interpretation and describes three groups in the Companions' conflict.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reported disputes among the Companions must be interpreted favorably and how scholars view those involved based on their ijtihad.

Core Idea
Even in conflict, every Companion is just and rewarded for their sincere effort.

The author has just affirmed that the Companions are the best of generations. Now he addresses a potential objection: how can they be the best if they fought each other? This passage explains the proper attitude towards those reports, using the well-known conflict between Ali and Muawiyah as an example, and describes the three groups that emerged along with the scholars' ruling on their efforts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the author's argument.
Notice the command 'Awwil' (interpret) at the beginning.
2
Identify the three groups
The passage mentions three groups of Companions in the conflict. Find each group and note what they did.
The third group did not fight but abstained.
3
Understand the scholars' ruling
The passage states a principle from the scholars. Understand what it means for the correct and mistaken mujtahid.
This shows that both are rewarded, not punished.
4
Reflect on the testimony of Allah and His Messenger
The passage concludes that Allah and His Messenger testified to the justice of all Companions. Consider how this reinforces the obligation to interpret favorably.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأَوِّلِ التَّشَاجُرَ الذِي وَرَدْ. لما ذكر أن صحبه خير القرون، احتاج للجواب عما وقع بينهم من المنازعات الموهمة قَدْحاً في حقهم، بمعنى أنهم لا يُصرُّون على عمد المعاصي، وإن لم يكونوا معصومين . وقد وقع تشاجر بين عليّ ومعاوية رضي الله عنهما، وقد افترقت الصحابة ثلاث فرق فرقة اجتهدت فظهر لها أن الحق مع عليّ فقاتلت معه، وفرقة اجتهدت فظهر لها أن الحق مع معاوية فقاتلت معه، وفرقةٌ توقفت، وقد قال العلماء: المصيب بأجرين والمخطئ بأجر، وقد شهد الله ورسوله لهم بالعدالة.
And interpret the reported conflict [favorably]. Since he mentioned that his Companions are the best of generations, he needed to answer what occurred among them of disputes that might imply criticism of their status, meaning that they do not persist intentionally in sins, even if they are not infallible. And a conflict occurred between Ali and Muawiyah, may Allah be pleased with them, and the Companions split into three groups: a group that exerted effort and it appeared to them that the truth was with Ali, so they fought with him; a group that exerted effort and it appeared to them that the truth was with Muawiyah, so they fought with him; and a group that abstained. And the scholars said: The correct one gets two rewards, and the mistaken one gets one reward. And Allah and His Messenger have testified to their justice.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of the reported disputes among the Companions and the obligation to interpret them in a good light. The author begins by stating the command to interpret the reported conflict (tashajur) that has been authentically transmitted. He explains why this is necessary: since the Companions are the best of generations, any reports that seem to imply criticism must be interpreted favorably. The meaning is that they do not persist intentionally in sins, although they are not infallible. Then the author mentions a specific example: the conflict between Ali and Muawiyah. The Companions split into three groups: those who sided with Ali, those who sided with Muawiyah, and those who remained neutral. The scholars have a principle: the one who is correct in his ijtihad gets two rewards, and the one who errs gets one reward. Moreover, Allah and His Messenger have testified to the overall justice of all Companions. Therefore, no one should be criticized for the disputes.

العدالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage clarifies that even the mistaken mujtahid receives a reward, and all Companions are testified to be just.

Key Takeaway
The Companions' conflicts do not diminish their justice; each is rewarded according to their sincere ijtihad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a disagreement among people you respect, do you rush to assume bad faith or do you grant the benefit of the doubt? The Companions' example forces you to consider: is your default to interpret conflicts favorably, even when your own inclinations side with one party?
?
If you were in a situation where your sincere reasoning led you to oppose someone you know is righteous, would you still be able to maintain respect and avoid condemning them? How does the principle that both sides can be rewarded challenge your own judgments of others?
?
The Companions' conflict was settled in the best way—each was rewarded for their effort. But what about your own conflicts? Are you willing to accept that someone who disagrees with you might be sincerely mistaken and still deserving of reward? Can you let go of the need to be 'right' and instead trust that Allah judges with perfect justice?

The Two Imams of Theology and the Master of Sufism
The commentator identifies the two imams of kalam and explains the mention of al-Junayd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the identity of the two great imams of theology and the role of al-Junayd as the master of Sufism, as well as the grammatical construction used to introduce him in the poem.

Core Idea
The great imams of theology and the master of Sufism are all guides of the nation.

In this passage, the commentator identifies two towering figures in Islamic theology: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. He then clarifies the poem’s reference to 'Abu al-Qasim' as none other than Muhammad al-Junayd, the master of the Sufis. This lesson unpacks the significance of these figures and the grammatical structure used to introduce al-Junayd.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the names and the grammatical explanation.
The passage has two parts: identification of two imams and explanation of al-Junayd.
2
Identify the three figures
Find Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, and Abu al-Qasim al-Junayd. Note their titles: the first two are imams of theology, the third is the imam of the Sufi order.
3
Understand the grammar
The phrase 'Thus Abu al-Qasim' is explained grammatically. 'Thus' is a fronted predicate, and 'Abu al-Qasim' is the delayed subject. This inversion emphasizes the likeness.
The commentator suggests the poet could have been more explicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإمام أبو الحسن الأشعري وأبو منصور الماتريدي.. إمام الطائفة الصوفية وقوله : (كَذَا أَبو القَاسِم) ، (كذا) خبرٌ مقدَّم ، و (أبو القاسم) مبتدأ مؤخَرُ؛ أي : مثل من ذكر في الهداية واستقامة الطريق أبو القاسم محمد الجنيد سيد الصوفية علماً وعملاً ، ولعل المصنف رأى شهرته بهذه الكنية، ولو قال : (جنيدهم أيضاً هداة الأمة لكان أوضح.
And Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi ... the Imam of the Sufi order. And His saying: 'Thus Abu al-Qasim' – 'Thus' is a predicate placed before, and 'Abu al-Qasim' is a subject placed after; meaning: like those mentioned in guidance and uprightness of the path, Abu al-Qasim Muhammad al-Junayd, the master of the Sufis in knowledge and action. Perhaps the author saw his fame by this epithet, and if he had said 'their Junayd also (are) guides of the nation' it would have been clearer.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage identifies two important figures: the two imams of theology (Ash'ari and Maturidi) and the imam of the Sufi order, al-Junayd. The commentator then explains the grammar of the poem's phrase 'Thus Abu al-Qasim', noting that it means al-Junayd is like the previously mentioned scholars in being a guide. He suggests that the poet could have been more explicit.

Refuting the Denial of Saintly Wonders
The author rebuts arguments of those who deny saintly wonders.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main arguments used by those who deny karamat (saintly wonders) and the beginning of the classical Sunni rebuttal.

Core Idea
Denying karamat due to fear of confusion with prophethood is a weak argument.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Murid addresses a polemical issue: whether saints can perform extraordinary events (karamat). Some scholars, including al-Halimi from the Ash'ari school and most Mu'tazila, denied them. Here, the commentator presents their two main arguments and starts to dismantle the first one. Even though the rebuttal is incomplete in our text, we can learn the structure of the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the language. Notice the poetic line being explained and the grammatical note about the hamza.
The passage begins by quoting and explaining the poem's line.
2
Identify the two arguments of the deniers
The commentator lists two reasons given by those who deny karamat. Find them and understand each: (1) confusion with prophethood, (2) loss of extraordinariness due to frequency.
The second argument is introduced by 'and that if they appeared...'
3
Examine the beginning of the rebuttal
The text says 'and the first was rebutted...' but cuts off. Consider what arguments might follow to distinguish miracles from saintly wonders.
This incomplete rebuttal invites reflection on how Sunni theologians typically differentiate mu'jiza from karama.
4
Take the checkpoint quiz
Test your understanding of which groups denied karamat by answering the multiple-choice question.
The answer is explicitly given in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَمَنْ نَفَاهَا فَانْبِذَنْ كَلامَهُ :أي: ومن نفى الكرامة، وقال بعدم جوازها كالأستاذ أبي عبد الله الحليمي من أهل السنة وجمهور المعتزلة اطرحن كلامه ولا تعول عليه. وأتى المصنف بهمزة الوصل للضرورة، فتكون مكسورة، وليست همزة قطع كما قد يتوهم؛ فإن الذي في القرآن ثلاثي قال تعالى: وَقَائِي إِلَيْهِمْ [الأنفال : ٥٨] . ، وتمسك من نفى الكرامة بأنه لو ظهرت الخوارق من الأولياء لالتبس النبي بغيره ؛ لأن الخارق (۱) إنما هو المعجزة، وبأنها لو ظهرت على أيديهم لكثرت بكثرتهم وخرجت عن كونها خارقة للعادة والفرض أنها كذلك، ورُدَّ الأول بأنه ليس
His saying: 'And whoever denies them, then cast away his speech' means: whoever denies karamah and says that it is not permissible, like al-Ustadh Abu Abdallah al-Halimi from Ahl al-Sunnah and the majority of the Mu'tazila, discard his speech and do not rely on it. The author used a connecting hamza out of necessity, so it is broken, and it is not a hamza of cutting as might be imagined; for what is in the Quran is triliteral, He said Almighty: 'and I am a warner to you' [al-Anfal:58]. And he who denies karamah clung to the argument that if marvels appeared from saints, the prophet would be confused with others, because the marvel (1) is actually the miracle, and that if they appeared at their hands, they would be many due to their multitude and would cease to be extraordinary, and the presumption is that they are such. And the first was rebutted by saying that it is not...
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage addresses the denial of karamat (saintly wonders). The author first explains the line of the poem: 'Whoever denies them, then cast away his speech' means that one should reject the opinion of those who deny karamah, mentioning al-Ustadh Abu Abdallah al-Halimi (from Ahl al-Sunnah) and the majority of the Mu'tazila as examples. The author then notes a grammatical point about the hamza. He presents two main arguments used by deniers: (1) If extraordinary events appeared at the hands of saints, people would confuse saints with prophets, because the extraordinary event (kharq) is what is normally considered a miracle (mu'jiza); (2) If such events appeared from many saints, they would become frequent and thus cease to be extraordinary, but the assumption is that they are indeed extraordinary. The author then begins to rebut the first argument: 'and the first was rebutted by saying that it is not...' but the text cuts off before completing the rebuttal. The passage thus lays out the objection and the opening of its response.

Key Terms
كرامة
المعجزة
الخارق
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Karamat would cause confusion between saints and prophets.
Correction: Miracles (mu'jizat) are specifically given to prophets as proof of prophethood, whereas karamat occur without such a claim; the distinguishing factor is the claim and context, not the event itself.

Checkpoint

D) The early Salaf and the Khawarij.

Key Takeaway
Deniers of karamat argue that saintly wonders would be confused with prophethood or become non-extraordinary, but these objections can be rebutted by noting the different context and purpose of each.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In your own life, are there times when you have blurred the line between what is genuinely from Allah and what is merely impressive by worldly standards? How can you cultivate a heart that seeks the true source of wonders?

Three Mu'tazilite Views on Killing and Death
Al-Ujhuri refutes three Mu'tazilite schools on death and killing, highlighting verbal agreement with Abu al-Hudhayl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Mu'tazilite schools regarding death and killing and how Ahl al-Sunna refutes them, noting the verbal agreement with Abu al-Hudhayl.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila have three distinct views—only Abu al-Hudhayl's is verbally similar to Ahl al-Sunna.

This passage addresses the Islamic theological question of whether a person killed has died a natural death or not. The Mu'tazila split into three schools: al-Ka'bi holds the killed person is not dead, the majority hold the killer cuts short the term, and Abu al-Hudhayl holds the person would have died anyway. Al-Ujhuri explains each and shows that the difference between Ahl al-Sunna and Abu al-Hudhayl is merely verbal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three Mu'tazilite positions
Read the passage to identify the three schools: al-Ka'bi, the majority, and Abu al-Hudhayl. Notice how each defines death and killing.
Al-Ka'bi uses Quran 3:158 to argue that killing and death are different acts.
2
Examine the evidence each school uses
Al-Ka'bi uses the conjunction of 'die' and 'killed' to argue for two separate acts. The majority argue for one term. Abu al-Hudhayl uses the impossibility of change in God's knowledge.
3
Grasp the Ahl al-Sunna position and the verbal agreement
Ahl al-Sunna distinguish between certainty based on God's knowledge and possibility based on human ignorance. Learn why Abu al-Hudhayl's view is considered verbally similar.
When considering God's knowledge, both agree the killed person would have died at that time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وغَيْرُ هَذَا بَاطِلٌ لا يُقْبَلُ أي وغير ما ذكر من مذاهب المخالفين لأهل السنة غير مطابق للواقع، لا يقبل عند العقلاء المتمسكين بالحق. وأشار المصنف بذلك للردّ على أهل الاعتزال، فإن لهم مذاهب ثلاثة : الأول مذهبُ الكعبي : وهو أن المقتول ليس بميت ؛ لأنَّ القتل فعل العبد، والموت فعله تعالى، واستدل على ذلك بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَين مُتُمْ أَوْ قُتِلْتُمْ ﴾ [آل عمران : ١٥٨] ، فإن العطف يقتضي المغايرة، وأهل السنة يقولون المعنى : ولئن مُتُّم من غير سبب
(۲) قوله: (وإلا فقد بان ... إلخ) حاصل ذلك أنه بالنظر إلى تعلّق علم الله بموته في هذا الوقت المدلول عليه بقتله الموجود خارجاً يُقال : لو لم يقتل لمات قطعاً، وقولهم أولاً : لو لم يقتل لجاز أن يموت وألا يموت بقطع النظر عن تعلق علم الله بموته في هذا الوقت، فتلخص أن أهل السنة بالنظر إلى تعلق علم الله بموته في هذا الوقت قاطعون بأنه لو لم يقتل لمات بلا قتل، وبقطع النظر عن تعلق علم الله بموته في هذا الوقت يقولون بعد قتله : إنه لو لم يقتل لعاش أو مات، ولا يقطعون يواحدٍ منهما ، فهم بعد القتل بالنظر إلى تعلق علم الله قاطعون بأنه لو لم يقتل لمات، وبقطع النظر ذلك يجوزون موته وحياته على تقدير عدم قتله، وكلٌّ من التجويز والقطع إنما هو بعد القتل. وحاصل الفرق بين مذهب أهل السنة ومذاهب المعتزلة الثلاث أن الكعبي منهم يقطع بعد قتله بأنه لو لم يقتل لعاش، وكذا جمهور المعتزلة إلا إن الكعبي يجعل له :أجلين : أجلاً على تقدير قتله، وأجلاً على تقدير موته، والجمهور يجعلون له أجلاً واحداً هو أجل موته، ويقولون: القاتل قطع عليه ذلك الأجل. عن وأما أبو الهذيل منهم فيقطع بعد قتله بأنه لو لم يُقتل لمات بلا قتل واستدل على ذلك بدليلين، ثانيهما أنه لو لم يمت لزم التغير في العلم وهو محال، والدليلان مذكوران في شرح المصنف. وبهذا يُعلم أنه ناظر إلى تعلق العلم بموته في هذا الوقت. وتقدَّم أن أهل السنة بالنظر إلى تعلق علم الله بموته في هذا الوقت قاطعون بأنه لو لم يقتل لمات بلا قتل وبهذا يعلم أن الخلاف بين أهل السنة وأبي الهذيل من المعتزلة لفظي لا معنوي . هذا ما ظهر في هذا المقام انتهى أجهوري.
His statement: 'And other than this is false and unacceptable' — meaning that what is mentioned other than that from the schools of those who oppose Ahl al-Sunna is not in accordance with reality, and is not accepted by rational people who adhere to the truth. And the author indicated by this the refutation of the Mu'tazila, for they have three schools: The first is the school of al-Ka'bi: that the killed person is not dead, because killing is the act of the servant and death is the act of Allah Most High, and he adduced as evidence His statement: 'If you die or are killed' [3:158], because the conjunction requires difference. And Ahl al-Sunna say the meaning: 'If you die without a cause...'
(2) His statement: 'Otherwise, it becomes clear...' — the conclusion of that is that in light of the connection of Allah's knowledge to his death at this time, indicated by his killing that exists externally, it is said: If he had not been killed, he would have died definitively. And their initial statement: If he had not been killed, it would be possible that he die and possible that he not die, disregarding the connection of Allah's knowledge to his death at this time. So it is summarized that Ahl al-Sunna, with regard to the connection of Allah's knowledge to his death at this time, are certain that if he had not been killed, he would have died without killing. And disregarding the connection of Allah's knowledge to his death at this time, they say after his killing: If he had not been killed, he would have lived or died, and they do not affirm one of them. So they, after the killing, with regard to the connection of Allah's knowledge, are certain that if he had not been killed, he would have died. And disregarding that connection, they permit his death and his life on the assumption of not killing him. And both the permission and certainty are only after the killing. And the summary of the difference between the school of Ahl al-Sunna and the three schools of the Mu'tazila is that al-Ka'bi among them is certain after his killing that if he had not been killed, he would have lived. Similarly the majority of the Mu'tazila, except that al-Ka'bi holds that he has two appointed terms: an appointed term on the assumption of his being killed, and an appointed term on the assumption of his death. The majority hold that he has one appointed term, which is the term of his death, and they say: The killer cut short that term. As for Abu al-Hudhayl among them, he is certain after his killing that if he had not been killed, he would have died without killing, and he adduced two proofs, the second of which is that if he did not die, a change in knowledge would be necessary, which is impossible. The two proofs are mentioned in the commentary of the author. And by this it is known that he is considering the connection of knowledge to his death at this time. And it has preceded that Ahl al-Sunna, with regard to the connection of Allah's knowledge to his death at this time, are certain that if he had not been killed, he would have died without killing. And by this it is known that the dispute between Ahl al-Sunna and Abu al-Hudhayl of the Mu'tazila is verbal, not substantive. This is what appeared in this context. End of al-Ujhuri.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of opposing views. The author first explains that the statement 'And other than this is false and unacceptable' rejects all doctrines that contradict Ahl al-Sunna. Then he describes the three Mu'tazilite schools: (1) Al-Ka'bi holds that the killed person is not dead because killing is the servant's act and death is God's act, citing Quran 3:158 as evidence. (2) The majority hold that there is one appointed term for death, and the killer cuts it short. (3) Abu al-Hudhayl holds that if the person had not been killed, he would have died at that time anyway, similar to Ahl al-Sunna's view after the fact. The footnote elaborates that Ahl al-Sunna distinguish between certainty based on God's knowledge and possibility based on our ignorance. It concludes that the difference between Ahl al-Sunna and Abu al-Hudhayl is merely verbal, as both agree that the killed person would have died if not killed, given God's knowledge.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الكعبي
أبو الهذيل
الأجل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazilites hold the same view on death and killing.
Correction: The passage shows three distinct schools: al-Ka'bi, the majority, and Abu al-Hudhayl, each with different arguments and conclusions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which Mu'tazilite school holds that if the person had not been killed, he would have lived?
A) Abu al-Hudhayl
B) Al-Ka'bi
C) The majority Mu'tazila
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila have three distinct views on killing and death, but Abu al-Hudhayl's view is verbally identical to Ahl al-Sunna when accounting for God's foreknowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever witnessed a death that seemed premature or unjust, and did that event shake your certainty that Allah's decree is always perfectly timed? How does this lesson's discussion of foreknowledge help you realign your heart with that truth?
?
When you hear about a murder or an accident, which explanation do you lean toward: that the killer truly cut short a life, or that the appointed moment had arrived by Allah's will? Sit with that inclination—what does it reveal about your deepest understanding of divine sovereignty?
?
This passage ends by calling a dispute 'verbal' between two groups. Is your own faith ever merely verbal—saying the right words while your heart secretly harbors doubts about Allah's complete control? What would it cost you to truly internalize that every death, even by violence, happens only when Allah has decreed it?

Khawarij and Mu'tazila on Grave Sinners
Compares Khawarij and Mu'tazila positions on grave sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing positions of the Khawarij and Mu'tazila regarding grave sinners and their respective theological justifications.

Core Idea
The Khawarij declare grave sinners disbelievers; the Mu'tazila place them in an intermediate station.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Mureed explains two deviant sects' views on grave sinners before presenting the orthodox Sunni position. Understanding these positions will help you appreciate why the Sunni view avoids both extremes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the Khawarij are discussed first, then the Mu'tazila.
The passage is a comparison, so pay attention to contrasts.
2
Identify the key difference
Compare the ruling for grave sinners: Khawarij say 'kufr'; Mu'tazila say 'not kufr but not iman' (manzila bayn al-manzilatayn).
3
Note the common consequence
Both groups agree that grave sinners are forever in Hell, but with different punishments: Khawarij punish as disbelievers, Mu'tazila as fasiqs.
This shows a shared extremism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخالفت الخوارج فكفَّروا مرتكب الذنوب وجعلوا جميع الذنوب كبائر، كما سيأتي، ولم يُكفّروا بتكفير مرتكب الذنوب، مع أن مَنْ كفّر مؤمناً كَفَر ؛ لأنهم قالوا ذلك بتأويل واجتهاد. وأما المعتزلة فأخرجوا مرتكب الكبيرة من الإيمان، ولم يُدخلوه في الكفر إلا بالاستحلال، فجعلوه منزلةٌ بين المنزلتين، فمرتكبُ الكبيرة مُخلَّدٌ عند الفريقين في النار، ويُعذَّب عند الخوارج عذاب الكفار ، وعند المعتزلة عذاب الفُسّاق.
And the Khawarij differed, so they declared the committer of sins to be disbelievers and made all sins major, as will come, and they did not consider themselves disbelievers for declaring a believer a disbeliever, even though whoever declares a believer a disbeliever has disbelieved; because they said that with interpretation and ijtihad. As for the Mu'tazila, they removed the committer of a major sin from faith, and did not enter him into disbelief except by deeming it permissible [istihlal], so they made him a station between the two stations. So the committer of a major sin, according to both groups, is forever in the Fire, and is punished according to the Khawarij with the punishment of disbelievers, and according to the Mu'tazila with the punishment of the wicked.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage compares the Khawarij and Mu'tazila positions on grave sinners. It begins by stating that the Khawarij differed and declared sinners disbelievers, considering all sins major. They did not consider themselves disbelievers for this declaration because they acted on interpretation and ijtihad. Then it describes the Mu'tazila view: they remove the grave sinner from faith but do not label him a disbeliever unless he deems the sin permissible; instead, they place him in an intermediate station. Both groups agree that the grave sinner is immortal in Hell, but the Khawarij punish him as a disbeliever while the Mu'tazila punish him as a wicked person (fasiq). This sets up the orthodox Sunni position that follows later.

Key Terms
الخوارج
المعتزلة
مرتكب الكبيرة
منزلة بين المنزلتين
التكفير
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sunni position on grave sinners aligns with either the Khawarij or Mu'tazila.
Correction: The Sunni position rejects both extremes: a grave sinner remains a believer but is subject to punishment in Hell if not forgiven, without being placed in an intermediate category or declared a disbeliever.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the Khawarij consider as the ruling for the committer of a sin?
A) They are in an intermediate station.
B) They are disbelievers.
C) They are still believers.
D) They are not judged.

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij and Mu'tazila offer contrasting but equally deviant views on grave sinners, which the Sunni position rejects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim sinning, do you ever feel a sense of inward pride or silent condemnation? How does the Khawarij's harsh takfir expose the danger of that attitude, and what are you going to do the next time such a thought arises?
?
The Mu'tazila invented a middle ground to avoid calling a sinner a disbeliever. Are you ever tempted to create your own 'middle stations' in your heart — excusing disobedience while still claiming faith — instead of calling sin exactly what it is and repenting? What sin have you been rationalizing?
?
If you were to die tonight in your current state of sin, which of the two groups' views would you prefer to be true? Why does that question unsettle you, and how does it urge you to take your own state of faith more seriously?

Refuting the Mu'tazila Definition of Rizq
Refutation of Mu'tazila definition of rizq using talazum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila definition of rizq as ownership is rejected by Sunni scholars, and how the principles of talazum (tard and aks) are used to refute it.

Core Idea
Defining rizq as ownership alone leads to absurdities like Allah being provided-for.

The Mu'tazila proposed a definition of rizq that seems simple but has drastic consequences. In this lesson, we examine their claim and see how Sunni imams refute it using logical co-implication.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila definition
Read the Arabic passage once and focus on the Mu'tazila claim: rizq is what is owned, not necessarily benefited from.
Notice how they exclude the element of benefit.
2
Identify the problematic consequences
The author points out two problems: a person may not receive his rizq, and one may eat another's rizq. Reflect on why these follow from the definition.
3
Understand the refutation via tard and aks
The author uses two logical tests: tard (co-implication in affirmation) and aks (co-implication in negation). See how each test fails for the Mu'tazila definition.
Tard: if ownership sufficed, Allah's ownership would make Him provided-for. Aks: if ownership required, animals/slaves would have no rizq.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why Sunni scholars upheld benefit as a necessary condition. This shows the importance of testing definitions with logical principles.
This lesson demonstrates the use of talazum in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : وقِيْلَ: لَا بَلْ مَا مُلِكُ أي : وقال جماعةٌ من المعتزلة : ليس الرزقُ ما انتفع به، بل هو ما ملك، فلا يُعتبر فيه الانتفاع ويُعتبر فيه المملوكية انتفع به أم لا . ويلزم على هذا أن الشخص قد لا يستوفي رزقه، وأنه قد يأكل رزق غيره، ويأكلُ غيره رزقه . وقوله : (ومَا اتَّبِعْ) أي: ولم يتبع هذا القول أئمتنا لفساده طرداً، وهو التلازم في الثبوت، وعكساً وهو التلازم في النفي؛ أما الأوَّل فلان الله تعالى مالك لجميع الأشياء، ولا يُسمَّى ملكه رزقاً اتفاقاً، وإلا لكان الله تعالى مرزوقاً . وأما الثاني فلخروج رزق الدواب والعبيد والإماء عند بعض الأئمة؛ كالإمام الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه، فإنه يقول : لا ملك للعبيد والإماء أصلاً، وقال الإمام مالك : يملكون ملكاً غير تام.
And His statement: 'And it was said: No, rather it is what is owned.' That is, a group of the Mu'tazila said: Sustenance is not what is benefited from, but rather it is what is owned. Thus benefit is not considered, but ownership is considered, whether it is benefited from or not. And this entails that a person may not complete his sustenance, and that he may eat the sustenance of another, and another may eat his sustenance. And His statement: 'and it was not followed' i.e., our imams did not follow this saying due to its corruption in both 'tard' i.e., the co-implication in affirmation, and 'aks' i.e., the co-implication in negation. As for the first, because Allah Almighty is the owner of all things, and His ownership is not called sustenance by agreement, otherwise Allah would be provided-for. As for the second, because it excludes the sustenance of animals, slaves, and concubines according to some imams, like Imam al-Shafi'i (may Allah be pleased with him), for he says that slaves and concubines have no ownership at all; and Imam Malik said that they have incomplete ownership.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the Mu'tazila definition of rizq. The Mu'tazila defined rizq as what is owned, regardless of benefit. The author shows the problematic consequences: if ownership suffices, then a person may not actually receive his full rizq (since others might own it), and one might consume another's rizq. The author then explains why this view was not followed by Sunni imams, using the principles of 'tard' (co-implication in affirmation) and 'aks' (co-implication in negation). The first: if all ownership were rizq, then Allah's ownership of everything would make Him 'provided-for', which is impossible. The second: if ownership is required, then animals and slaves who do not have full ownership (according to Shafi'i and Maliki) would have no rizq, which is false.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الملك
طرد
عكس
ملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that rizq is simply anything owned, regardless of benefit.
Correction: The Sunni position is that rizq is what one benefits from, and ownership alone is insufficient, as shown by logical refutation.

Checkpoint
The author's first argument against the Mu'tazila definition is that it would imply that Allah is ______.
A) not generous
B) provided-for (marzuq)
C) unjust
D) unaware

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila definition of rizq as ownership is invalid because it fails the logical tests of tard and aks.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of 'rizq' in your daily duas, do you automatically equate it with ownership of money, property, or possessions? How might this narrow definition blind you to the countless ways Allah provides through benefit—health, family, opportunities—that you do not own? What would it take for you to re-define rizq as every blessing that benefits you, owned or not?
?
The Mu'tazila clung to their definition despite its logical flaws because it fit their rational framework. Reflect on an intellectual position you hold that, if pushed to its logical conclusion, would lead to an absurdity about Allah's perfection. Are you willing to abandon that position for the sake of truth, even if it means humbling your own reasoning?
?
If Allah owns everything, yet is never 'provided-for,' your sustenance is purely a gift—not earned by ownership. Does your heart truly accept that you are dependent on Allah for every bite, even the food you buy with your own money? How does this lesson change your sense of security and gratitude today?

The Two Types of Provision and the Mu'tazilite Definition
The author lists two types of rizq and presents the Mu'tazilite definition with its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of provision (physical and spiritual) and the Mu'tazilite definition that equates provision with ownership rather than benefit.

Core Idea
Provision is both apparent and inward, but the Mu'tazila limit it to ownership.

This lesson begins the section on sam'iyyat (matters known through revelation). The author immediately establishes that provision (rizq) comes in two forms: one for the body, like food, and one for the heart, like knowledge. He then presents the Mu'tazilite counter-definition that sparked a major theological debate. We will see the consequences that follow from their definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of rizq
Read the first two lines where the author states that provisions are of two types: apparent for bodies and inward for hearts. Notice the examples given.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite definition
Read the part beginning with 'وقيل: لا بل ما ملك'. Understand that the Mu'tazila define rizq as what is owned, regardless of benefit.
They reject benefit as a condition.
3
Consider the consequences
Read the sentence following the definition that lists two necessary consequences: a person may not exhaust his provision, and a person may eat another's provision.
These consequences are used later in the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السمعيات
قائدة الأرزاق نوعان:
ظاهرة للأبدان؛ كالأقوات.
وباطنة للقلوب؛ كالعلوم والمعارف.
وقوله : وقِيْلَ: لَا بَلْ مَا مُلِكُ أي : وقال جماعةٌ من المعتزلة : ليس الرزقُ ما انتفع به، بل هو ما ملك، فلا يُعتبر فيه الانتفاع ويُعتبر فيه المملوكية انتفع به أم لا . ويلزم على هذا أن الشخص قد لا يستوفي رزقه، وأنه قد يأكل رزق غيره، ويأكلُ غيره رزقه .
وقوله : (ومَا اتَّبِعْ) أي: ولم يتبع هذا القول أئمتنا لفساده طرداً، وهو التلازم في الثبوت، وعكساً وهو التلازم في النفي؛ أما الأوَّل فلان الله تعالى مالك لجميع الأشياء، ولا يُسمَّى ملكه رزقاً اتفاقاً، وإلا لكان الله تعالى مرزوقاً .
وأما الثاني فلخروج رزق الدواب والعبيد والإماء عند بعض الأئمة؛ كالإمام الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه، فإنه يقول : لا ملك للعبيد والإماء أصلاً وقال الإمام مالك : يملكون ملكاً غير تام.
The [section on] sam'iyyat (matters heard from revelation).
The leading [statement]: Provisions are two types:
Apparent for bodies, like foods;
And inward for hearts, like sciences and cognitions.
And His statement: 'And it was said: No, rather what is owned' means: And a group of the Mu'tazila said: Provision is not what one benefits from, but rather it is what is owned. So benefit is not considered in it, but ownership is considered, whether one benefits from it or not. And this entails that a person may not receive all of his provision, and that he may eat the provision of others, and others may eat his provision.
And His statement: 'And what was followed' means: And our imams did not follow this opinion due to its corruption, both in positive entailment (al-tarad), which is entailment in affirmation, and in converse (al-'aks), which is entailment in negation. As for the first: because God Almighty is the owner of all things, and His ownership is not called provision by consensus, otherwise God would be provided for. As for the second: because it excludes the provision of cattle, male slaves, and female slaves according to some imams, like Imam al-Shafi'i (may God be pleased with him), for he says: Slaves and female slaves have no ownership at all. And Imam Malik said: They have incomplete ownership.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'rizq' (provision) and refutes the Mu'tazilite position. The author first states that provisions are of two types: apparent (e.g., food) and inward (e.g., knowledge). Then he presents the Mu'tazilite definition: rizq is what is owned, regardless of benefit. He points out two necessary consequences: (1) a person might not exhaust his allotted provision, and (2) a person might eat another's provision. The author then gives two arguments (tard and 'aks) to show why the imams rejected this view. The first argument (tard): if rizq were simply ownership, then God's ownership of all things would entail that God is 'provided for', which is impossible. The second argument ('aks): the Mu'tazilite definition would exclude the provision of animals and slaves, since some imams (like al-Shafi'i) hold that slaves do not own property at all. Thus the correct definition is that rizq is what one benefits from.

Key Terms
رزق
تملك
انتفاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That rizq (provision) is simply property ownership, regardless of use or benefit.
Correction: The correct Sunni view is that rizq is what one benefits from, whether owned or not.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite definition presented in the passage, what is rizq?
A) What one benefits from
B) What one owns
C) What one eats
D) What one learns

Key Takeaway
Provision (rizq) in its full sense includes both material and spiritual sustenance; the Mu'tazila wrongly reduce it to mere ownership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you value the inward provision of knowledge and spiritual insight as much as your daily bread? When was the last time you thanked Allah for something you learned?
?
If provision were only about ownership, would you feel secure in Allah's promise of sustenance? How does this lesson challenge your trust in Allah's provision beyond material wealth?
?
Are there areas in your life where you benefit from what you do not 'own'—like health, relationships, or intellect? How can you acknowledge these as part of Allah's rizq and adjust your gratitude accordingly?

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Definition (Tard and 'Aks)
The author refutes the Mu'tazilite definition using tard and 'aks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical arguments—tard (positive entailment) and 'aks (converse entailment)—used by Sunni scholars to refute the Mu'tazilite definition of rizq.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite definition fails because it would imply God is provided for and excludes animals and slaves.

After presenting the Mu'tazilite definition, the author now explains why the Sunni imams did not adopt it. He employs two logical tests: tard (positive entailment) and 'aks (converse). These tests show that the definition leads to absurd consequences. We will examine each argument in detail.

Guided Path
1
Understand tard and 'aks
Read the definition of tard as entailment in affirmation and 'aks as entailment in negation. These are standard logical tools for testing definitions.
Tard: if the definition is correct, then every instance of the definiendum must match the definiens. 'Aks: if the definiens is absent, the definiendum must also be absent.
2
Examine the first argument (tard)
Read the first argument: if rizq is defined as owned thing, then God's ownership of all things would make God 'provided for', which is impossible by consensus.
This shows the definition fails the test of tard.
3
Examine the second argument ('aks)
Read the second argument: the definition would exclude the provision of cattle and slaves, since according to some imams (like al-Shafi'i) they do not own property. But they clearly have provision. This fails the test of 'aks.
Imam Malik's view of incomplete ownership is also considered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السمعيات
قائدة الأرزاق نوعان:
ظاهرة للأبدان؛ كالأقوات.
وباطنة للقلوب؛ كالعلوم والمعارف.
وقوله : وقِيْلَ: لَا بَلْ مَا مُلِكُ أي : وقال جماعةٌ من المعتزلة : ليس الرزقُ ما انتفع به، بل هو ما ملك، فلا يُعتبر فيه الانتفاع ويُعتبر فيه المملوكية انتفع به أم لا . ويلزم على هذا أن الشخص قد لا يستوفي رزقه، وأنه قد يأكل رزق غيره، ويأكلُ غيره رزقه .

As for the first: because God Almighty is the owner of all things, and His ownership is not called provision by consensus, otherwise God would be provided for. As for the second: because it excludes the provision of cattle, male slaves, and female slaves according to some imams, like Imam al-Shafi'i (may God be pleased with him), for he says: Slaves and female slaves have no ownership at all. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazilite definition of rizq as ownership is correct and has no logical problems.
Correction: The definition leads to absurdities: it would make God 'provided for' and would exclude the provision of those without full ownership.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument (tard) used against the Mu'tazilite definition?
A) It would exclude the provision of animals.
B) It would make God 'provided for'.
C) It contradicts the consensus of scholars.
D) It requires benefit to be considered.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite definition of rizq fails logical consistency: it wrongly includes God and wrongly excludes those without full ownership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's provision (rizq), do you ever subconsciously limit it to what you own? How does this lesson challenge that narrow view and expand your trust in Allah's absolute provision, even for those who own nothing?
?
The Mu'tazilites' definition seemed logical on the surface but failed when tested against divine attributes. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah that you have never critically examined? Will you commit to testing them against the Quran and Sunnah today?
?
This refutation uses a sharp logical argument. How often do you engage your intellect to defend the correct creed, rather than repeating it without understanding? What step will you take to strengthen your own ability to defend Ahl al-Sunnah's positions?
