Halal, Makruh, and Haram Sustenance
Classification of provision and refutation of Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of provision according to Ahl al-Sunna and why the Mu'tazilite denial of haram as provision is rejected.

Core Idea
God provides all three types: halal, makruh, and haram.

This lesson explains the types of sustenance in Islamic theology. The author defines halal, makruh, and haram, and addresses a key controversy with the Mu'tazila, who argued that forbidden things cannot be considered provision. Through this passage, we see the Ash'ari insistence that all sustenance comes from God, regardless of its legal ruling.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening verse
Start by reading the verse: 'So God provides the halal, so know! And He provides the disliked and the forbidden.' This sets the doctrinal claim that all three are from God.
Notice the poetic structure and the early placement of 'fa'lamā'.
2
Understand the definition of halal
Halal is defined as what is permissible by text, consensus, or clear analogy. The author also advises against over-inquiry in later times due to corruption.
Al-Qazwini's quote reinforces that denying halal existence is ignorance.
3
Distinguish makruh and haram
Makruh is prohibited with a non-emphatic prohibition, haram with an emphatic one. An example of makruh is the jallala (animal fed on filth).
This distinction is based on the strength of the prohibition.
4
Recognize the rebuttal of the Mu'tazila
The author explicitly states this refutes the Mu'tazila, who deny that haram is provision because they believe in rational good and evil (tahsin wa taqbih 'aqli).
The Ash'ari position is that acts are not inherently good or evil; God's command determines moral value.
5
Reflect on the inclusive nature of provision
The waw (and) in 'makruh and haram' is interpreted as 'or', meaning God provides any of the three for each person. The early 'fa'lamā' emphasizes this all-encompassing provision.
This counter the Mu'tazilite claim that haram is not from God's provision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٢٠ - فَيَرْزُقُ اللهُ الحَلالَ فَاعْلَمَا ويَرْزُقُ المَكْرُوهَ وَالمُحَرَّمَا
قوله : (فَيَرْزُقُ اللَّهُ الحَلالَ مُفرَّحٌ على مذهب أهل السنة والحلال ما كان مباحاً بنص أو إجماع أو قياس جلي، ولا ينبغي اليوم أن يسأل عن أصل الشيء؛ لأن الحلال ما جهل أصله، والأصول قد فسدت واستحكم فسادها، فأخذ الشيء على ظاهر الشرع أولى من السؤال عن شيء يتبين تحريمه .
قال القزويني : ومن قال : إن الحلال ليس بموجود، فقد طعن في الشريعة، وهو أحمق حصل له ذلك من جهله ؛ فإن الله لم يكلّف الخلق عين الحلال في علم الله تعالى، بل كلَّفهم أن يصيبوا الحلال في اعتقادهم وظنّهم)
وقوله : (فَاعْلَمَا) بنون التوكيد الخفيفة المنقلبة ألفاً وكان حقه التأخير عن قوله : ( ويرزق المكروه (والمحرما) لكنه قدَّمه للضرورة، ونبه به على أنه تعالى يرزق كل أحدٍ من الأقسام الثلاثة اجتماعاً وانفراداً كذا قال الشارح تبعاً لوالده، وفيه خفاء؛ لأن ذلك لا يُشعر به قوله : (فاعلما)، وإنما يُستفاد ذلك من ذكره الأقسام الثلاثة، مع جعل الواو بمعنى (أو) التي لمنع الخلو. وقوله : (وَيَرْزُقُ المَكْرُوهَ وَالمُحَرَّمَا فالأوَّلُ ما نهى عنه نهياً غير أكيد، كما : في خبر ابن عمر : وهو أنه نهى عن أكل الجلالة، وشرب لبنها حتى تُعلف أربعين ليلة .
والثاني : ما نهى عنه نهياً أكيداً، ورد المصنف بذلك على المعتزلة القائلين بأن الحرام لا يكون رزقاً، بناءً على التحسين والتقبيح العقليين.
120 - So God provides the halal, so know! And He provides the disliked (makruh) and the forbidden (haram).
His statement: 'So God provides the halal' – an opening on the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna. Halal is what is permissible by text, consensus, or clear analogy. Today one should not ask about the origin of a thing, for halal is what is unknown in its origin, and the origins have become corrupt and their corruption has become entrenched. So taking a thing according to the apparent ruling of the Law is better than asking about something which might turn out to be forbidden.
Al-Qazwini said: Whoever says that halal does not exist has slandered the Shari'a, and he is a fool; that happened to him due to his ignorance. For God did not oblige the creation to [find] the exact halal in God's knowledge, but rather He obliged them to hit upon the halal according to their belief and conjecture.
And His statement: 'So know!' with the light nun of emphasis which changes to alif; it should have come after His statement 'and He provides the disliked and the forbidden', but He put it forward out of necessity, and alerted by it that He provides everyone of the three categories collectively and individually – thus said the commentator following his father, but there is obscurity in this, for that is not indicated by His statement 'So know!', rather that is derived from mentioning the three categories, with the waw meaning 'or' which prevents emptiness. And His statement: 'And He provides the disliked and the forbidden'. The first is what is prohibited with a non-emphatic prohibition, as in the report of Ibn 'Umar: that he forbade eating the jallala (animal fed on filth) and drinking its milk until it is fed clean fodder for forty nights. The second is what is prohibited with an emphatic prohibition. And the author rebuts thereby the Mu'tazila who say that the forbidden is not provision, based on [the principle of] rational good and evil (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih al-'aqliyyayn).
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage explains the three types of provision: halal, makruh, and haram, and refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that haram cannot be provision. The author first defines halal as what is permissible by evidence. He notes that in later times, one should not investigate origins too much because corruption has spread; acting on the apparent ruling suffices. He quotes al-Qazwini criticizing those who deny the existence of halal. Then he discusses the word 'fa'lamā' (so know!), noting it is placed early for poetic necessity, and clarifies that the three categories apply to everyone collectively and individually, with the waw meaning 'or'. He then defines makruh as what is prohibited non-emphatically (e.g., the jallala), and haram as what is prohibited emphatically. The author explicitly states that this rebuts the Mu'tazila who deny that haram is provision, because they base their ethics on rational good and evil. By affirming that God provides even haram, the author upholds the Ash'ari position that acts are not inherently good or evil but are made so by divine command.

Key Terms
حلال
مكروه
حرام
تحسين وتقبيح عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some Mu'tazila believe that forbidden things (haram) cannot be considered provision from God because they are evil.
Correction: The author asserts that God provides all types, including haram, because moral value is determined by divine law, not inherent nature.

Checkpoint
How does the author distinguish between makruh and haram?
A) Makruh is prohibited by a non-emphatic prohibition, haram by an emphatic one.
B) Makruh is what is disliked by people, haram is forbidden by God.
C) Makruh is permissible but discouraged, haram is absolutely forbidden.
D) Makruh only applies to food, haram to all actions.

Key Takeaway
God provides all sustenance—halal, makruh, and haram—and the Mu'tazilite denial of haram as provision is rejected because moral values depend on divine command, not rational judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a doubtful matter in your daily sustenance, do you trust the apparent Halal or do you let your doubts paralyze you? How can you train yourself to act on the apparent ruling without excessive investigation?
?
If you were to truly believe that even the Haram you consume is from God's provision, would that change your attitude towards seeking lawful earnings? Reflect on a time you compromised on Halal for convenience. What would you do differently now?
?
Al-Qazwini says that denying the existence of Halal is slandering the Shari'a. Have you ever doubted that Halal opportunities exist in your life? How does this lesson challenge you to trust in God's provision?

The Poetic Necessity of Advancing 'Fa'lama'
Explains the grammatical placement of 'fa'lama' and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the poet advanced the word 'fa'lama' before its expected position and how the commentator derives a theological point about provision from this placement.

Core Idea
Poetic necessity can shift a word's position without breaking meaning.

The commentator begins by addressing a grammatical peculiarity in the poem: the word 'fa'lama' appears earlier than expected. This is not a mistake but a deliberate poetic necessity. The commentator also notes that this placement hints at a deeper theological truth about the all-encompassing nature of Allah's provision. Let's examine the passage closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the grammatical explanation
Read the passage where the commentator explains the light emphatic noon and the advancement of 'fa'lama' for necessity.
The noon becomes alif in pronunciation.
2
Understand the necessity
Notice that the commentator explicitly states that advancement is due to necessity (al-ḍarūrah), which is a common device in Arabic poetry.
3
Consider the theological inference
The commentator notes that some infer from this placement that Allah provides all three categories collectively and individually, but he critiques that this inference is weak.
The inference is actually from the later enumeration with 'aw' meaning 'or'.
4
Reflect on the waw meaning 'or'
The commentator clarifies that the true indication comes from treating the waw as 'or' to imply that at least one category is always present in provision.
This leads to the understanding that provision includes halal, makruh, and haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (فَاعْلَمَا) بنون التوكيد الخفيفة المنقلبة ألفاً وكان حقه التأخير عن قوله : ( ويرزق المكروه (والمحرما) لكنه قدَّمه للضرورة، ونبه به على أنه تعالى يرزق كل أحدٍ من الأقسام الثلاثة اجتماعاً وانفراداً كذا قال الشارح تبعاً لوالده، وفيه خفاء؛ لأن ذلك لا يُشعر به قوله : (فاعلما)، وإنما يُستفاد ذلك من ذكره الأقسام الثلاثة، مع جعل الواو بمعنى (أو) التي لمنع الخلو. وقوله : (وَيَرْزُقُ المَكْرُوهَ وَالمُحَرَّمَا فالأوَّلُ ما نهى عنه نهياً غير أكيد، كما : في خبر ابن عمر : وهو أنه نهى عن أكل الجلالة، وشرب لبنها حتى تُعلف أربعين ليلة. والثاني : ما نهى عنه نهياً أكيداً، ورد المصنف بذلك على المعتزلة القائلين بأن الحرام لا يكون رزقاً، بناءً على التحسين والتقبيح العقليين.
And his statement: (فَاعْلَمَا) with the light emphatic noon which is converted to alif, and it should have been delayed after his statement: (ويرزق المكروه والمحرما) but he advanced it due to necessity, and he alerted by it that He, exalted, provides every one of the three categories collectively and individually. Thus said the commentator following his father, but there is obscurity in it; because that is not indicated by his statement (فاعلما), rather that is derived from his mention of the three categories, with making the waw have the meaning of (or) which prevents emptiness. And his statement: (وَيَرْزُقُ المَكْرُوهَ وَالمُحَرَّمَا): the first is what is forbidden by a non-emphatic prohibition, as in the report of Ibn 'Umar: that he forbade eating the animal fed on impurities and drinking its milk until it is fed for forty nights. And the second: what is forbidden by an emphatic prohibition. The author refuted by that the Mu'tazila who say that the forbidden is not provision, based on rational good and evil.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the line 'فَاعْلَمَا' from the poem. The commentator first discusses the grammatical point: the word 'فاعلما' uses a light emphatic noon that becomes an alif, and though it would normally come after the phrase 'ويرزق المكروه والمحرما', the poet advanced it for poetic necessity. The commentator notes that the poet intended by this to alert the reader that Allah provides all three categories (presumably halal, makruh, haram) both collectively and individually, though this inference is not directly from 'فاعلما' but from the later enumeration of the three categories with the waw meaning 'or' to indicate that at least one is always present. Then the commentator defines the two types of prohibited provision: the first is al-makruh (disliked), which is forbidden by a non-emphatic prohibition, exemplified by the report of Ibn 'Umar where the Prophet forbade eating from an animal fed on impurities and drinking its milk until it has been fed clean fodder for forty nights. The second is al-haram (forbidden), which is forbidden by an emphatic prohibition. The author uses this classification to refute the Mu'tazila, who, based on their rationalist theory of good and evil (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih al-'aqliyyan), deny that the haram can be considered provision (rizq) from Allah. By affirming that haram is indeed provision, the author upholds the Ash'ari position that all things, including the forbidden, are created by Allah and thus are provision.

Key Terms
فاعلما
نون التوكيد
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the word 'fa'lama' had to be delayed grammatically, and its advancement is a mistake or changes the meaning.
Correction: The advancement is a poetic necessity (ḍarūrah) and does not affect the meaning; the theological point about provision comes from the list, not from the word order.

Checkpoint
Why did the poet advance the word 'فاعلما' before its expected position?
A) To emphasize knowledge.
B) For poetic necessity.
C) To indicate provision types.
D) To refute the Mu'tazila.

Key Takeaway
The advancement of 'fa'lama' is a poetic necessity, not a grammatical error, and the theological inference about provision comes from the later enumeration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a text, do you ever jump to conclusions based on word placement alone? Reflect on a time you assumed a certain meaning from a verse or statement without considering the full context. How does this lesson's attention to grammatical detail challenge you to be more careful in your understanding?
?
The commentator here made an inference that was not supported by the actual words. Have you ever been swayed by a scholarly opinion without verifying it against the primary source? Commit to a habit: before accepting an interpretation, examine the linguistic and contextual evidence yourself—even if it means admitting your initial understanding was wrong.

Classification of Provision: Makruh and Haram
Defines makruh and haram and refutes Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of makruh and haram as types of provision, and how the author refutes the Mu'tazila position that haram is not rizq.

Core Idea
Even forbidden things are provision from Allah, contrary to Mu'tazila rationalism.

In this passage, the commentator defines two categories of provision: makruh (disliked) and haram (forbidden). He provides a detailed example of makruh from a hadith and then clarifies that both are considered rizq from Allah. This directly counters the Mu'tazila, who, based on rational good and evil, argue that the forbidden cannot be from Allah. Let's see the definitions and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the passage where the commentator defines makruh as what is forbidden by a non-emphatic prohibition and haram as what is forbidden by an emphatic prohibition.
Note the example from the hadith of Ibn 'Umar about the animal fed on impurities.
2
Identify the sectarian refutation
The passage ends by stating that the author refutes the Mu'tazila who deny that haram is provision, based on rational good and evil.
The Ash'ari position is that all things are from Allah, including the forbidden.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila believe that reason can determine good and evil, so something evil like the forbidden cannot be attributed to Allah as provision.
4
Reflect on the Ash'ari response
By including haram in the definition of rizq, the author affirms that Allah provides everything, even if it is forbidden for us to consume.
This upholds divine sovereignty over all creation.

Immediate Repentance from Major Sins
Commentator explains immediate repentance from major sins and refutes Mu'tazilite multiplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why repentance from major sins must be immediate and why delaying it is only one additional sin, not multiplied by time.

Core Idea
Delaying repentance adds one sin, not many.

In this passage, the commentator explains a key ruling about repentance: it must be performed immediately for all sins, especially major ones. He then addresses a controversial Mu'tazilite claim that delayed repentance multiplies into many sins. Let's read the passage to see the correct position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the commentator's precise language.
2
Identify the two types of sins
The passage mentions minor and major sins. Find the ruling for major sins and why the author focuses on them.
Major sins are the primary concern, but minor sins share the same ruling.
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite view
The passage describes the Mu'tazilite position that each moment of delay adds a new sin. See their geometric progression example.
They claim if you delay one moment, you have two sins; delay another, four; and so on.
4
Appreciate the correction
The commentator refutes this, stating that delay adds only one sin. Note how he limits the multiplication.
The key phrase is 'but it is one sin, even if prolonged'.
5
Connect to practice
Reflect on why immediate repentance is crucial and how this understanding protects against excessive scrupulosity.
This teaching encourages swift repentance without fear of endless multiplication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٢٥ - مِنْهُ المَتابُ وَاجِبٌ في الحَالِ وَلَا انْتِقَاضَ إِنْ يَعُدْ لِلحَالِ قوله : (فَالثَّانِي مِنْهُ المَتابُ وَاجِبٌ في الحَالِ) أي: إذا علمت أن الذنوب قسمان: صغائر وكبائر .. فاعلم أن الثاني وهو الكبائر منه المتاب واجب عيناً في حال التلبس بالمعصية فوراً، فتأخيرها ذنب آخر، لكنه ذنب واحد، ولو تراخي، نعم يتفاوت في الكيف باعتبار طول الزمان وقصره، خلافاً للمعتزلة القائلين بتعدده بتعدد الزمان، حتى لو أخَرها لحظة بعد لحظة الذنب فأربعة ذنوب : الذنبُ الأَوَّلُ ، وتأخيرُ توبته في اللحظة الأولى، وتأخيرُ التوبة من هذين في الثانية، وإِن أَخَرَ لحظةً أخرى فثمانية، وهكذا، وإنما اقتصر المصنف على الثاني لأنه الأهم، وإلا فالأول وهو الصغائر كذلك، وعبارة النووي: (واتفقوا على أن التوبة من جميع المعاصي واجبة على الفور، ولا يجوز تأخيرها سواء كانت المعصية صغيرة أو كبيرة) انتهت.
125. From it repentance is obligatory immediately, and there is no invalidation if he returns to the state. His statement: (Then the second from it, repentance is obligatory immediately) i.e., if you know that sins are of two types: minor and major, then know that the second, which is major sins, repentance from it is individually obligatory immediately while engaged in the sin, so delaying it is another sin, but it is one sin, even if prolonged. Yes, it varies in quality based on the length or shortness of time, contrary to the Mu'tazila who say it multiplies with the multiplicity of time, such that if he delays it moment after moment, the sin becomes four sins: the first sin, and the delay of his repentance in the first moment, and the delay of repentance from these two in the second moment, and if he delays another moment, then eight, and so on. And the author only focused on the second because it is more important, otherwise the first, which is minor sins, is likewise. And al-Nawawi's statement: (They agreed that repentance from all sins is obligatory immediately, and it is not permissible to delay it, whether the sin is minor or major). End.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the first part of the poem line: 'From it repentance is obligatory immediately.' The commentator first divides sins into minor (sagha'ir) and major (kaba'ir). Repentance from major sins is an individual obligation (wajib 'ayni) that must be performed immediately while engaged in the sin. Delaying that repentance is itself a sin, but crucially, it is only one sin regardless of how long the delay lasts, though the sin's quality may vary with time. This directly refutes the Mu'tazila, who argued that each moment of delay multiplies the number of sins geometrically. The commentator illustrates their view: if one delays repentance moment by moment, the first delay yields two sins (original + delay), the second yields four, then eight, etc. The commentator rejects this multiplication is not correct. He also notes that the poem's author focused on major sins because they are more important, but the same immediate obligation applies to minor sins, as confirmed by al-Nawawi's citation of scholarly consensus.

Key Terms
صغائر
كبائر
المتاب
التوبة
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Delaying repentance from a major sin creates multiple new sins for each moment of delay.
Correction: Delaying repentance is a single additional sin, not multiplied by time.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Mu'tazila view on delaying repentance?
A) It does not create an additional sin.
B) It multiplies the sin with each moment of delay.
C) It is only one sin regardless of delay.
D) Minor sins do not require immediate repentance.

Key Takeaway
Immediate repentance from major sins is obligatory, and delaying it adds only one sin, contrary to the Mu'tazilite view of multiplication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you delay repentance from a sin, do you actually believe it is only one additional sin, or do you feel weighed down by every passing moment? How does knowing the truth change your urgency to repent right now?
?
Are you treating immediate repentance as a casual suggestion or as an absolute obligation (wajib 'ayni) upon you at this very moment? What specific sin are you currently delaying repentance from?
?
The Mu'tazila thought each moment of delay multiplies sins beyond count. Do you sometimes feel your sins are too many to be forgiven? How does knowing that Allah counts only one sin for delay encourage you to turn back now and trust His mercy?

Repentance Not Invalidated by Returning to Sin
Author refutes the Mu'tazila claim that repentance is invalidated by relapsing into sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Ahl al-Sunna, repentance remains valid even if one later relapses into the same sin, contrary to the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
Repentance once accepted is not undone by future sin.

In this passage, the author clarifies a key difference between Sunni and Mu'tazila views on repentance. While the Mu'tazila require lifelong abstinence from the sin for repentance to be valid, Sunni scholars maintain that a sincere repentance remains intact even if the person later stumbles again. The commentary also highlights the Sufi perspective that relapsing after repentance is worse than persistent sin without repentance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a concise poetic line followed by commentary.
2
Identify the two opposing views
The passage presents the Sunni view (no invalidation) and the Mu'tazila view (invalidation). Find each and note the reason given for the Mu'tazila position.
The Mu'tazila make non-repetition a condition of repentance.
3
Understand the Sufi perspective
The author adds a Sufi saying that relapsing is uglier than seventy sins without repentance. Reflect on why this might be.
This is not a matter of doctrine but of spiritual advice.
4
Review the key takeaway
Ask yourself: What is the main lesson here about the permanence of sincere repentance?
The core idea is that repentance is not undone by subsequent sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وَلَا انْتِقَاضَ إِنْ يَعُدْ لِلحَالِ) أي ولا انتقاض لتوبة التائب الشرعية إن يعد للحال التي كان عليها من التلبس بالذنب، فلا يعود ذنبه الذي تاب منه بعوده له، خلافاً للمعتزلة في قولهم بانتقاض التوبة بعوده للذنب، فيعود ذنبه الذي تاب منه بعوده له؛ لأنَّ من شروط التوبة عندهم ألا يعاود الذنب بعد التوبة، وعند الصوفية معاودة الذنب بعد التوبة أقبح من سبعين ذنباً بلا توبة.
His saying: 'And no invalidation if he returns to the [same] state' meaning: There is no invalidation of the legal repentance of the repentant if he returns to the state he was in of being engaged in sin, so his sin from which he repented does not return by his returning to it, contrary to the Mu'tazila in their statement that repentance is invalidated by returning to the sin, so his sin from which he repented returns by his returning to it, because among the conditions of repentance according to them is that he not repeat the sin after repentance. And according to the Sufis, repeating the sin after repentance is uglier than seventy sins without repentance.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage explains a line from the poem: repentance is not invalidated by returning to the same sin. The author clarifies that the sin previously repented from does not return. This opposes the Mu'tazila, who make the condition of repentance that one does not repeat the sin; thus returning invalidates it. The author then notes the Sufi view that such relapse is worse than committing seventy sins without repentance.

Key Terms
انتقاض
معاودة الذنب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Returning to sin after repentance means the original repentance was invalid.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna hold that sincere repentance is accepted and not nullified by subsequent sin; the sin is a new matter requiring a new repentance.

Checkpoint
What do the Mu'tazila believe about returning to a sin after repentance?
A) It does not affect the repentance
B) It invalidates the repentance and the sin returns
C) It is worse than seventy sins without repentance
D) It requires a new repentance but does not invalidate the old one

Key Takeaway
Repentance, once sincerely performed and accepted by Allah, is not invalidated by relapsing into the same sin; the Mu'tazila view is rejected by Ahl al-Sunna.

The Imam Is Not a Pillar of Faith
Explanation that the imam is not a fundamental article of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the imam is not a fundamental article of faith (rukn) whose denial constitutes disbelief (kufr).

Core Idea
The imamate is not a necessary belief; denying it does not make one a disbeliever.

The first verse of this passage clarifies an important theological boundary: the imam (leader) is not a 'pillar' (rukn) of Islam like the shahada or the five pillars. This means that while the imamate is important, it is not an article of faith known necessarily from religion. Denying it does not automatically put someone outside the fold of Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and commentary
Start by reading the Arabic verse and its literal translation. Then read the commentary to understand the doctrine.
The commentator distinguishes between pillars of faith and the imamate.
2
Understand what 'rukn' means
A 'rukn' (pillar) is something so fundamental that denying it is kufr. The commentator lists the actual pillars: shahada, prayer, zakat, etc.
The imamate is not among these.
3
Identify the key distinction
The imamate is not 'known necessarily from the religion,' so its denier is not a disbeliever. This is a crucial point to avoid extremism.
4
Reflect on the misconception
A common mistake is to equate rejection of the imam with rejection of Islam. This passage corrects that error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَلَيْسَ رُكْناً يُعْتَقَدْ فِي الدِّينِ وَلَا تَزِغْ عَنْ أَمْرِهِ المُبِينِ قوله : (فَلَيْسَ رُكْناً يُعْتَقَدْ في (الدِّيْنِ أي : فليس نصب الإمام ركناً يعتقد في قواعد الدين المجمع عليها المعلومة بالتواتر بحيث يكفر منكرها ، كالشهادتين والزكاة والصلاة وصوم رمضان والحج ؛ لأنه ليس معلوماً من الدين بالضرورة، فلا يكفر منكره. وقوله : (وَلَا تَزِغْ عَنْ أَمْرِهِ (المُبِينِ أي : ولا تخرج عن امتثال أمره الواضح الجاري على قواعد الشريعة وفي كلامه حذف الواو مع ما عطفت، والتقدير (عن أمره ونهيه كما أشار إليه الشارح، ولو حمل الأمر في النظم على الشأن لعم الأمرين جميعاً، فتجب طاعته على جميع الرعايا ظاهراً وباطناً ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿أَطِيعُوا اللَّهَ وَأَطِيعُوا الرَّسُولَ وَأُولى الأمر منكر) [النساء: ٤٥٩، وهم العلماء والأمراء، ولقوله : «مَنْ أطاعَ أميري فقد أطاعني، ومَنْ عصى أميري فقد عصاني (۱) ، لكن لا يُطاع في الحرام (١)، والمكروه، وأما المباح فإن كان فيه مصلحة عامة للمسلمين وجب طاعته فيه، وإلا فلا ؛ فلو نادى بعدم شرب الدخان المعروف الآن وجبت عليهم طاعته؛ لأنَّ في إبطاله مصلحة عامةً ؛ إذ في تعاطيه خِسَّةٌ لذوي الهيآت ووجوه الناس، خصوصاً إذا كان في القهاوي، وقد وقع أنه أمر بترك الدخان في الأسواق والقهاوي، فيحرم الآن(٢).
So he is not a pillar believed in the religion, and do not deviate from his clear command. His statement: 'So he is not a pillar believed in the religion' means: the appointment of the imam is not a pillar believed in the agreed-upon foundations of the religion known by tawatur such that its denier is deemed a disbeliever, like the two testimonies, zakat, prayer, fasting Ramadan, and Hajj; because it is not known necessarily from the religion, so its denier is not deemed a disbeliever. And His statement: 'and do not deviate from his clear command' means: do not go out from obeying his clear command that proceeds upon the rules of sharia. And in his speech there is deletion of the waw with what it coordinating, and the estimation is 'his command and his prohibition' as the commentator indicated. And if 'command' in the poem is interpreted as 'affair', it would encompass both matters. So obedience to him is obligatory upon all subjects outwardly and inwardly, due to His saying: 'Obey Allah, obey the Messenger, and those in authority among you' (al-Nisa 4:59), and they are the scholars and the rulers, and due to his saying: 'Whoever obeys my amir has obeyed me, and whoever disobeys my amir has disobeyed me.' But he is not obeyed in what is unlawful or disliked. As for permissible matters, if there is a public benefit for Muslims, obedience to him is obligatory, otherwise not. So if he called for not drinking the known tobacco now, obedience would be obligatory upon them, because in its prohibition there is public benefit, since its use involves degradation for people of dignity and faces, especially in coffeehouses. And it has occurred that he ordered the abandonment of tobacco in markets and coffeehouses, so it is now unlawful.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two lines of the poem. The first line states that the imam is not a 'pillar' (rukn) in the religion, i.e., not a fundamental article of faith that is known necessarily from religion, such that denying it would constitute unbelief. The commentator draws a distinction between the imamate and the essentials of Islam like the shahada and the five pillars; the imamate is not among them, so its denier is not automatically a disbeliever. The second line commands not to deviate from the imam's clear command. The commentator expands that this includes both command and prohibition, and that obedience is required from all subjects openly and in secret, based on Qur'an and hadith. However, there are limits: the imam is not obeyed in haram or makruh. For mubah (permissible) acts, obedience is only required if there is a general benefit. The commentator gives a contemporary example: if the imam prohibits tobacco for public benefit, it becomes obligatory to obey, and the prohibition is now considered haram.

Key Terms
ركن
إمام
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that denying the imamate (e.g., rejecting the political leader) is an act of disbelief.
Correction: This passage explicitly says that the imam is not a pillar of faith, so denial does not constitute kufr. Only rejection of the fundamental pillars (like prayer and zakat) leads to disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, which of the following is NOT a pillar (rukn) that makes its denier a disbeliever?
A) The two testimonies (shahadatan)
B) Prayer (salat)
C) The imamate (imamah)
D) Fasting Ramadan (sawm)

Key Takeaway
The imamate is not a fundamental article of faith; denying it does not render one a disbeliever.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disagree with another Muslim on a matter of leadership or secondary obligation, do you ever silently question their faith? How does this passage challenge you to examine the boundaries you place on who is a believer?
?
Do you treat the imamate (or any similar religious obligation) as if it were a pillar, judging those who differ? What would it cost you to accept that someone can deny an obligation you hold dear and still be a Muslim?
?
If you were to fully internalize that only matters known by tawatur are pillars, how would it change the way you engage with Muslims of different sects? Are you willing to extend that charity, even when it feels uncomfortable?

Obedience to the Imam and Its Limits
Explanation of the duty to obey the imam and its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand when obedience to the imam is obligatory, especially regarding permissible matters with public benefit.

Core Idea
Obey the imam in all clear commands, except sin, and in mubah only for public benefit.

The second verse commands not to deviate from the imam's clear command. The commentary expands on the nature of this obedience: it includes both commands and prohibitions, is owed by all subjects inwardly and outwardly, but has limits. The imam is not obeyed in what is haram or makruh. For permissible (mubah) acts, obedience becomes obligatory only if there is a general public benefit (maslahah 'ammah). The commentator gives a contemporary example regarding tobacco.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and commentary
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, focusing on how the imam's command is to be followed.
The phrase 'clear command' implies the command must align with sharia.
2
Understand the categories of actions
The commentary distinguishes between haram, makruh, and mubah. The imam is not obeyed in haram or makruh.
For mubah, obedience depends on public benefit.
3
Analyze the condition of public benefit
For mubah acts, the imam's order is only obligatory if there is a clear public benefit. The tobacco example illustrates this.
This prevents arbitrary authority.
4
Apply the principle
Consider how this principle applies to modern contexts: when can a leader's order in non-religious matters be binding?

Rebellion Only for Kufr
Commentary on rebellion only for kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that obedience to the imam is mandatory until he commands kufr, at which point allegiance must be discarded.

Core Idea
Allegiance to the imam ends only at kufr, not at sin.

This lesson examines the first half of the poem's commentary, establishing the only condition that permits rebellion: when the imam commands disbelief. The commentator clarifies that the subject must discard allegiance publicly if possible, otherwise secretly, relying on Allah's protection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the commentary on 'Except with kufr, then throw away his covenant.' Note the precision: it is when the imam commands kufr, not merely commits it.
The Arabic uses 'amara' (commands) to specify action.
2
Understand the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between discarding allegiance openly and secretly. Consider why secrecy is allowed in some cases.
This shows concern for the subject's safety.
3
Reflect on 'Allah alone suffices'
The second line reassures that Allah will protect against the harm of a kafir imam. Why is this stated separately?
It reinforces trust in divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إِلَّا بِكُفْرٍ فَانْبِذَنَّ عَهْدَهُ أي : إلا إذا أمر بكفر فاطرحن بيعته جهراً، فإن لم تقدر على الجهر بذلك فاطرحها سِرا . وقوله : (فَاللَّهُ يَكْفِينَا أَذَاهُ وَحْدَهُ أي : فالله تعالى يكفينا أذى الإمام الذي أمر بالكفر وحده؛ إذ هو الذي ناصيته بقدرته (۳) قوله : بِغَيْرِ هَذَا لَا يُبَاحُ صَرْفُهُ أي : بغير هذا الكفر من جميع المعاصي لا يجوز خلعه عن الإمامة لا جهراً ولا سرا . وقوله : (وَلَيْسَ يُعْزَلْ إِنْ أُزِيلَ وَصْفُهُ) بسكون اللام من (يعزل) للوزن؛ أي: وليس يُعزل إذا وُلّي مستكملاً للشروط، ثم أزيل وصفه السابق؛ وهو العدالة بطرو الفسق، خلافاً لطائفة ذهبوا إلى أنه يُعزل بذلك.
His statement: 'Except with kufr, then throw away his covenant' means: except if he commands kufr, then discard your allegiance to him openly, and if you are not able to do so openly, then discard it secretly. And his statement: 'And Allah alone suffices us against his harm' means: Allah Almighty alone suffices us against the harm of the imam who has commanded kufr, for He is the one who holds his forelock by His power. His statement: 'Other than this, his removal is not permitted' means: other than this kufr, from all sins, it is not permissible to remove him from the imamate, neither openly nor secretly. And his statement: 'And he is not removed if his description is removed' (with a sukun on the lam of 'yu'zal' for the meter) means: he is not removed if he was appointed while fulfilling the conditions, then his previous description—that is, his justice ('adala)—is removed through sinfulness, contrary to a group who held that he is removed by that.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on four consecutive lines of the poem dealing with the limits of obedience and removal of the imam. The first line states: 'Except with kufr, then throw away his covenant.' The commentator explains that if the imam commands kufr (disbelief), the subject must discard his allegiance openly if possible; otherwise secretly. The second line: 'And Allah alone suffices us against his harm' emphasizes that Allah's protection alone is enough against such an imam. The third line: 'Other than this, his removal is not permitted' clarifies that removal from the imamate is not allowed for any sin short of kufr, whether done openly or secretly. The fourth line: 'And he is not removed if his description is removed' addresses the situation where the imam was appointed while fulfilling conditions (including justice), but later loses his justice through sin (fisq). The commentator explicitly states that he is not removed, contrary to a group who held otherwise. This is a classical Sunni position that rebellion against the imam is not permitted for sin alone.

Key Terms
كفر
بيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think rebellion is allowed for any injustice.
Correction: The passage limits rebellion exclusively to the case where the imam commands kufr; other sins do not permit it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition must allegiance be discarded?
A) If the imam becomes a fasiq
B) If the imam is unjust
C) If the imam commands kufr
D) If the imam is incompetent

Key Takeaway
Obedience to the imam is absolute except when he commands kufr; then allegiance must be renounced, openly or secretly, with trust in Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a commitment or relationship in your life where you have been considering breaking it because of personal discomfort or injustice, even though it does not involve commanding you to disbelieve? Will you trust Allah's sufficiency and remain obedient, as this lesson demands?
?
If your leader—whether in government, family, or community—explicitly commanded you to do something against your faith, would you have the courage to silently renounce that allegiance if you couldn't openly oppose? How does your reliance on Allah measure up?
?
This lesson limits rebellion to only one cause: commanding disbelief. How often do you justify disobedience in your heart for lesser grievances? What would it take for you to accept this discipline and attribute your protection only to Allah?

Sects and Disagreements
beginner
4 min
Imam Not Removed for Fisq
Commentary on no removal for sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a qualified imam is not to be removed from office even if he later becomes sinful (fasiq), contrary to the view of some groups.

Core Idea
A sinning imam is not deposed; only kufr justifies removal.

This lesson covers the second half of the commentary, reinforcing that removal from the imamate is not permitted for any sin short of kufr. Additionally, it addresses a specific scenario: an imam appointed while just but later becoming sinful remains in office, rejecting an opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the commentary on 'Other than this, his removal is not permitted' and 'And he is not removed if his description is removed.' Note the strong prohibition.
The phrase 'la yubah' (not permitted) is clear.
2
Distinguish between the two cases
The first sentence explicitly forbids removal for any sin except kufr. The second addresses a specific scenario where the imam was appointed with all conditions, then becomes sinful—he is not removed.
The second case is a subset of the general rule.
3
Note the opposing view
The passage mentions a group that held the imam should be removed for sin. Consider why the commentator rejects this.
This reflects the Sunni principle of avoiding fitna.

Source Text

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe a sinful imam must be deposed.
Correction: The passage states that removal is not permitted for sins other than kufr, and even a just iman who becomes sinful is not removed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to an imam who was appointed while just but later becomes sinful?
A) He is removed immediately
B) He is not removed
C) He is removed only if he commands kufr
D) He is removed secretly

Key Takeaway
An imam who becomes sinful is not removed; removal is permitted only for commanding kufr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a leader sinning, do you instinctively feel he deserves to be removed? How does this lesson challenge your desire for immediate justice and call you to prioritize stability over personal outrage?
?
If you were in a position of authority, would you be more concerned with maintaining your own power or with preserving the community's well-being? This lesson says a sinning imam is not removed – does that make you uneasy? What does that unease reveal about your own attachment to rule?
?
The text says removal is only for kufr. Have you ever secretly justified criticizing or opposing a leader because of their sins? How does this passage hold you accountable to the principle of patience and the prohibition of rebellion?
